# The Pragmatics of Managing Negative Emotions in a Chinese Context

Rong Lei

# The Pragmatics of Managing Negative Emotions in a Chinese Context

Drawing from rich, naturally occurring language data and synthesizing insights from the fields of interpersonal pragmatics and Chinese interpersonal communication, this book presents a compelling exploration of the management of negative emotions through an interpersonal pragmatics lens.

The volume seeks to answer a number of key questions at the intersection of these two fields, including: how do negative interpersonal emotions manifest in the context of Chinese culture? What connections exist between emotions and interpersonal dynamics? In what ways are these emotions intertwined with the moral framework of Chinese society? How are such negative emotions effectively navigated and regulated? The analysis goes beyond mere examination of emotional expressions, delving into the cultural nuances that shape the understanding and handling of emotions. The book elucidates, for instance, how unmet ganqing expectations can trigger emotions like anger and sadness, and in turn, the employment of rapport management strategies in their mitigation.

The book provides profound insights into a previously overlooked and little understood area of study, making it of interest to students and researchers in pragmatics, discourse analysis, communication studies, and psychotherapy.

# Foreword

This book, written by the young scholar Dr. Rong Lei, associate professor from the Center for Linguistics and Applied Linguistics at Guangdong University of Foreign Studies in P.R. China, provides a way of understanding negative emotions and their management from a combined culture-specific and interpersonal pragmatics approach in the Chinese context. As a complex phenomenon, emotions are believed to have various functions in our lives—for instance, positive emotions can help participants to communicate with others, and thus have more fulfilling results, but negative emotions can cause communication breakdowns or other unexpected outcomes on many occasions. Emotional management has therefore been considered essential either in ordinary lives or in institutional settings, involving the ability to manage and control emotions healthily and constructively.

This study about the pragmatics of managing negative emotions is of great importance because emotions can be both socially and interpersonally constructive. Negative emotions are unpleasant emotions that participants commonly experience in response to certain situations or events. Examples of negative emotions can include anger, fear, sadness, guilt, shame, jealousy, and anxiety. These emotions can be difficult to manage; thus they can have negative impacts on communication and interpersonal relationships. Thus, as an inseparable part of human activity, it is expected that emotions can be expressed and responded to appropriately in interaction. Effective emotion management can help participants cope with stress and challenging situations, improve interpersonal or group relationships, and even promote overall well-being. This means that language and discourse cannot be said to be completely devoid of emotional issues. The study of emotions is crucial not only for understanding the mechanisms of human minds, which has long been approached in psychology and neurology (Allegretta et al. 2021) but also for the study of discursive behaviours and strategy choices in language use.

Human interaction is strongly loaded with emotions, and rich expressions of emotions can be found in both English and Chinese. Emotions are expressed through various speech acts and can be managed by choosing flexible devices in context. Although there have been some studies about emotions from the perspectives and approaches of other disciplines, relevant topics await further research in the field of pragmatics or linguistics in general. For example, the main factors of power—distance and rank of imposition, which determine (im)politeness in Brown and Levinson's (1978, 1987) theory—imply certain emotions. Within their framework, the differences of power are likely to create both positive emotions (love, respect, satisfaction, appreciation, sympathy, etc.) and negative emotions (fear, hate, contempt, awe, etc.). The rank of imposition of a speech act in a culture often implies certain emotions. For instance, in Chinese culture, the act of persuading a guest to eat more ('Have another piece of cake', or serving more food to his bowl) is a way of showing hospitality or politeness. This is considered a case of positive emotion management, but in Western cultures, such an act is quite impositional and impolite, and so a negative emotion is most likely to arise. This shows that emotional issues can be culturally specific in interaction.

Emotions and interpersonal issues are interrelated with each other and interplay with each other in interactional practices. Goffman (1967), Brown and Levinson (1978,1987), and many other researchers maintain that face is associated with emotional reactions. If a social or interpersonal event establishes a face for a participant, he tends to 'feel good'; otherwise he probably feels bad or hurt, and then a loss of face can be felt. Affect has been mentioned in their preface to the 1987 edition, but they have not given emotions much room in their politeness framework. Brown and Levinson (1987) call for further attention to the interconnection between emotions and the distance variable. Theoretically, there are gaps in research about the functions of emotions and their interplay with face, (im)politeness, or identity. This consideration is different from that about power, distance, and other social variables and their effects on interpersonal effects. In some studies, emotional issues have been considered in theoretical frameworks about communication. For instance, in Spencer-Oatey's (2000, 2002, 2008) rapport management framework, it is argued that negative emotions arise when a participant's face is threatened, or behavioural expectations are not met. Emotion is then the product of social interaction in which face or sociality rights are involved. In a monograph about using language to offend, Culpeper (2011) has also discussed the emotional consequences of impoliteness, and how emotion and offence are closely associated with each other. His study shows that impoliteness is an emotional issue which can be caused by threatening quality face, violating equity rights, and other offensive acts in interactions.

There have been repeated pleas for the study of emotions, especially the interplay between emotions and relational work, emotions and (im)politeness or other issues of interpersonal pragmatics. For instance, Langlotz and Locher (2008) have examined relational work with attention to the intersection of cognition, interaction, and emotion. They argue that emotions play a quintessential role concerning relational work. When participants display emotions, they signal their evaluation of the ongoing linguistic negotiation of their social relationships as well as the transactional state of the shared practices. These point to a close link between the cognitive and emotional dimensions. Langlotz and Locher (2017) hold that emotions are closely associated with sociality and relationship building, and so some devices and strategies can be employed to manage negative emotions for relationship management in social interactions.

In recent years, emotions or emotional issues in interaction have attracted more attention. For instance, how to do things with emotional expressions is a key issue from the speech act perspective (Scarantino 2017). Scarantino(2017), maintaining that the speech act theory can help to shed light on how emotional expressions convey social motives, proposes a framework of affective pragmatics for exploring the emotional expressions in practice. Within his framework emotions can do various things, similar to speech acts. The interrelatedness between impulsiveness and negative emotional behaviours has been investigated with a focus on destructive emotional arguments (Kienpointner 2008). Negative emotions are found to be destructive, and as a result, impulsiveness can be caused. This proves that (im)politeness is not purely linguistic but an emotional issue. In pragmatics, more attention is paid to emotions or emotional issues in different contexts and cultures, and a special volume on the sociopragmatics of emotion (Alba-Juez and Haugh, forthcoming) is being published. By taking a sociopragmatic perspective, the researchers take the indexical, social, and moral value of the expression of emotion in discourse and interaction into consideration. Such a relatively new approach to the study of emotions allows the readers to see the emotional phenomena in interpersonal, professional or institutional contexts in a clarifying and enriching light.

Dr. Lei's study has made contributions to the study of Chinese ganqing/感情 (emotional attachment or affection), to the study of interpersonal pragmatics in many aspects, and to the study of cultural pragmatics as well. Chinese is a language full of expressions of emotions, such as qinggan/情感 (emotion, affection), gongqing/共情 (empathy, sympathy), tongqing/同情 (sympathy, compassion), wenqing/温情 (tender feeling, warmth), rouqing/柔情 (tender feeling, tenderness), qinqing/亲情(love, affection), youqing/友情 (friendship, camaraderie), manyi/满意 (satisfaction), xihuan/喜欢 (like), and so on. Their variants are also included. Such

expressions or speech acts are related to interpersonal and even social issues, and they are key factors for building rapport and keeping harmony in a society. There exist differences between Chinese and other languages; some can be very subtle. In Chinese qing/情 (emotion) or ganqing/感情 (emotional attachment or affection) is a sensitive topic, which is quite context-bound. On the occasions of family talks or talking about family members, qing/情 (emotion) or ganqing/感情 (emotional attachment or affection) can be strongly loaded with affection (e.g. love or respect), but it might be relationship orientated on other occasions, such as showing a close or good relationship between the addressee(s). In interactions, participants tend to maintain, improve, or control qing/情 (emotion) or ganqing/感情 (emotional attachment or affection) for communicative purposes, but it can also be hurt or even damaged. Negative emotions such as bu xihuan/不喜欢 (dislike, disfavour), fangan/反感 (disgusting, antipathy), exin/恶心 (disgusting, nauseating), taoyan/讨厌 (disagreeable, dislike), fennu/愤怒 (angry), fenben/愤恨 (resent), or chouhen/仇恨(hatred, hostile) tend to cause unexpected outcomes. Impoliteness is one of the negative interpersonal effects. What is worse, it is also likely to bring various kinds of social disorders if negative emotions are not effectively managed. Thus, how to do emotion work or how to do things with emotions is a topic for further research from different perspectives and approaches.

The Pragmatics of Managing Negative Emotions in a Chinese Context is an investigation into ganqing in the Chinese sociocultural context from the perspective of interpersonal pragmatics. Several research gaps regarding emotions, the interplay between emotions and relational work, and emotion management have been addressed within interpersonal pragmatics. Rich Chinese culture-specific examples are provided. It can enrich our emic understanding of negative emotion management and fill some knowledge gaps concerning those underexplored issues of emotional pragmatics. It is a surprising fact that to date no book-length study has been dedicated to the theme. Hopefully, there are more studies from a pragmatic perspective about the functions of emotions in building and managing relationships, the strategic devices for emotion management, the interplay between emotions and (im)politeness or morality, and how to do relational work or things with emotions in the future. I recommend this study most sincerely: reading the present volume is a must.

# 1 Introduction

# 1.1 Motivation for the Pragmatic Study of Emotion Management

Research on emotion management has long been discussed in psychological works, involving both the issues of emotion self-management and the management of others' emotions (e.g., Thompson 1994; Greenberg 2017). Interest in managing emotions has continued growing over the years, especially in health settings. Such a trend is perhaps inevitable, given that managing patients' or clients' emotional status is always a necessity/premise for performing institutional tasks and achieving institutional goals of health communication. Yet despite there being increasing interest in emotion management, only comparatively few studies have explored it from a linguistic tradition (e.g., Ruusuvuori 2007; Voutilainen et al. 2010; Weiste 2016; Hartman 2019; Muntigl et al. 2023), let alone from the perspective of interpersonal pragmatics.

The interplay between perceived rapport and emotional reactions provides a theoretical foundation for examining emotion management from an interpersonal pragmatics perspective. In social interaction, people judge whether the rapport between themselves and others has been enhanced, challenged, or maintained. Threats to rapport tend to create negative emotional reactions (Spencer-Oatey 2005, 2011). Having identified the reasons for co-interlocutors' negative emotions, interlocutors can intervene to effectively manage co-interlocutors' perceptions of rapport, which would bring changes to co-interlocutors' emotional status. This is the foremost rationale behind this investigation.

Other rationales drive this research. First, the existing research on emotions is mainly approached from an outcome-orientated perspective, for instance, how emotions serve as a response to impoliteness/rapport threats (e.g., Spencer-Oatey 2002; Locher & Langlotz 2008; Culpeper 2011; Langlotz & Locher 2012, 2013, 2017). Little empirical research has taken a process-orientated approach, for instance, by examining how perceived rapport threats trigger emotions, and how to manage interlocutors'

emotions through 'relational work', which is assumed to change people's perceptions of rapport (Locher & Watts 2008: 96).

Second, it is mainly under the relational principles/moral orders of Western society or culture that the emotions are interpreted, which calls for an examination of cultural-specific principles/moral orders on the understanding of emotions. As Langlotz and Locher (2013) note, emotions are social and interpersonal phenomena, whose interpretation should be founded on the social and moral backgrounds in which they occur, and should also consider the social roles of interlocutors. Given that most of the existing research on interpersonal emotions is firmly located within Western, mostly English-speaking contexts (e.g., Spencer-Oatey 2005, 2011; Langlotz & Locher 2013, 2017), further studies are thus needed to take an indigenous perspective and to examine the influence of cultural-specific principles on the interpretation of emotions.

Thirdly, the prior research on emotion management has mainly been examined under the framework of conversation analysis (e.g., Voutilainen et al. 2010; Weiste 2016; Muntigl et al. 2017; Hartman 2019), without sufficient consideration of the pragmatic mechanisms underlying emotion management. Given the close link between emotions and interpersonal relationships (e.g., Andersen & Guerrero 1998; Langlotz & Locher 2013, 2017), we can take an interpersonal pragmatics approach and look at how interlocutors' perception of interpersonal relations affects emotion, and how to manage interlocutors' emotional status by impacting upon their perceptions of interpersonal relationships.

Fourthly, the Rapport Management Framework (RMF, Spencer-Oatey 2000, 2008), which focuses primarily on the management of relationships between direct interlocutors, does not fully explain how interlocutors manage harmonious/disharmonious relations that exist between co-interlocutors and non-present third parties (NPTPs). Further research is therefore needed to describe the operation of this type of rapport management.

In addition to the four motivations discussed above, this study is also based on the two possibilities. On the one hand, the psychotherapeutic interaction, which is emotionally intensive in a special way (Fiehler 1990; Pawelczyk 2011), can provide rich and valuable data for the exploration of interpersonal emotions through analyses of longer stretches of psychotherapeutic discourse, how interlocutors' negative emotions are dynamically constructed, what they point to, and how they are managed are observable for linguistic analysis (Langlotz & Locher 2017: 307-310). On the other hand, the meta-pragmatic comments made by interlocutors provide a lens

through which the interrelationship between emotions, principles/social norms/moral orders, and perceptions of rapport (i.e., the quality of the relationship) can be explored. Though interlocutors neither articulate their emotions directly nor refer to their relationship with others through labels such as 'rude', 'inattentive', or 'disrespectful', the 'emotion meta-comments' (Isik-Guler & Ruhi 2010: 638) and 'meta-pragmatic emotions' (Spencer-Oatey 2011) provide a set of linguistic resources that make people's perceptions of rapport, their perceptions regarding the observance or the violation of the social norms/expectations communicatively observable.

# 1.2 Situating the Interpersonal Pragmatics Study of Emotion Management in Psychotherapy Sessions

As a therapeutic school that is recommended for the treatment of depressed feelings, interpersonal psychotherapy (IPT) is a venue for clients' displays of negative emotions. Moreover, as a therapeutic school that is grounded on attachment theory and interpersonal theory (Klerman et al. 1984; Weissman et al. 2018), IPT focuses specifically on interpersonal relationships as a means of bringing about interpersonal change and symptomatic recovery, to help clients improve their interpersonal relationships (Stuart & Robertson 2015: 3). These make IPT sessions a rewarding site for exploring the displaying of emotions, the interplay between emotions and interpersonal relationships, as well as for the examination of emotion management. I will address the management of negative emotions in IPT by drawing the following example. The data is presented to illustrate the research object—negative emotions caused by disharmony (or discordant rapport) between the two parties.

In Turn 1, a client discloses the prior social interactions between her and her father. From the client's complaints directed toward her father, the disharmony between the client and her father is observed. The negative emotions are displayed when she discloses the disharmony that exists between her and her father. On the one hand, her anger towards her father is expressed through the speech act of complaints (Selting 2010, 2012) (Turn 1). On the other hand, a strong degree of sadness is displayed through vocal cues (i.e., sniffing, crying) (Hepburn & Potter 2007; Sorjonen & Peräkylä 2012). Locating these negative emotions within the interactional sequences in which they occur, they are responding to the disharmony between the client and her father (Locher & Langlotz 2008; Isik-Güler & Ruhi 2010). Importantly, it is the disharmony between the client and the NPTP, rather than the disharmony between the therapist and the client, that has triggered the client's negative emotions.

Extract 1-1 C, an adult in her thirties, talks about her prior interaction with her parents (C stands for the client, and T refers to the therapist).

<table><tr><td>1</td><td>→</td><td>C: 我现在能感受到一点的就是，比如我给他买生日礼物的时候，说“生日快乐”的时候，他就说“你妈开心我才开心”，%我会觉得隔着的是我妈，我感受到的就是我根本就不喜欢你，然后就是“你怎么做都没有用”。然后我就觉得，其实我爸和我妈，然后包括弟弟，好像-他们三个才是一家的% (哭))，我就会觉得就真的是捡来的，你只配在家做事((哽咽))……比如当我想看一下电视，当我想要去休息放松，真的想和大家在一起，想把大家当一家人的时候，他们要赶着我去学习，他们好像就是说，你就是-就不配呆在这里，就是赶我走的方式。</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>What I can feel a bit now is, when I bought him a birthday present, for example, when I said &#x27;Happy birthday&#x27; to him, he replied: &#x27;Only when your mother is happy would I be happy&#x27;. % I thought the obstacle was my mother, and I could perceive my father&#x27;s utterance &#x27;I don&#x27;t like you at all, whatever you do is useless&#x27;. Then I thought that my father, my mother, and my younger brother, belonged to one family% ((crying)). Then I would have the feeling that I was the kid who was picked up, I only deserved to do housework at home… ((choking)). For example, when I wanted to watch TV, to relax, to stay with my parents, and to treat them as a family, they would rush me to study. It seemed that they were saying, you just didn&#x27;t deserve to be here, and their words were a way of driving me away.</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td rowspan="2">→</td><td>T: 就说立刻把你打发走。This means to send you away at once.</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>C: 对，“这是你的事情，你做完了，你走吧”，这个就是赶我走的方式((抽鼻子))。可是当时我弟弟也在看，我弟弟当时也被赶走了啊，但是往往最后可能我弟弟也会留下来，走的是我。Yes, &#x27;it was your job. You finished your work, and then you could leave&#x27;. This was the way to drive me away ((sniffing)). But my younger brother was also watching TV at that time, and my brother was driven away as well, but often my brother would end up staying and I would leave.</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>→</td><td>T: 是啊，你是那个寄人篱下的哦。你弟弟是可以任性一点的，你却没有资本任性。Yes, you are the one who is dependent on them for a living. Your younger brother can be a little self-willed, but you don&#x27;t have the capital to be so.</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>→</td><td>C: 而且我真的是可以感觉到对我弟弟的关心。或许是因为我妈妈喜欢我弟弟，所以我爸爸也喜欢我弟弟，我妈妈不喜欢我，所以他也不喜欢我。“如果连你妈妈都开心了，我可能会接受你。”And I can really feel my father&#x27;s concern for my brother. Maybe because my mother likes my younger brother, so does my father, and my mother doesn&#x27;t like me, so my father doesn&#x27;t like me either. &#x27;If your mother is happy, I might accept you&#x27;.</td></tr></table>

To understand the nature of the disharmony that exists between C and her father, the subsequent meta/comments made by C are analyzed. C, by complaining about her father's lack of feelings towards her ('I don't like you at all', Turn 1), and her parents' detachment from her ('my father, my mother, and my younger brother belonged to one family'; 'their words were a way of driving me away', Turn 1), projects negative assessment of her father's (also her mother's) behaviour, evoking evaluations of a threat to ganqing. According to Gao and Ting-Toomey (1998: 23-24), the affective dimensions of Chinese interpersonal relationships are guided by three relational principles, ganqing, renqing, and bao. Ganqing, which is usually found in 'long time and intimate social bonds' (Lee & Dawes 2005: 35), emphasizes 'affection and closeness' and emotional attachment in the parent-child relationship (Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998: 24). Given the role relationship between C and her father, ganqing is considered to be the relational principle that underpins their interaction. From C's complaints, the content of which highlights emotional detachment, it can be drawn that it is her father's violation of the ganqing principle or her unmet ganqing expectation that has resulted in the disharmony between them and has evoked C's negative emotions.

What is discussed above indicates it is the ganqing violation-induced discordant rapport (hereafter GQVI discordant rapport) that exists between clients and NPTPs that has triggered clients' negative emotions. When confronted with negative emotions, therapists are expected and obliged to make some effort to manage them, given that managing clients' negative emotions is one of the most vital tasks therapists face in psychotherapeutic interactions (Pawelczyk 2011: 109). Then, what kind of efforts will be taken by therapists to manage clients' negative emotions, the following extract illustrates one of the many ways adopted by therapists.

Extract 1-2 C, an adult in her thirties, talks about her prior interaction with her parents.

<table><tr><td>8</td><td>→</td><td>T:</td><td>你爸爸可能觉得-对他最大的困难，最难解决的问题就是你妈妈啊，如果能够讨好你妈妈，让你妈妈开心，他就高兴了=Your father might think- the biggest problem for him, the biggest problem to solve is your mother. If you can please your mother and make her happy, he will be happy=</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td></td><td>C:</td><td>=我觉得他是自己没有办法，他没有办法搞定我妈，所以= I don&#x27;t think he can handle it on his own, he can&#x27;t handle my mother, so</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>→</td><td>T:</td><td>=[就把任务=[He gave me the task of pleasing my mother</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td></td><td>C:</td><td>[让我来搞定我妈。[Let me please my mother.</td></tr></table>

<table><tr><td>12</td><td>→</td><td>T: 是啊!感觉对于你爸爸来说，你妈妈对他来说是他解决不了的难题。
Yes! It feels like, for your father, your mother is a problem he can&#x27;t solve.</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td></td><td>C: 其实我觉得我妈妈身上很多东西，他都不喜欢，就不是他喜欢的那种人。
He doesn&#x27;t like a lot of things about my mother; she is simply not the kind of person he likes.</td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>→</td><td>T: 看样子他是没有办法哦。
It seems that he just can&#x27;t help it.</td></tr><tr><td>15</td><td></td><td>C: 对。
That&#x27;s right.</td></tr><tr><td>16</td><td></td><td>[省略6个话轮](T通过举例进一步阐述其观点)。
[6 turns omitted] (T further elaborates on his views with examples).</td></tr><tr><td>23</td><td></td><td>C: 我现在觉得不是很难过了……
I don&#x27;t feel so sad now...</td></tr></table>

Rather than responding directly to C's negative emotions, T speaks on behalf of C's father by mentioning the difficulties he encounters ('your father might think—the biggest problem for him, the biggest problem to solve is your mother', Turn 8). In so doing, T aims to account for C's father's inappropriate behaviour—treating the daughter in an emotionally detached way. From C's response in the next turns (i.e., the formulation of T's utterance), it can be drawn that the account offered by T on behalf of C's father is accepted and acknowledged by C (Lerner 2004; Vehviläinen 2008). The acceptance of the account indicates the restoration of the social order and the bridging of the gap between action and expectation (Scott & Lyman 1968: 46; Firth 1995: 200). As for C's emotional status, a positive change in her emotional status is observable both from her 'verbal descriptions of the emotional states' (Langlotz & Locher 2012: 1601) in Turn 23 and the voice cues when she speaks at the later part of the extract (i.e., she speaks in a normal pitched voice, compared with her sobbing and crying sound at the beginning of this extract, cf. Extract 1-1).

The analysis of the therapist's utterances in Extract 1-2 exemplifies one way employed by therapists to manage clients' negative emotions (i.e., anger and sadness) caused by GQVI discordant rapport during Chinese psychotherapy sessions. Are there any other types of negative emotions evoked by the GQVI discordant rapport that exists between clients and NPTPs? Are there any other ways employed by therapists to manage clients' negative emotions? Is there any factor that would influence therapists' choice of ways when manage clients' negative emotions? These are the key questions to be explored.

Additionally, when therapists manage the disharmony that exists between clients and NPTPs, they speak for NPTPs and provide accounts on their behalf, a phenomenon which is termed 'surrogacy' according to Mazer et al. (2014: 38). Such a way of rapport management, given that it is not the direct rapport management between therapists and clients, but rather a type of rapport management that involves therapists'

undertaking of surrogate roles in managing the rapport between clients and NPTPs, it is termed surrogate-based rapport management in this study. Accordingly, the linguistic strategies taken by therapists to manage the disharmony between clients and NPTPs are termed surrogate-based rapport management strategies.

# 1.3 Objectives of the Study

Based on the motivations/rationales presented in Section 1.1 and the research object exemplified above, this study aims to explore how therapists manage clients' negative emotions through managing GQVI discordant rapport. The following three research questions guide this research:

(1) What types of negative emotions do clients display when they disclose the GQVI discordant rapport that exists between them and NPTPs during psychotherapy sessions? How are these negative emotions displayed?

The task here is to discover the types of negative emotions clients display when GQVI discordant rapport (i.e., unmet ganqing expectation-induced disharmony or unfulfilled ganqing expectation-induced disharmony) is being disclosed. The identification and classification of these emotions are based on the following emotional cues: (a) the verbal cues (e.g., language-specific emotion words/vocabularies, speech acts) and vocal cues (e.g., voice quality) that are documented in the literature (Planalp 1998; Hepburn & Potter 2007; Selting 2010; Prior 2016; Räabis et al. 2019) and ways for implicating emotions (Langlotz & Locher 2012) will be referenced for the identification of different types of negative emotions; and (b) the types of negative emotions, which involve both self-conscious emotions, other-condemning emotions (Haidt 2003; Culpeper 2011), and sadness, are discussed under the types of GQVI discordant rapport.

In addition to focusing on the types of negative emotions, the study also explores how emotions are named and expressed. For instance, whether they are implicitly or explicitly manifested, and whether the models of emotional displays are associated with the sociocultural backgrounds of the interactants (e.g., high-context culture or low-context culture).

(2) How are these negative emotions managed by therapists through surrogate-based rapport management strategies?

Therapists are dedicated to managing the disharmony between clients and NPTPs when they are managing clients' negative emotions, given that it is the disharmony between clients and NPTPs that has evoked these emotions. Drawing on the RMF, the participation structure of dyadic therapist-client

interactions (the two actual participants are the therapist and the client, and the NPTP is the virtual participant), and the discourse role therapists undertake when they are managing the disharmony between clients and NPTPs, surrogate-based RMF is proposed. Based on these, different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies employed by therapists to manage negative emotions are examined.

(3) Why do therapists employ different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies to manage these negative emotions?

The contextual factor that influences therapists' choice of the types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies will be discussed. The social distance between clients and NPTPs, that is, whether it is featured as a higher degree of intimacy or a lower degree of intimacy is found to be a key factor that influences therapists' employment of surrogate-based rapport management strategies when managing clients' negative emotions.

The objectives of the book are aimed at proposing a ganqing approach to rapport (management) and bringing broader im/politeness insights. On the one hand, the analysis will show how violation of the cultural-specific ganqing principle results in damaged rapport between two parties, adding to recent work on rapport (management).

The threats to the harmonious relationship, therefore, are not limited to face-threatening behaviour; equity rights/association rights-threatening behaviour, renqing-threatening behaviour, qingmian-threatening behaviour, and ganqing-threatening behaviour are also involved. On the other hand, the Chinese concept of ganqing, which encompasses concern for the feelings of others by showing consideration and attentiveness, resonates with many previous discussions on politeness. For instance, Kádár and Pan (2010) argue that showing emotional 'warmth' (wenqing,温情) is a demonstration of politeness in Chinese communication. Zhai (2006) finds that 'real' emotions constitute an important part of politeness in mainland China, and Fukushima and Haugh (2014) analyze attentiveness (title/zhoudao,体贴/周到) as the moral order that underpins evaluations of im/politeness by Chinese-Taiwanese participants. Other studies (Gu 1990; Ruhi 2009) also discuss the interrelationship between emotion and politeness. Therefore, an exploration into ganqing in the Chinese sociocultural context would enrich the emic understanding of politeness (Zhao & Ran 2019), which remains largely underexplored.

# 1.4 Organization of the Book

The whole book, which revolves around the three research questions above, encompasses nine chapters. This chapter offers a general introduction, including the motivations, rationales, and objectives of

the research. The research object is further exemplified by drawing on interpersonal psychotherapist-client conversations in a Chinese context. The contents of the remaining chapters are as follows. Chapter 2 works towards an interpersonal pragmatics analysis of emotion management. Prior research on emotion and emotion management is reviewed, in particular, the role of rapport management in emotion management is laid out. These early studies are argued to be the foundation on which the conceptual framework is built. Chapter 3 focuses on proposing a ganqing approach to rapport (management). Its necessity, its components, and its application in pragmatics research are discussed. The conceptual framework of the study is introduced in Chapter 4. The rationale for choosing Spencer-Oatey's RMF as the theoretical foundation, the explanatory power when applying analysis to interlocutors' management of disharmony between co-interlocutors and NPTPs, and the solutions for the deficiencies are presented. Then, a rapport-based RMF is put forth and the model of rapport-orientated management of negative emotions is proposed.

Chapter 5 deals with data and methods, including the data sources, data features, standards for coding negative emotions, standards for coding GQVI discordant rapport, standards for coding surrogate-based rapport management strategies, the interactional approach for analyzing the data, and the dimensions for observing the effect of therapists' intervention on clients' negative emotions. Chapter 6 lays out the types of negative emotions clients display when disclosing the GQVI discordant rapport that exists between them and NPTPs. The different types of GQVI discordant rapport and the rapport threats are also presented. Chapter 7 examines how different types of negative emotions are managed by therapists via different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies. Chapter 8 accounts for therapists' use of different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies when managing clients' negative emotions. Chapter 9 is a summary of the major research findings. The theoretical and practical implications, the limitations, and the future directions are discussed.
