# 2 Towards an Interpersonal Pragmatics Analysis of Emotion Management

# 2.1 Introduction

This chapter works towards an interpersonal pragmatics analysis of emotion management. More specifically, it focuses on the interplay between evaluations of rapport and emotional reactions, and how perceptions of disharmony evoke negative emotions in particular. I will start with definitions and expressions of emotions, and then move to the causes of negative emotions. The focus will be on the relational triggers of negative emotions and rapport/relationship management. The final section of this chapter outlines the under-researched area of negative emotions and their management, which serves as the rationale behind the study of emotion management from an interpersonal pragmatics approach, as well as the foundation for proposing the conceptual framework of the study.

# 2.2 Definitions and Linguistic Expressions of Emotions

# 2.2.1 Definitions of Emotions

Emotions are conceptualized as socially and culturally shaped products in the eyes of linguists (Harré 1986; Goddard 2002). Like the nature of emotions in linguistics, emotions in the field of pragmatics are not psychological entities or inner psychological status (Planalp 1999), but rather 'externalized and communicative phenomenon' and 'socially and culturally shaped and malleable products' (Langlotz & Locher 2013:91). Emotions are not individual phenomena but rather interactive phenomena that are emergent in social interaction (Günthner 2011; Sorjonen & Peräkylä 2012; Langlotz & Locher 2017).

# 2.2.2 Types of Emotions

Broadly speaking, emotions can be classified as positive and negative emotions (Eisenberg et al. 1998). Positive emotions consist of emotion types such as love, joy, desire, etc., while negative emotions refer to feelings

that people want to avoid, sadness, fear, and anger being the typical ones. To describe the emotions that are closely associated with social norms and cultural expectations of a society or community, the notion of moral emotions is proposed (Haidt 2003; Tangney et al. 2007). According to Haidt (2003), moral emotions are 'those emotions that are linked to the interest or welfare either of society as a whole or at least of a person other than the judge or agent' (p.853). Moreover, Haidt (2003) argues that typical moral emotions can be identified from two dimensions: (1) whether these emotions are self-interested or disinterested, and (2) whether these emotions result in moral, prosocial actions. He further distinguishes two types of negatively valenced moral emotions: (1) other-condemning emotions, which involve anger, disgust, and contempt; and (2) self-conscious emotions, which encompass embarrassment, shame, and guilt. As for sadness, it is low on both counts mentioned above. According to Culpeper (2011: 61), sadness involves 'self-concern and results in lethargy or actions to help the self'. Based on this classification, Culpeper (2011) discusses the interconnections between the type of negatively valenced moral emotions and the type of impoliteness perceived by interlocutors. Impoliteness triggered by violations of sociality rights is probably followed by other-condemning emotions of anger, disgust, and contempt, while threats to one's face are more likely to evoke self-conscious emotions such as embarrassment and shame.

The emotions concerned in this study involve both other-condemning emotions (i.e., anger) and self-conscious emotions (i.e., guilt). Sadness is also widely observed. Rather than displaying alone, sadness is always accompanied by anger or guilt. According to Langlotz and Locher (2017: 310), under some circumstances, interlocutors are entangled in the emotional 'interaction chains' (i.e., emotions that trigger further emotions). In line with this argument, sadness in this study is considered a type of negative emotion triggered by anger or guilt.

# 2.2.3 Linguistic Expressions of Emotions

As a social construct, one's emotional status can be displayed through complex patterns of emotional cues. Adopting a two-partite distinction, Ochs and Schieffelin (1989) argue that emotions are displayed non-verbally and/or verbally. The non-verbal means involves facial expressions, gestures, and body orientation; while the verbal means of emotional signaling encompasses an emotional lexicon and grammatical and discourse structures. Clark (1996) maintains that emotions can be expressed through describing (e.g., I am sad), indicating (e.g., pointing one's finger toward someone as a bodily indication of anger), and demonstrating (e.g., imitating the furious expression of the interlocutor during a conflictual event). According to Planalp (1998), emotions are

signalled through vocal cues, verbal cues, body cues, physiological cues, and facial cues. Fiehler (2002) argues that emotions are expressed in the following ways: experiential declarative formulas (e.g., I feel sad); frozen metaphorical idioms (e.g., it boils within me), and the metaphorical use of expressions (e.g., I was completely blocked). Similarly, Schwarz-Friesel (2007) contends that emotions can be signalled through verbal expression, visual intensification, verbal descriptions, and meta/comments.

More emotion-signaling strategies are reported. Drawing on an ethnographical approach, Samra-Fredericks (2010) observes that emotions can be expressed verbally through emotive labels, and non-verbal cues such as frowning, clenched fists, and clenched teeth. Other cues, such as the tempo of voice and the emphasis on specific words or phrases, are also important indicators of emotions. By investigating data collected from the Mailonline site, Langlotz and Locher (2012) find that interlocutors' emotional stances are signalled through expressions (e.g., exclamations, intensification, name-calling, verbalization of emotional reactions, smileys, interjections, and emotional constructions); implications, lexical connotations, metaphors, and their stylistic implications; emotion words, verbal descriptions/ascription of emotional states. Analyzing data from the Chinese discussion forum, Gong (2014) reports that online commentators mainly use the emotional strategies of direct expression, implicit indexing, and explicit description to index their emotional stances in online conflictive commentaries. In a recent study, Raabis et al. (2019) examine the linguistic means used by the complainant to express and name emotions in Estonian school-related complaints. Syntactic means, lexical means (e.g., intensifiers and exaggerations, extreme case formulations, swear words), rhetorical means (e.g., repetition, rhetorical question, irony, imitations), and naming emotions are frequently used.

Generally speaking, interlocutors' emotional statuses are communicated through linguistic and paralinguistic resources, so a composite means of emotion representation is always adopted. What's more, multi-modal analyses of emotions have been developed in the ethnography of communication (Goodwin & Goodwin 2001; Goodwin et al. 2002; Garcés-Conejos Blitvich 2022). These reviews shed light on the recognition of clients' negative emotions. Emotion lexicon, implications, meta-pragmatic emotions, the speech act of complaints, vocal cues, and prosodic cues such as crying, and sniffing, are the main emotional cues that are referred to in this study.

# 2.3 Causes of Negative Emotions

In linguistic studies, the nature of negative emotions and their causes is discussed. This section reviews the causes of negative emotions in the field of linguistics with an emphasis on studies in the field of pragmatics.

# 2.3.1 Linguistic Studies on Causes of Negative Emotions

Though the research topic of emotions has been extensively explored in linguistic studies, only several of them have touched upon the causes of negative emotions. For instance, it is reported that negative emotions are associated with frustrating situations. Neglect, abuse, and trauma are all incidents that give rise to negative emotions (Paivio & Pascual-Leone 2010; Muntigl et al. 2017). Other scholars (e.g., Jones 2001; Guerrero & La Valley 2006; Langlotz & Locher 2013) argue that negative emotions are closely related to conflict issues. In Guerrero and La Valley (2006), an interconnection between emotion types and their causes in conflict situations is reported: anger and annoyance are related to face attack, aggression, threat, unfairness, inequity, or incompetent behaviours; while the feelings of hurt and irritation are caused by conflict types such as accusations, threats, negative evaluations, lies, irony, or sarcasm. Guilt, with subtypes of shame, embarrassment, and regret, is correlated with relational/role obligations, normative standards, or being considered as worse by others.

The above literature indicates that negative emotions have their elicitors, including frustrating situations, and discrepancies in claims or beliefs (e.g., disagreements or conflicts between interlocutors). Some relational factors, such as face threats, are mentioned vaguely. This limitation motivates the exploration of negative emotions from an interpersonal pragmatics perspective and examines the underlying causes of negative emotions.

# 2.3.2 Pragmatic Studies on Triggers of Negative Emotions

In pragmatic literature, negative emotions are evoked by face threat/impoluteness (e.g., Goffman 1967; Culpeper 2011; Langlotz& Locher 2012; Vergis & Terkourafi 2015), and rapport threat (Spencer-Oatey 2002, 2005, 2008; Spencer-Oatey & Xing 2003; Spencer-Oatey & Franklin 2009).

(1) Face threat. The strong link between face and emotion has been discussed in early works. In Goffman's (1955, 1967) introduction of face into the academic world, face is referred to as 'the positive social value a person effectively claims for himself by the line others assume he has taken during a particular contact' (Goffman 1955: 213). According to him, face is both an individual and collective concept, the threatening or losing of which would bring emotional consequences. Individually, if an event fails to establish a face a person might have expected, he will 'feel bad' or 'feel hurt' (Goffman 1967: 6). Collectively, the members share a face in many relationships, thus a member's improper act when in the presence of a third party would become 'a source of acute embarrassment to the other members' (ibid: 41). In Brown

and Levinson's politeness theory, face is conceptualized as 'the public self-image that every member wants to claim for himself' (Brown & Levinson 1987: 61) and two types of face are distinguished. Positive face concerns one's desire to be approved by others, while negative face involves one's desire to be unimpeded in his/her actions. Furthermore, face is a vulnerable phenomenon associated with emotional reactions. Ting-Toomey and Cocroft (1994) observe that if one's face is violated, feelings of anxiety are unavoidable. Shimanoff (1994) uses the term 'face-threatening emotions' to describe the close link between face and emotions. People react with embarrassment when he/she loses face.

More studies have supported the interlink between face threats and negative emotions. For instance, Spencer-Oatey (2007) states that 'face is associated with affective sensitivity' (p. 644). If one's face is damaged or threatened, emotional reactions like anger (e.g., irritation, frustration) and sadness (e.g., displeasure, shame/guilt, embarrassment/insult) would follow. Similarly, Ruhi (2009) contends that damage to one's face gives rise to emotional reactions such as embarrassment, shame, and anger. Gerholm (2011) maintains that children's perceptions of face threats are associated with sadness and embarrassment.

Examining interlocutors' emotional reactions from the perspective of face threat illuminates the understanding of clients' negative emotions in this study. Since face concerns primarily people's public self-image, therefore, interpersonal interactions that occur within the context of family or between close friends without outsiders are less sensitive to face. A synthesized examination of the interactional environment, an interlocutor's role, each party's rights, and obligations, and morality-based expectations are needed when a participant's negative emotions are to be interpreted (Langlotz & Locher 2013, 2017).

(2) Impoliteness. As a derivational concept of traditional politeness theory, impoliteness has attracted abundant research attention over the years. Scholars have examined the impoliteness phenomenon in a variety of interpersonal contexts such as military training discourse (e.g., Culpeper 1996; Bousfield 2007), computer-mediated interaction (Graham 2007; Bou-Franch & Garcés-Conejos Blitvich 2014), classroom settings (Dobs & Garcés-Conejos Blitvich 2013), local governance meetings (Tracy 2008), legal discourse (e.g., Archer 2011), mediation contexts (Zhao & Ran 2019), political discourse (e.g., Zhou 2020; Zhu & Zhu 2022), etc.

The interplay between morality, impulsiveness, and people's emotional reactions is frequently discussed. On the one hand, the evaluation of

impoliteness is based on social norms or relational principles (e.g., Culpeper 2005, 2011; Locher & Watts 2008; Tayebi 2016). The notion of morality, which concerns the obligations associated with interlocutors, is made clear by Goffman (1967: 49) in his early work:

Rules of conduct impinge upon the individual in two general ways: directly, as obligations establishing how he is morally constrained to conduct himself; indirectly as expectations, establishing how others are morally bound to act concerning him.

The violation of social norms leads to evaluations of immorality and evaluations of impoliteness (Culpeper 2011). When social norms are infringed, the offences interlocutors perceive can be reflected through their responses or counter-arguments to co-interlocutors (Parvaresh & Tayebi 2018).

On the other hand, negative emotional reactions are the result of impolite acts/events (e.g., Culpeper 2011; Langlotz & Locher 2012, 2013; Garcés-Conejos Blitvich 2013). In Isik-Güler (2008), the acts or events which might give rise to negative emotions involve face attacks, rights offences, expressive impulsiveness, inattentiveness to others, disregard of custom, aggressive/offensive self-presentation, self-emotion mismanagement, physical and mental bullying. It is widely accepted that impulsiveness leads to negative emotions. For instance, Culpeper (1996) maintains that 'impoliteness strategies are orientated towards attacking face, an emotionally sensitive concept of the self' (p. 350).

The association between emotions and impulsiveness has been further examined from a cross-cultural perspective. Culpeper et al. (2014) address the question of whether people from different cultures experience different emotions when they are confronted with impulsiveness events. An analysis of 500 reports of impulsiveness events generated by undergraduates from England, Finland, Germany, Turkey, and China finds that key concepts such as 'face' and 'sociality rights' are closely related to specific emotions; cognition plays an essential role in the interpretation of impulsiveness. The results show fewer cultural variations at a higher level of emotional categories, but more at the sub-level of emotions.

Data from more contextual settings support the claim that impoliteness would evoke negative emotions. Locher and Watts (2005) contend that impolite verbal behaviours such as coercive impositions, insults, and threats, tend to trigger negative emotions. Analyzing military training discourse, Bousfield (2007) reports that military impoliteness is marked based on two factors: one concerns the ability to create an emotional response in recruits, and the other concerns the ability to make soldiers obey the order. Drawing upon asynchronous online newspaper comments, Langlotz and Locher (2012) report how disagreements among online readers reflect

face attacks and how the disagreements between interlocutors escalate into interpersonal conflicts, resulting in interlocutors' negative emotional displays.

The close link between impulsiveness and emotion is also reflected in the way impulsiveness is defined. In Kienpointer (2008), impulsiveness is referred to as 'a kind of prototypically non-cooperative or competitive communicative behaviour' which disrupts interpersonal relations and creates the 'emotional atmosphere of mutual irrelevance and antipathy' (p. 245). More studies support the argument that interlocutors have a range of emotional reactions when they perceive impulsiveness (e.g., Dobs & Gracés-Conejos Blitvich 2013).

In sum, the existing research on impulsiveness enlightens the understanding of negative emotions in interpersonal communication. That is, negative emotions can either be the result of impulsiveness or the trigger of impolite behaviours. Moreover, it is based on moral order that people make judgments on social acts, about whether they are polite or impolite.

(3) Rapport threat. People's perceptions of rapport or dynamic assessment of the bases of rapport often result in significant emotional reactions. The definition of rapport varies slightly in Spencer-Oatey's works. In her early studies, rapport is referred to as 'the relative harmony and smoothness of relations between people' (Spencer-Oatey 2005: 96). In Spencer-Oatey and Franklin (2009), the scope of rapport has been expanded and it is referred to as 'people's subjective perceptions of (dis)harmony, smoothness-turbulence, and warmth-antagonism in interpersonal relations' (p. 102). Whatever definition the researcher adopts, the bases of rapport include face sensitivities, behavioural expectations, and interactional goals. When people interact with each other, they make dynamic judgments as to whether they have gained face, or lost face, whether their behavioural expectations have been met, and whether their interactional goals have been achieved. People's dynamic judgment of rapport is accompanied by their emotional responses (Spencer-Oatey 2005: 116).

The claim that perceptions of rapport are associated with people's emotional reactions is supported empirically. Based on interview data from participants of four different workplace project partnerships, Spencer-Oatey (2011) reports the interconnection between interpersonal relationships and emotions. Drawing on interviewees' meta-pragmatic emotional comments, the relational issues or relational matters among participants are studied. In workplace settings where people focus more on goal-related issues, people's meta-pragmatic emotional comments could conceptualize 'the relational' in pragmatics in a better way than the evaluative judgments of others' (im)politeness. In a study that explores

relating at work from the grassroots perspective, Spencer-Oatey (2013) proposes a model to delineate the facets of 'relating at work'. The relational atmosphere, relational networks, contextual factors, relational dynamics, relating goals, and relating strategies are the major constituents. People's emotional reactions, identity dynamics, and rapport dynamics are the three sub-components of relational dynamics.

Culpeper's (2011) study further supports the interconnection between perceptions of rapport and emotional reactions. Rather than directly focusing on people's perception of rapport, he explores the interpersonal relationship from the perspective of impoliteness (Culpeper 2011: 48). When interlocutors' quality face is offended, the dominant emotional reaction would be sadness, followed by anger and fear; when interlocutors' equity rights are violated, it would evoke negative emotions of sadness, fear, and emotions of being threatened, intimated, or offended, etc. When people's association rights are violated, negative emotions of sadness, anger, and fear tend to ensue.

A review of Spencer-Oatey's work and the relevant ones has important implications for this study: (1) people's dynamic perceptions/assessments/judgments on the nature of interpersonal relations (i.e., whether it is smooth, disharmonious, conflictual) tend to have a great impact on one's emotional status. (2) People's assessments/judgments on rapport can be observed from the meta-pragmatic emotion comments they made to the relational issues or relational matters. The meta-pragmatic emotion comments can provide a window for people to observe rapport/interpersonal relations. (3) The association between perceptions of rapport and people's emotional reactions is mainly examined in Western academia, with few indigenous studies having been done. For a more comprehensive understanding of the interplay between rapport and emotion types, it is necessary to expand the research by examining relational issues in a wider variety of interactional environments and sociocultural contexts.

# 2.4 Management of Negative Emotions in Linguistic Studies

The major concern of this section is to review the prior linguistic studies on emotion management. Broadly speaking, emotion management distinguishes between the self-management of emotion and the interactional management of emotion (Beebe & Lachmann 2002: 22). Self-management, which concerns the 'regulatory processes within individuals' (Peräkylä & Ruusuvuori 2012: 91), has been mainly discussed in psychological studies and communication studies (e.g., Planalp 1999; Greenberg 2004; Gross & Thompson 2007). For instance, Planalp (1999) proposes two strategies for managing emotions in interpersonal communications: (a) surface acting, which concerns how the communicators alter their emotional status through stimulating, intensifying, prohibiting, or masking their emotional

expressions, to adapt to the appropriate means of emotional display; and (b) deep acting, which concerns communicators' attempt to change their inner emotional states. It involves practices such as readjusting appraisals to certain events, avoiding negative emotion-sensitive events, and changing one's psychological reactions by taking medications. The interactional management of emotions, which has been predominantly investigated in linguistic studies (e.g., sociolinguistic studies, conversation analysis, and pragmatic studies), will be reviewed and critically commented on to highlight the key empirical gaps.

# 2.4.1 Prior Linguistic Studies on Emotion Management in Non-institutional Settings

The displays of emotions and their interactional management are discussed in everyday interactions. Drawing on face-to-face complaint storytelling, Selting (2010) examines how the teller displays anger or indignation through rhetorical, lexico-semantic, syntactic, and phonetic-phonological resources, and how the recipients react to the tellers' negative emotions by enacting a matching emotion. In Selting (2012), the ways through which the recipient of complaint stories react to the tellers' negative emotions are examined. Specifically, the recipient tunes with the teller through 'tellings of a complaint story of their own' (p. 387). Couper-Kuhlen (2012) reports that the recipients match the complaint storytellers' negative emotions through prosodic matching. Examining news announcements between strangers in the context of homestays, Maynard and Freese (2012) find that the recipient reacts to the positive valence or negative valence reflected in the news by using matching prosodic cues. Such a way of reacting contributes to interpersonal harmony and creates a sense of sharedness. In the context of the telling of amusing stories, Selting (2017) reports that affiliation is the recipient's preferred response to the teller's affectivities.

In sum, the recipient's tuning with or affiliating with the speaker's display of emotions (both positive emotions and negative emotions) is the preferred means of managing emotions in daily interactions. Reacting in such a way is constructive to the relational harmony between the two parties.

# 2.4.2 Prior Linguistic Studies on Emotion Management in Institutional Settings

Compared with emotion management in mundane interactions, the management of emotions in institutional contexts is more goal-orientated. The relevant literature will be reviewed by the types of institutional settings. Firstly, the interaction between doctors and patients in general medical settings involves emotion management. Diagnostic news delivery is an

activity in which the management of emotions is highly relevant (Maynard & Frankel 2006; Heritage & Lindström 2012). For instance, when doctors are delivering bad news to patients, they tend to preface it with hesitations, and neutral or even positive evaluations. Doctors' shrouding of bad news in such a manner indicates that they have pre-empted the negative influence that the bad news might have brought on the patient's emotional status. Additionally, when patients are describing their troubles, the moment which is characterized by patients' negative emotions, the professionals respond by displaying understanding, expressing compassion, or aligning with patients' talk (Ruusuvuori 2007).

Secondly, psychotherapy and counselling are rewarding sites for exploring emotion management, given that clients' mental and emotional transformation is the primary institutional task of psychotherapeutic interaction (e.g., Antaki et al. 2007; Pawelczyk 2011; Peräkylä & Sorjonen 2012). The analyses reveal the following:

(a) (Re)formulation has been a key resource therapists use to manage clients' negative emotions (Rae 2008). Weiste (2016) finds that when psychiatric clinicians are confronted with outpatients' maladaptive emotional states during their self-disclosure of emotion-related experiences, they adopt two formulation sequences to manage negative emotions. In the first, the clinician recognizes what has been described by the outpatients as valid. In the second, the clinician redirects the outpatient's troublesome experiences through the formulation sequences.

(b) The role of grammatical resources in managing negative emotions is explored. Hartman (2019) examines how what-if conditional constructions are utilized by therapists and clients to manage tasks that are relevant to emotions. Clients use what-if constructions to express worry and doubt, while therapists use what-if constructions to manage clients' emotions since these constructions can re-frame clients' worries, provide an alternative perspective for clients' reference, and stimulate positive changes.

(c) Specific interactional structures (i.e., chair work) have been employed by therapists to manage clients' negative emotions. Drawing naturally occurring data from emotion-focused psychotherapy sessions, Muntigl et al. (2017) investigate how therapists use 'chair work' to guide clients to talk about a conflicted aspect of self or with non-present significant others to cope with their unresolved negative emotions.

(d) The practices of recognition and interpretation are also resources that are used by therapists to manage patients' negative emotional status, as reported by Voutilainen et al. (2010). Pawelczyk (2011) maintains that therapists can provide emotional support and manage emotions

by aligning with clients' prior turn emotionally (e.g., using the grammatical form of interjection), validating or normalizing client's situation, and mirroring (e.g., therapists referencing their own experiences) (Pawelczyk 2011: 187-195).

Thirdly, telephone helplines, emergency call lines and airlines are institutional sites where emotion management is investigated (e.g., Whalen & Zimmerman 1998; Shaw & Kitzinger 2013; Widdershoven et al. 2021). Drawing on 911 calls, Whalen and Zimmerman (1998) examine how the call takers respond to callers' affective displays (e.g., crying, expressions of sorrow), the latter of which would negatively influence the communicative exchange. To facilitate the collection of essential information and to keep track of interaction, call takers actively manage their own and callers' emotions by taking practices such as remaining calm, using directives (e.g., calming down), and consoling the caller. Fuoli and Bednarek (2021) draw on data from the Twitter profiles of 13 major airlines to explore how an agent handles callers' emotions as well as managing his/her own.

In sum, the relational issues in causing and managing emotions have been overlooked in the prior studies. Since the research object of this study is negative emotions and interpersonal interaction is the most important elicitor, it is necessary to consider the role of rapport management in the process of emotion management.

# 2.5 Rapport Management in Emotion Management

# 2.5.1 Necessity of Rapport Management

The term 'relational management' adopted by Isik-Güler and Ruhi (2010: 629) is similar to the 'rapport management' proposed by Spencer-Oatey (2005), both of which concern 'the management (mismanagement) of relations between people' (ibid: 96). The interconnection between emotion and rapport management has been characterized as bi-directional. On the one hand, (mis)management of relationships often results in significant emotional reactions. On the other hand, (mis)management of relationships can be triggered by specific emotions (e.g., Isik-Güler & Ruhi 2010; Vergis & Terkourafi 2015). In what follows, the dimensions of rapport management in negative emotion management are reviewed.

# 2.5.2 Dimensions of Rapport Management

# (1) Managing Rapport Threats

Previous work indicates that emotions are not only a product of face and a reflection of changes in face, but they also motivate the changes in face status through facework practices (e.g., Ruhi & Isik-Güler 2007; Locher

& Langlotz 2008; Gerholm 2011; Qi 2011; Ran & Zhao 2018). Negative emotions are the drivers of facework practices, and these practices concern how an interlocutor controls his/her embarrassment (Goffman 1967: 13). Two types of facework practices are proposed: avoidance facework practices and corrective facework practices. The avoidance facework is employed by a person to prevent threats to his/her face, while corrective facework functions to restore face when one's face has been at risk or has been lost.

Facework and facework strategies have been extensively discussed (e.g., Brown 1977; Brown & Levinson 1987; Penman 1990; Lim & Bowers 1991; Cupach & Metts 1994; Shimanoff 1994; Ting-Toomey & Kurogi 1998; Locher & Watts 2005; Archer & Jagodzinski 2015). Among these studies, some scholars examine facework from an avoidance or a preventive perspective, while others concentrate on the interactional process it undergoes. According to Brown and Levinson (1987), facework is limited to the redressing of face threat to maintaining face equilibrium. Similarly, Locher and Watts (2005) consider facework as an appropriate and polite behaviour that is used to mitigate face threats.

When the interlocutor's face has been at risk and it has resulted in negative emotional reactions, corrective or restorative facework practices are expected. In addition to the notion of 'corrective facework' (Goffman 1967), restorative facework is noted in Brown (1977). This type of facework concerns the actions taken to 'repair damage to lost face [and are] occurring in response to events that have already transpired. Thus, it is past-orientated and defensive' (Brown 1977: 281).

There is a rich body of work concerning how interlocutors repair or rehabilitate face through restorative facework practices, with accounting functioning as an important means of achieving this. Scott and Lyman (1968) note that accounts are 'talk used to transform pejorative ascriptions and resolve problematic events' (Buttny 1993: 16). The role of emotion and affect in accounting transgressive behaviour is explicitly manifested in Buttny (1993). The negative emotions resulting from transgressive behaviours (i.e., face threat) motivate the enactment of accounting practices. Some accounting practices and their linguistic realizations are discussed. In Holtgraves' (1992) study, four types of accounting strategies that function as face support are reported: (1) justification: admitting responsibility but minimizing offensiveness; (2) excuse: admitting offensiveness but minimizing responsibility; (3) concession: admitting offensiveness and responsibility; and (4) refusal: admitting nothing. Since accounts can change the original meaning (usually the worst possible reading or virtual offences) that interlocutors might have given to action, accounts thus have a face-preserving or rehabilitating function.

The role of face management in negative emotion management has been supported by empirical studies in the field of pragmatics. Drawing on

parent-child interaction, Gerholm (2011) reports on the moments when children's negative emotions arise due to face threats. These children, when they are rejected by peers, their misconceptions are disclosed by others, or they are commented on by their peers as lacking sufficient knowledge, would display negative emotions (e.g., crying, changing prosody, and changing facial expressions). These surfaced emotions, which encompass sadness, embarrassment, shame, and hurt, are linked to the notion of face, i.e., the children react to their threatened dignity and self-esteem unpleasantly by displaying these negative emotions. Children's negative emotions have motivated parental facework practices since the parents try to restore face on the children's behalf. For instance, when a child exhibits sadness because of interpersonal rejection, the explanation (i.e., a kind of facework) is enacted by the mother to restore the child's threatened self-esteem. When a child feels embarrassed because a misconception is disclosed by others, the father clarifies the confusion and bypasses the predicament, aiming to help the child restore face.

Based on data collected from intervention-based reality TV, Gordon (2011) examines the ways that parents negotiate parental responsibilities for their kids' poor eating and exercising habits and simultaneously repair their parental face and the threatened identities. The practice of accounting is utilized by parents to deal with face threats.

Interlocutors' emotional reactions play a significant role in social accounts, the latter of which can explain behaviour, repair fractured social interaction, and thus manage interlocutors' negative emotions.

In addition to excuses, facework practices of justifications, physical remediation, avoidance, and apologies are frequently utilized by interlocutors to heal damaged relationships or to reassert one's capabilities (Brown 1977; Cupach & Metts 1994; Ting-Toomey 2004). Since face and facework are culture-specific notions, some cultural-unique facework practices are reported. For instance, Al Zidjaly (2012) reports that when Muslims' identities are attacked on the website of Al Jazeera—a noted Arabian political news agency—linguistic practices such as 'self-attack' and 'self-praise' are employed by Muslim Arabs to save their collective face.

To sum up, accounting serves as an important facework practice as well as a linguistic practice to manage negative emotions caused by face threats. Moreover, there is a lack of empirical research that examines the role of face management in negative emotion management.

# (2) Managing Behavioural Expectations

Behavioural expectations are one of the three major bases on which people make judgments or assessments about the quality of their interpersonal relations with others (Spencer-Oatey & Franklin 2009). It is based

on principles, behavioural conventions, rituals, norms, protocols, and contractual or legal agreements that people use to make judgments about the social appropriateness of behaviours. People use evaluative comments (e.g., warm, friendly, considerate, respectful, rude) to reflect their perceptions of social relations and the rapport between interlocutors (Spencer-Oatey 2005, 2011).

People's judgments or assessments regarding whether their behavioural expectancies are met can often evoke/elicit significant emotional reactions, either the emotions of themselves or the emotional reactions of others. The emotions, especially negative emotions need to be handled properly if the interactional goals are concerned and the interpersonal relations are taken into consideration. Very few studies have examined emotion management triggered by unmet behavioural expectations. One exception can be found in Ho (2017), who, drawing on negative reviews posted by travellers on websites such as Trip Advisor, Orbitz, and Expedia, examines how the hotel management responds to these negative reviews. The negative comments, which comprise criticism, complaints, and unfavourable comments, can be understood as travellers' display of negative emotions due to unmet behavioural expectations (i.e., the expected kind of service defined by the contractual requirements between the hotel and its customers). To manage rapport with dissatisfied customers and to achieve service recovery, rapport management strategies are explored. In managing face sensitives, the manager shows gratitude to customers' negative comments to make customers feel they are respected and important. In attending to association rights, the manager expresses his feelings upon reading those negative comments, aiming to form an effective association. By giving customers options and minimizing the impositions upon them, customers' equity rights are attended to. In managing interactional goals, the manager reacts by acknowledging the problems mentioned by the customers, explaining the triggers of the problem, or describing actions taken to resolve the problems.

Ran and Zhao (2018) is another study that concerns the management of interlocutors' negative emotions resulting from unmet behavioural expectations. Utilizing data from interpersonal conflict mediation between acquaintances in the Chinese context, the researchers discuss how the unmet culturally-based expectations of qingmian evoke interpersonal conflicts and interlocutors' negative emotions (e.g., anger, disappointment). To resolve conflicts between the two conflicting parties and to attend to the offender's negative emotional reactions, a mutual affection-based face is built by the mediator. Qingmian practices such as soliciting the offender's request for mortification, showing deference, soliciting attentiveness, and soliciting reciprocity are enacted by the mediator to manage the unmet expectations of qingmian between two conflicting parties.

Since other studies that focus on rapport management strategies might shed light on negative emotion management caused by violations of behavioural expectations, this section will review rapport management strategies that are discussed in a range of interactional environments, involving both oral communication and written communication.

Rapport management strategies in mundane conversations. García (2009) examines how Peruvian Spanish speakers manage behavioural expectations, face wants, and interactional wants when congratulating others. In tending to co-interlocutors' association rights, interlocutors pay more attention to empathy than to involvement. Specifically, empathy is expressed through discursive moves of approving, congratulating, and expressing good wishes. Besides, interlocutors' identity face is enhanced through expressions of approval/acceptance, the expression of happiness, hinting at one's desire to be invited, and the use of grounders.

Cultural factors play a significant part in the management of rapport. Based on social talk collected from the task-orientated interactions of Japanese and Thai speakers, Aoki (2010) conducts a comparative study to explore how Japanese speakers and Thai speakers construct rapport in casual group talk. For Japanese participants, rapport is primarily reflected through communally shared topics, whereas rapport in the Thai group is reflected through attending to each member's self. Due to the 'loosely structured social system' in Thai culture, individual-based topics are a major concern, and Thai selves are promoted as the basis for constructing rapport with others. Linguistically, Thai people prefer to use intensifiers to display involvement and intimacy, and in this way to create a friendly atmosphere.

Rapport management strategies in institutional settings. Drawing data from business settings, Campbell et al. (2003) examine how managerial leaders improve their relationships with team members through communicative strategies such as the showing of interest in the co-participants' social roles, the use of in-group markers, and seeking agreements. Active listening, being intimate, giving deference, and minimizing imposition are linguistic tactics that are used to manage the recipients' sociality rights. Paramasivam (2011) examines the ways to manage the interpersonal relations between pilots and controllers in air traffic control in Malaysia. Rapport is found to be achieved via linguistic devices of justification, terms of address, conventionally politeness expressions (e.g., please), modals, and conditional language use. Drawing on institutional conversation, Pounds (2019) discusses how rapport is managed in police officers' interviews with suspects. Rapport is operationalized in two major aspects: empathy and face. Empathy is achieved by showing an understanding of the suspect's feelings and demonstrating positive regard. As for face, it is enhanced through the strategies of suggesting the innocence of the suspect, claiming reduced

responsibility, demonstrating active listening, expressing affiliation, using humour, etc. The analysis reveals that the rapport-orientated technique is more effective when eliciting information from suspects of terrorism.

Rapport management strategies in written communications. Hernández-López (2019) reports how the hosts manage their relationship with guests on the social platform of Airbnb. They attend to the guests' equity rights by giving them freedom and independence; attend to their association rights by investing time, effort, and affect; attend to their face needs through the speech acts of complimenting, praising, thanking, and recommending. Ho (2011) examines the ways to enhance the relationship between English teachers of public education when the relationship is challenged in request emails. In terms of the discourse domain, they attend to the relationship by blending institutional, professional, and personal discourses. Specific discursive moves, such as expressing feelings, ideas, emotions, and wishes, convincing the recipient, and showing gratitude for requesting help, are employed to enhance or maintain rapport. Inductive and deductive rhetorical approaches are also exploited by team members to manage the relationships.

Overall, prior research illuminates the exploration of negative emotion management, triggered by unmet behavioural expectations, to be discussed in this study. Linguistic practices of accounting, justifying, excusing, denying, and rationalizing can be used to restore face and heal discordant rapport, while linguistic devices such as complimenting, praising, thanking, displaying empathy, and giving approval/acceptance can enhance the relationship between interlocutors.

# 2.6 a Critique of Previous Studies

The reviews of the literature on emotions have been examined from a wide range of dimensions, including (non)linguistic expressions of emotions, classification of emotions, the triggers of negative emotions, and the practices/strategies that are used for managing negative emotions. These studies shed light on the research focus (i.e., the management of negative emotions) of this study at least in three aspects: (a) how to identify interlocutors' negative emotions in interaction; (b) the idea of negative emotions as social constructs whose interpretation needs to consider the social/moral ground in which the interaction takes place, as well as the social roles of interlocutors; and (c) interpersonal interactions as the major elicitors of negative emotions, and the role of rapport/relational management in emotion management as having been seldom discussed. Despite the achievements and the implications, the investigation into emotion and emotion management should be expanded and deepened due to the following limitations.

(1) Insufficient research into the influence of culturally specific principles on emotions. The role of social and cultural norms/principles in influencing people's perceptions of rapport and one's emotional reactions needs to be examined. It has been widely accepted that one's perception of rapport is largely dependent on interlocutors' evaluation of violations or adherence to the social norms/principles, and the perceived rapport is accompanied by one's emotional reactions. For instance, Locher and Watts (2008) argue that negative evaluation is a representation of negative emotion, being articulated when 'the level of relational work does not match their expectations' (p.79). Spencer-Oatey (2011) maintains that the infringements of personal expectations/social norms are always emotion-sensitive. These studies, which are mainly examined in Western academia, have the following limitations when applied to this study.

The relationship type between interlocutors tends to be less intimate (e.g., between service providers and service users), thus the social norms/principles that apply to those interactions might not be adequate when a close relationship is concerned. As far as the social relationship between interlocutors is concerned, Spencer-Oatey's (2002) study concerns Chinese students' (mostly recent arrivals in Britain) interactions with a wide range of people (e.g., school teachers, classmates, hostesses, employers, taxi drivers) and no further differentiation is done in terms of the relationship type between interlocutors. In another study conducted by Spencer-Oatey and her research partner (Spencer-Oatey & Xing 2003), the relationship type between the participants is that of business partners (i.e., Chinese delegation members and the staff of the British company).

With regard to the relationship type of the participants in this study, it focuses on people's interactions with those with whom they are close or have a long-term relationship. Under such circumstances, the relational principle that underpins their interaction should be different from the principles that underpin the interaction of more distant relationships, which requires further exploration and explanation.

Little research has examined the role of social norms/principles in influencing people's perceptions of rapport, in particular in the Chinese sociocultural context. One exception can be found in Ran and Zhao (2018). Utilizing Chinese data on interpersonal conflict mediation, the researchers report how the unmet qingmian expectations between acquaintances trigger interpersonal conflicts and negative emotions. It is based on this pragmatic principle of qingmian that people evaluate the appropriateness of acquaintances' behaviour.

The significance of broader socio-cultural expectations and the interpersonal histories between participants in influencing people's perceptions of rapport has been commonly acknowledged by scholars

(e.g., Chang & Haugh 2011; Arundale 2010; O'Driscoll 2007). All call for an investigation of the social expectations in specific relationship types, and how these expectations influence people's perceptions of rapport and their emotional reactions. This exploration is further demanded by the differences in advocated cultural values, i.e., between individualism and collectivism. For instance, an independent self emphasizes equity and liberty whereas an interdependent self (e.g., a Chinese self) values interdependence, mutuality, and reciprocity (Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998: 8-19).

(2) Lack of empirical studies on the interplay between emotions and relationships. Prior research has documented a unilateral interplay between emotions and interpersonal relations. Many studies (e.g., Spencer-Oatey 2002, 2011; Kienpointer 2008; Isik-Güler & Ruhi 2010; Culpeper 2011; Ho 2017) have verified that people's perceptions of disharmonious relations/threatened rapport would evoke negative emotions. Namely, emotions have always been explored from an outcome-orientated approach and they are understood as responses to impulsiveness or relational disharmony. Very few studies have taken a process-orientated approach to examine how negative emotions occur due to disharmonious relations, and how they are managed through relationship management strategies. An exploration that approaches from this perspective would contribute to a more integrated understanding of, and make more easily observable, the interconnection between emotions and interpersonal relations.

(3) Inadequate pragmatic studies on emotion management. In the existing research, the linguistic studies on emotion management are mainly explored from a conversation analytic approach. Drawing on data from telephone helplines and emergency call lines (e.g., Bolden 2010; Shaw and Kitzinger 2013), CA-grounded investigation of emotion management finds that call-takers, when they found callers' outbursts of emotions would impede their collection of essential information, would actively manage their own or the callers' emotions by remaining calm, using directives (e.g., let's calm down) and reassuring the callers (e.g., the help is on the way). More CA-grounded studies examine emotion management by drawing data from psychotherapy and counselling. For instance, Weiste (2016) reports that therapists use two types of formulation sequences to manage clients' maladaptive emotional states, and Voutilainen et al. (2010) present how the practices of recognition and interpretation are used by therapists to respond to clients' negative emotions. In addition to these studies, Muntigl et al. (2017) examine the role of specific interactional structures in emotion management. This study shows how therapists guide clients to speak with conflicted aspects of themselves or with non-present significant

others to cope with unresolved feelings. During this interactional process, the management of participants' epistemic authority, as well as deontic authority, which accommodates the transition of four distinct interlocking interactional phrases, are discussed.

(4) Lack of research on norms of emotional expression in the Chinese social context. The field of emotions has come a very long way in the last 40 years, however, the norms of emotional expression in different sociocultural contexts is an area that needs to be further addressed. Except for a few works on Chinese which I have referred to above (e.g., Gong 2014), more research on the expression of emotions is examined in the Western sociocultural context. There is a need to explore how the high-context communication culture influences the emotional expression of Chinese people, including the ways negative emotions are displayed.

# 2.7 Conclusion

This chapter, building on the last, suggests that emotion management can be examined from an interpersonal pragmatics approach. A review of the literature finds that negative emotion is an interpersonal phenomenon. Interpersonal sensitive events, such as face threats, impulsiveness, and rapport threats are closely associated with people's emotional reactions. For instance, if people perceive their expectations that result from interactional principles are not met, people may perceive the damaged rapport between them and co-interlocutors, followed by emotional reactions such as anger/ sadness. An analysis of the sequential unfolding of emotions also supports this point.

In addition to interpreting emotions from an interpersonal pragmatics perspective, the fact that rapport can be dynamically managed in interaction is crucial for investigating emotions and emotion management from the interpersonal pragmatics approach. The rapport practices such as doing facework, and accounting for the unmet expectations in interpersonal communication would influence people's perceptions of rapport and bring changes to people's emotional status.

The limitation of research in emotion management is an important motivation for working towards an interpersonal pragmatics approach. The prior studies on emotion management mainly take a conversation analytic approach, focusing on the linguistic practices in which the interactants respond to the negative emotions and the sequential features. However, the triggers of negative emotions and their roles in emotion management have been greatly overlooked. This calls for discourse analysts to adopt an interpersonal pragmatics perspective to examine the triggers of negative

emotions and the practices to manage these triggers, to manage negative emotions in interaction.

Examining emotion management from an interpersonal pragmatics approach will also illuminate research on rapport, and in particular the interactional principles on which people's perceptions of rapport are based. Over the past 20 years, the equity principle and the association principle have long been discussed in the rapport literature. Despite its explanation power, these two principles are mainly rooted in Western culture. The culturally specific interactional principle that influences people's perceptions of rapport is worthy of investigation. Such an endeavour points to the universality and specifically of interactional principles in interpersonal communication across the world and across different cultures.
