# 3 Arguing for a Ganqing Approach to Rapport (management)

# 3.1 Introduction

Following on from the interpersonal pragmatics approach, Chinese people's negative emotions in interaction are understood and managed, producing a culturally specific approach to rapport (management). This chapter proposes a ganqing approach to rapport management, providing new insights into the interactional principle/relational principle/pragmatic principle in Chinese interpersonal communication. I shall start by elaborating on four notions—ganqing, renqing, qingmian, and emotion. The definitional issues and their interplay will be discussed. I will then argue for why a ganqing approach to rapport management is needed, particularly the aspects that the fundamental interactional principles of the RMF cannot fully explain. Then, I describe the interplay between ganqing, rapport, and negative emotions, attempting to explain the rationale for managing negative emotions through rapport management. Finally, I elaborate on the concept of ganqing and connect it with the related concept of im/politeness in a much more explicit manner.

# 3.2 the Backdrop for a Ganqing Approach

What are the prior approaches to rapport (management)? Why is it necessary to put forth a new approach? These are the issues to be discussed.

# 3.2.1 Face Approach to Rapport (management)

According to Spencer-Oatey (2008), people's perceptions of rapport are influenced by three key factors: face sensitivities, behavioural expectations, and interactional goals. These three factors point to different approaches to rapport (management).

The face approach is one of the key approaches discussed in the literature (Spencer-Oatey 2000, 2005). If people want to have a harmonious and smooth relationship with others, they need to consider their interlocutors

and their face conditions. Conditions such as gaining face, keeping face, threatening face, and losing face, can affect people's judgement of rapport. That is, whether the rapport between them and interlocutors is enhanced, maintained, or damaged.

The 'face' concept is conceptualized and operationalized when we examine rapport (management) from a face approach. The types of face being discussed are summarized:

(1) Brown and Levinson's notion of face, which defines face as 'the public self-image that every member wants to claim for himself' and distinguishes between positive face and negative face. According to them, a positive face is a person's desire to be appreciated and approved by others, in terms of personality, behaviours, values and so on. Negative face concerns a person's desire to be unimpeded by others, and the desire not to be imposed upon them. Based on this interpretation of face, the researchers have examined the issues of rapport (management). For instance, a student's critical comments on the teacher challenge the teacher's sense of competence as a teacher, thus making the teacher lose face concerning the attribute of competence and threatening the rapport between them (Spencer-Oatey 2007). Schröder (2014) takes up the notion of 'face' established by Brown and Levinson (1987) to discuss the interplay of (im)politeness, conflict styles, and rapport management in Brazilian-German interaction.

(2) Spencer-Oatey's notion of face involves the respectability face and the identity face. The former refers to the prestige, honour or 'good name' that a person or social group holds and claims within a (broader) community. Unlike respectability face, which is pan-situational, identity face is situation-specific, reflects people's social values, and relates to specific social attributes. Researchers have adopted this approach to rapport management. For example, Harrington (2018) investigates how the collections agents at a UK-based utility company call centre attend to the debtors' identity face and convey other rapport-relevant information to maintain and enhance rapport with them.

In addition to respectability face and identity face, Spencer-Oatey (2008) proposes quality face and social identity face when discussing rapport-related topics. Quality face involves personal qualities, abilities, and competence, while social identity face is concerned with the roles a person plays in society. People have the desire to be thought of as close friends or important clients. This is closely associated with their self-images or face. Adopting this rapport approach, Sun and Chen (2023) explore how producers of public signs enhance rapport by promoting the target

audience's social identity face. Also, this study reports how the producer attends to the target audiences' quality face by indirectly projecting them as literally 'elegant', therefore enhancing rapport with them.

As discussed above, the rapport (management) researchers take face as a central concern when examining rapport management practices in interaction. Whatever the construct of face the analysis is rooted, it is widely accepted that face wants, face-threatening acts, face-enhancing acts, and other face-relevant issues provide a window through which people's rapport with interlocutors can be perceived and managed.

# 3.2.2 Equity and Association Rights Approach to Rapport (management)

Behavioural expectations based on interactional principles are key elements that influence people's perceptions of rapport. The equity principle and the association principle are two superordinate principles. Based on the interplay of the equity principle, association principle, and people's perceptions of rapport, an equity approach and association approach to rapport (management) can be generalized.

First, the equity approach explores how people are concerned about equity issues for interlocutors and how these issues influence people's dynamic judgement of rapport. These issues involve (a) cost-benefit considerations (the extent to which we are exploited or disadvantaged, and the belief that costs and benefits should be kept roughly in balance through the principle of reciprocity); and (b) autonomy-imposition considerations (the extent to which people control us or impose on us) (Spencer-Oatey 2008: 16). The research on rapport has been examined using this approach. For instance, Ho (2017) finds that the hotel manager demonstrated his/ her awareness of the reviewers' desire for 'personal consideration from others' (Spencer-Oatey 2008: 16) by minimizing the imposition on the reviewer—giving them options not to act as suggested. When taking an equity approach, the researchers also investigate the types of expectations that are formed based on the equity principle, and how breaches of expectations lead to disharmony (discordant rapport) between interlocutors (Spencer-Oatey 2002; Spencer-Oatey & Xing 2019).

Second, the association rights approach to rapport (management) examines the types of behavioural expectations based on the association principle and the practices that people attend to these expectations for managing the rapport between interlocutors. The behavioural expectations, which are formed based on the association principle, include (a) the expectation of interactional involvement; and (b) the expectation of affective involvement (Spencer-Oatey 2008: 16). In addition to the expectations, many studies examine the linguistic practices employed when attending to the association rights. For example, Harrington (2018) reports that the debt collector manages the relevant association rights by

encouraging interlocutors' involvement, sharing appropriate concerns, and showing respect. Sampietro (2019) finds that the Peninsular Spanish interlocutors use smiling emojis to display closeness and involvement.

# 3.2.3 Interactional Goal Approach to Rapport (management)

In the RMF, the interactional goal is a third factor that influences interactional rapport. When interacting with others, people may have interpersonal goals or task-focused goals, or both. These goals or wants can significantly influence people's perception of rapport since any failure can bring people annoyance and frustration. This points to an interactional goal approach to rapport (management). For instance, Ho (2017) investigates how the hotel management manages rapport with dissatisfied customers. The managers manage the reviewer's interactional goals by acknowledging the problem, explaining the cause of the problem, apologizing, or describing the action taken to rectify the problem. In Sun and Chen (2023), the analysis reveals that a directive public sign that prohibits smoking attends to interlocutors' interactional goals and enhances rapport by directing them as to where to smoke.

# 3.2.4 Qingmian and Renqing Approach to Rapport (management)

In addition to the three approaches discussed above, rapport (management) has been approached from culturally specific perspectives within pragmatics research. The qingmian-orientated relationship/rapport management proposed by Ran and Zhao (2018) is noteworthy. From an emic perspective, the culturally important notion of qingmian is key to understanding rapport management in Chinese. Qingmian refers to the 'emotional ties between at least two acquaintances based on long-term renqing and mianzi practices' (ibid: 185). The relationship between two parties can be damaged by qingmian threat, involving inattentive behaviour, failure to reciprocate renqing, and face-threatening behaviours. To manage the disharmony, interlocutors can request modifications, do deference, solicit attentiveness, solicit reciprocity, etc.

The qingmian-orientated relationship management also discusses the interrelationship between emotion and relational work. Qingmian threat is associated with disharmony and emotional reactions (the other condemning emotions and self-conscious emotions). Emotional reactions are important indicators of the quality of the status quo of the relationship between interlocutors. Therefore, this moral order has important theoretical implications for understanding the role of emotions in relationship/ rapport management.

In some studies, the influence of renqing on rapport (management) is discussed separately. The renqing principle is proposed as a pragmatic

principle that explains the emic features of rapport (management) in Chinese culture. The renqing approach to rapport has been employed to explain interpersonal relationships in daily life contexts. Ran and Zhao (2018) examine how an experienced folk mediator resolves interpersonal conflicts between relatives or neighbors by using the renqing principle. Renqing approach is not limited to daily interaction but can also be applied to the institutional context. For instance, Zhao and Ran (2022) discuss the influence of renqing on government-citizen interactions. Drawing on data from an e-government platform, they investigate how the Chinese local government enhances rapport with citizens to construct an image of 'displaying emotional concern'. Based on the analysis, a rapport management model for government-citizen interaction based on the renqing principle, reason, and law is proposed. In another study, Ran (2018) explores how Chinese renqing functions in managing rapport and its reciprocity.

# 3.2.5 Critical Overview of Prior Approaches to Rapport (management)

(1) Most of the existing research examines rapport (management) by applying Spencer-Oatey's RMF. However, as Spencer-Oatey has maintained earlier when examining rapport management issues, it is crucial to analyze the similarities and differences that occur across cultures, contexts, and individuals (Spencer-Oatey 2005: 117).

(2) From a culturally specific or emic perspective, the qingmian approach to rapport and the renqing approach to rapport have been proposed. Qingmian and renqing mainly exist among family relatives, friends, or neighbors. Accordingly, these two approaches mainly apply to the interpersonal interaction between the members of these particular social groups, neglecting the interaction in more intimate and closer relationships. For example, between the parent and the child, husband-wife in the family context. The interactional principle or pragmatic principle that underpins their interaction needs to be addressed. Also, we need more empirical evidence of rapport (management) in intimate and close relationships to see how the specific relational principle unfolds in the interaction.

# 3.3 on a Ganqing Approach to Rapport (management)

# 3.3.1 Definitional Issues

In this section, key notions and their interconnections will be delineated.

Ganqing vs. Emotion Emotion and ganqing (感情) are two distinct notions, varying in terms of conceptualization and content. It can be 'misleading to equate the western concept of "emotions" with qinggan or ganqing, as many Chinese beginners in English do' (Sun 1991: 10). Among

the many definitions made of ganqing (Yang 1994; Kipnis 1997; Chen & Chen 2004; Wang 2007, to name a few), this study adopts the viewpoint that ganqing is a relational principle or social norm that Chinese people should follow when they interact with specific guanxi members (Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998; Ledeneva 2008; Zhu 2015; Chang 2018). As for emotions, they are 'socially and culturally shaped and malleable products' that emerge dynamically during the interaction (Langlotz & Locher 2013: 91).

Ganqing vs. Renqing The concepts of ganqing and renqing (人情) are used somewhat differently in Chinese interpersonal communication, though both serve as the relational principle or moral order that underpins Chinese people's interaction. (1) Ganqing is said to be the affective component of guanxi and focuses on emotional attachment and affection, while renqing represents the conative component of guanxi, emphasizing the reciprocal exchange of favours with empathy (Barnes et al. 2011; Yen et al. 2011). (2) Ganqing is found in long-term guanxi members (Yang 1994), such as parents and children, close friends (Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998), co-workers (Jacobs 1979), and long-term business partners (e.g., Chen & Chen 2004; Wang 2007; Yen et al. 2017). With regards to renqing, it is more prevalent in relationship dyads such as those involving acquaintances and relatives (Zhao 2017; Ran 2018; Ran & Zhao 2018; Zhao & Ran 2019, 2022).

Ganqing vs. Qingmian Qingmian (情面) refers to 'the emotional ties between at least two acquaintances based on long-term renqing and mianzi practices' (Ran & Zhao 2018), unlike ganqing which emphasizes mutual affection and relational obligations. As far as the types of interpersonal relationships are concerned, ganqing mainly applies to the social interaction between intimate and long-term relationships, while qingmian is a moral expectancy underpinning the communication between family relatives, friends, or neighbours.

# 3.3.2 Ganqing as a Relational Principle

Definition of ganqing Ganqing (感情), an emic notion of emotivity in Chinese (Chang 2013: 43), has been conceptualized in various ways. It refers to the emotional commitment and social bonds in long-standing and intimate relationships (e.g., Yang 1994; Chen & Chen 2004), also a relational principle that Chinese people should follow in interpersonal communication (Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998; Zhu 2015; Chang 2018), and a resource for social exchange (e.g., Yen et al. 2017). Ganqing is also an indicator of the closeness of guanxi or is equal to guanxi under some circumstances (Kipnis 1997; Wang 2007: 82).

The key points regarding the notion of ganqing can be summarized as follows: (a) the intimate social bonds/emotional attachment/affect that exist between direct family members, close friends, cooperative partners,

or other in-group members; (b) an important component/affective side/ emotional side of guanxi or a synonym of guanxi;(c) a moral order/social norm; and (d) a resource for social exchange or business purposes.

Major components of ganqing Ganqing has been widely accepted as a relational principle or social norm that underpins Chinese people's interpersonal communications (e.g., Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998; Ledeneva 2008; Zhu 2015; Chang 2018). When it is regarded as a relational principle, its major components involve:

(a) Feelings/emotional feelings/affectionate feelings. Ganqing means 'caring about someone's feelings' (Zhao & Pan 2019), 'emotional feelings' (Ledeneva 2008), and 'mutual good feelings' (Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998; Santangelo 2005). Similarly, some scholars argue that ganqing stresses 'empathic understanding' (Yang 1994), 'emotional understanding' (Chen & Chen 2004), or mutual empathic understanding. To sum up, treating specific guanxi members with feelings or mutual good feelings is a key element in ganqing.

(b) Attachment/emotional attachment. Ganqing means a close relationship between specific guanxi members. Other notions such as 'emotional attachment' (e.g., Yang 1994; Wang 2007; Chang 2013, 2018; Yen et al. 2017), 'closeness' (Gao and Ting-Toomey 1998), 'connections' (Chen & Chen 2004), 'relational connections with others' (Hsiang 1974; Chang & Holt 1994), or 'inner attachment' (Guo 2001) are also used.

(c) Reciprocal obligations. According to the relational principle of gan- qing, one is obligated to reciprocate the other's favours, to 'live up to one's social obligations', 'help one another' (Yang 1994: 122), and to fulfil mutual obligations within a reciprocal relation (Chang & Holt 1994).

# 3.3.3 a Ganqing Approach to Rapport (management): a Theoretical Proposal

A ganqing approach attends to the relationship aspect of communication using the relational principle of ganqing. According to the ganqing principle, interlocutors have a fundamental belief that they are entitled to be treated with emotional/affectionate feelings by people from the intimate or close relationship, to have a close relationship with them, and have an obligation to reciprocate the favours they have received. The ganqing principle results in interlocutors' perceptions of role relationships, including rights and obligations associated with them.

When interlocutors have specific interactional wants and role obligations, they make dynamic judgements as to whether these wants are being achieved, the obligations are being fulfilled, and whether they are

being impeded. Accordingly, if they are paying attention to the quality of interpersonal relationships with the intimates, they make dynamic judgements as to whether they have been treated with affectionate feelings. Simultaneously, they have expectancy reactions to the verbal and non-verbal behaviour they experience and perceive it as positively eventful, negatively eventful, or simply ignore it. These judgements can often result in significant emotional reactions.

When these judgements are made, and as these emotional reactions occur, people will have to decide how they manage them verbally and nonverbally. The management process will be the central focus of this study.

# 3.3.4 Understanding Negative Emotions: an Application of a Ganqing Approach

In this section, the relationship between the ganqing principle, perceived sociality rights and obligations, rapport, and emotion will be discussed.

Drawing on the studies that concern how ganqing serves as a relational principle underpinning people's interaction with those close to them, and following Spencer-Oatey's research on the interplay between norms/principles, perceived sociality rights and obligations, perceived rapport, and people's emotional reactions, Figure 3.1 is drawn. Ganqing serves as a socio-pragmatic interactional principle that underpins Chinese people's interpersonal communication, just as the equity principle and association principle are two fundamental principles that underpin interaction (e.g., Spencer-Oatey 2000, 2008). According to the ganqing principle, a Chinese self is expected to be treated with good feelings by specific guanxi members and to have a close relationship with them. These two aspects are the perceived sociality rights (Spencer-Oatey 2008: 15-16) concerning the relational principle of ganqing. Furthermore, ganqing involves one's fulfilment of reciprocal obligations when interacting with these guanxi members.

Subsequently, if (1) a person perceives that his/her ganqing expectation is not met or (2) he/she perceives himself/herself as not fulfilling reciprocal obligations, this would negatively influence his/her perceptions of rapport and result in evaluations of ganqing violation-induced discordant rapport (hereafter GQVI discordant rapport), the latter of which can often result in one's negative emotional reactions (cf. Figure 3.1).

# 3.4 the Relationship of Ganqing to Im/politeness

Emotivity and im/politeness: Wenqing (温情, warmth), qingmian (情面, mutual affection-based face), renqing(人情, emotion), and empathy are key notions of emotivity. These notions have been discussed in several works on im/politeness. Kádár and Pan (2011) assert that showing wenqing is a demonstration of politeness in Chinese communication, while a lack of genuineness or 'insincerity' often presupposes the lack of politeness. Qingmian, which refers to the emotional ties between at least two acquaintances, is also closely associated with politeness. The violations of qingmian are related to impoliteness evaluations in the Chinese sociocultural context (Zhao & Ran 2019).

In addition to the three qing-related notions, empathy is also related to politeness. According to Larina and Ponton (2022: 201), 'politeness is not only social but is also a psychological phenomenon based on empathy, whose manifestations may vary across cultures'. In Japanese culture, empathy is considered an attitudinal stance encompassing concern for the feelings of others (Fukushima & Haugh 2014: 168).

Though the above notions concern the interrelationship between emotivity and im/politeness in one way or another, the type of interpersonal interaction that these notions apply is limited. For example, wenqing is believed to be very important in business interaction, given that emotive discourse, such as inserting lengthy 'warming-up' sections in professional lectures, is considered to promote long-term business relationships (Zhu 2015). Qingmian mainly lies in acquaintanceship relationships in the context of Chinese interpersonal communication, such as between family relatives, friends, or neighbours. As for renqing, it mainly exists in the interaction between 'relatives, neighbours, classmates, colleagues, teachers and students, people sharing a natal area, and so forth' (Hwang 1987: 952). Empathy is said to be important, especially in Japanese culture (Clancy 1986; Uchida 2011). Wierzbicka (1997: 275) regards it as one of the most important ideals in Japanese society. In Chinese society, title (体贴, attending to others' feelings) and thoughtfulness are roughly considered the equivalents of empathy (Chang 2018; Fukushima & Haugh 2014).

On ganqing and im/politeness Ganqing describes the emotivity that exists between intimate and long-term relationships. The pragmatic

principle/relational principle of ganqing is characterized by mutual good feeling/emotional understanding and emotional attachment. Also, ganqing emphasizes one's obligation to reciprocate the others' favours. When one communicates with a guanxi member (i.e., with whom one has an intimate or long-term relationship), it is heavily based on ganqing expectations that the Chinese self's behaviour or the guanxi member's behaviour is evaluated as 'polite', 'non-polite', or 'rude'. That is to say, ganqing concerns the conceptualizations of linguistic politeness and non-linguistic politeness in intimate and close relationships. Moreover, it describes the modern Chinese politeness phenomena, particularly the unfolding of politeness in interaction.

# 3.5 Conclusion

This chapter summarizes the existing approaches to rapport (management) and proposes a new approach. Ever since Spencer-Oatey's proposal for the notion of rapport and studying interpersonal interaction, how different interactional principles function in managing rapport and interpersonal relationships has been discussed. These point to different approaches to rapport (management). This chapter first explicitly claims there are six approaches to rapport (management): the face approach, equity approach, association rights approach, interactional goal approach, qingmian approach, and the renqing approach. The former four approaches are built on Spencer-Oatey's rapport management framework, which focuses on how face sensitivities, behavioural expectations based on the equity principle and association principle, and interactional goals, function in managing rapport and interpersonal relationships. The vast majority of studies have drawn on the four approaches to analyze interpersonal interaction, including both institutional and non-institutional interactions. However, further analysis reveals that these four approaches are more often used to study interpersonal relationships between non-intimate or more distant social members, leaving the rapport between the intimates under-examined.

Two Chinese scholars have discussed how the renqing principle and qingmian principle function in managing interpersonal relationships drawing on interactions in the Chinese sociocultural context. In their research, it is the relationship between neighbours, acquaintances, and family relatives that are discussed. What are the essential factors for constructing and managing interpersonal relationships between intimate social members such as parents and children, and husbands and wives? These are the questions to be explored.

In addition to discussing the necessity of investigating the rapport between the intimate ones and working towards a new approach to rapport (management), the theoretical foundation for proposing the new

approach is also introduced. Ganqing has long been studied in sociology, psychology, and communication studies in the Chinese literature. Ganqing is found to be an essential factor as well as a relational principle that influences Chinese people's interpersonal communication, in particular the communication between parents and children, husbands and wives, and close friends. Despite the importance of ganqing in Chinese interpersonal interaction, its linguistic manifestations, and how ganqing can be invoked in interaction, have been under-researched.

A ganqing approach to rapport (management) is therefore proposed, dedicated to accounting for the rapport and interpersonal relationships between intimate social members in Chinese society. Moreover, the newly proposed ganqing approach is drawn up in order to understand interlocutors' negative emotions in interpersonal psychotherapy sessions, given the interrelationship between interactional principle, rapport, and people's emotional status. By examining ganqing and proposing a ganqing approach to rapport(management), this study highlights the importance of ganqing in im/politeness research. It expands people's emic understanding of im/politeness and interpersonal interaction.

# 4 Description of Conceptual Framework

# 4.1 Introduction

This chapter establishes a theoretical framework to account for therapists' management of clients' negative emotions during Chinese psychotherapy sessions. It starts with an introduction of Spencer-Oatey's (2000) Rapport Management Theory (RMF), the aspects that the RMF can apply to the current data, and the limitations of RMF when applying to the dyadic therapist-client interaction. A surrogate-based RMF is then proposed. This chapter ends by introducing the rapport-orientated negative emotion management model.

# 4.2 Spencer-oatey's Rmf

The RMF provides a useful framework for understanding clients' negative emotions. In what follows, the aspects where rapport management succeeds in explaining and fails to explain are discussed. Such an endeavour aims to account for the necessity of incorporating the surrogate role into the process of rapport management and introducing the surrogate-based RMF.

# 4.2.1 Where Rmf Succeeds

Following the RMF, negative emotions provide a window for discerning what types of discordant rapport they appear as responses to, as well as the links between emotion categories and face, or perceived sociality rights and obligations.

The relationship between interlocutors needs to be considered if the rapport and interlocutors' emotional reactions are to be properly interpreted. According to Grossen and Orvig (2011: 54), psychotherapy sessions sometimes consist not only of actual participants but also of absent/ virtual participants who play a role in the dynamics of the conversation. In this study, the actual participants are therapists and clients, and the

absent participants are the persons who have close or long-term relationships with clients.

The rapport between therapists and clients is not the major concern (cf. Figure 4.1). The interpersonal relationships clients have with NPTPs, the relationship of which is disclosed in clients' self-reports is what the study aims to focus on. Given that it concerns the relationship between interlocutors, some aspects of the RMF still apply. For instance, clients' negative emotions can be understood as responses to the discordant rapport that exists between them and NPTPs, and the perceptions of discordant rapport can be a result of unmet behavioural expectations (or one's sociality rights have been infringed).

# 4.2.2 Where Rmf Fails

(1) Inadequate description of interlocutors' management of the relationship between co-interlocutors and NPTPs. Reviewing the literature, rapport management is often characterized as rapport management between direct interlocutors (cf. Table 4.1 for an overview), whether it discusses rapport management in face-to-face(F-t-F) communication or in computer-medicated communication (CMC).

The rapport management in these studies has been mainly concerned with how an interlocutor manages the rapport he/she has with other interlocutors directly. Given the unique participation structure of dyadic therapist-client interaction (cf. Figure 4.1), the rapport management involved in the process of negative emotion management is not direct rapport management

Table 4.1 Previous research on rapport management

<table><tr><td>Authors</td><td>Type of relationship involved</td><td>Context</td></tr><tr><td>García (2009)</td><td>Peruvian Spanish speakers</td><td rowspan="3">Colloquial contexts(F-t-F)</td></tr><tr><td>Aoki (2010)</td><td>Between Thai and Japanese speakers</td></tr><tr><td>Placencia et al. (2015);Lai (2014); Omotunde and Ogunrotimi (2022)</td><td>L2 learners of English University students Between family members</td></tr><tr><td>Spencer-Oatey and Xing (2003)</td><td>British-Chinese business people</td><td rowspan="5">Service encounters(F-t-F)</td></tr><tr><td>Clark et al. (2003)</td><td>Salesperson–customer</td></tr><tr><td>Placencia (2005)</td><td>Shopkeepers–customers</td></tr><tr><td>Lauriks et al. (2015)</td><td>Employer–employee; businessman–customer</td></tr><tr><td>Yuan and Zhang (2019)</td><td>Expert–consultant in Business phone-in</td></tr><tr><td>Campbell (2005)</td><td>Physician–patient</td><td rowspan="2">Healthcare contexts(F-t-F)</td></tr><tr><td>Hernández-López (2011)</td><td>General practitioners–patients</td></tr><tr><td>Paramasivam (2011)</td><td>Pilot-controller;</td><td rowspan="6">Air traffic interaction/Educational/academic context</td></tr><tr><td>Adel (2011)</td><td>University students in online group work;</td></tr><tr><td>Ho (2011)</td><td>Colleagues in an educational context;</td></tr><tr><td>Chen and Li (2015)</td><td>Peers in a classroom setting;</td></tr><tr><td>Jiang (2020)</td><td>Teacher–students;</td></tr><tr><td>Jiang and Yue (2023)</td><td>Between academic blog commenters</td></tr><tr><td>Ho (2017, 2020); Feng and Ren (2019); Hernández-López (2019, 2020); Cenni and Goethals (2020); Izquierdo and Blanco (2023); Creelman (2022)</td><td>Hotel management–dissatisfied customers; Chinese management-online consumers; Host-guest in Airbnb (a social platform)</td><td>Service contexts(CMC)</td></tr><tr><td>Wang (2015)</td><td>Advertisers–the consumer</td><td rowspan="4">The public context and others</td></tr><tr><td>Chen and Xue (2017)</td><td>Between Web users of Baidu Tieba</td></tr><tr><td>Sun (2018)</td><td>Public sign administrators–the public</td></tr><tr><td>Chen and Chen (2020)</td><td>Online rumour makers–the recipients of rumours</td></tr></table>

between one interlocutor and a co-interlocutor, it concentrates on how an interlocutor (A) manages the rapport a co-interlocutor (B) has with a NPTP. How do we understand A's participation role in the dynamics of rapport management? How does A manage the rapport between B and the NPTP (i.e., what types of rapport management strategies are employed)? These questions have not been fully addressed in the literature on rapport management.

(2) Fundamental interactional principles in RMF may not fully explain the cultural-specific phenomenon. According to the RMF, rapport is influenced by the perceived sociality rights and obligations, the latter of which is largely based on two fundamental socio-pragmatic interactional principles, i.e., equity and association. The equity principle stresses the components of cost-benefit and autonomy-imposition, while the association principle values the components of interactional involvement-detachment and affective involvement-detachment. This study not only concerns an equal relationship (i.e., clients' interaction with their couple, and with the close friends) but also a hierarchical one (i.e., the clients' interaction with their parents). The interpersonal relationship and communication, being influenced by Confucianism, emphasizes the relational obligations to whom we are close (i.e., filial obligations and reciprocating the favours), which cannot be fully and adequately reflected in the equity principle and the association principle. Therefore, empirical investigations are needed to examine more concrete 'cultural values' that underpin the interaction in the specific types of interpersonal relationships and to explore how they influence Chinese people's perceptions of rapport (Spencer-Oatey 2007: 90).

(3) The inadequacies of rapport orientations. Since clients' negative emotions are caused by discordant rapport with whom they are close or have long-term relationships, rapport-oriented management involves the management of disharmonious relationships and the rapport orientations therapists hold during this process. Before discussing whether the rapport orientations mentioned in the RMF apply to the current data, their contents are first introduced. The four rapport orientations (Spencer-Oatey 2000: 29-30; Spencer-Oatey & Franklin 2009: 117-118) proposed in the RMF involve:

(a) Rapport enhancement orientation: a desire to strengthen or enhance harmonious relations between the interlocutors;

(b) Rapport maintenance orientation: a desire to maintain or protect harmonious relations between the interlocutors;

(c) Rapport neglect orientation: a lack of concern or interest in the quality of relations between the interlocutors;

(d) Rapport challenge orientation: a desire to challenge or impair harmonious relations between the interlocutors (emphasis added).

The rapport orientation cannot be directly applied to this study given there is no harmonious relationship to start with. Lauriks et al.'s (2015) proposal of 'discordant rapport' sheds light on the resolution of this problem. Drawing on data from small business interactions, Lauriks et al. claim that not only the harmonious relationships be maintained, challenged, or enhanced, but the 'discordant rapport' (i.e., the disharmonious relations) can also be managed. They propose that people may deliberately maintain or promote discordant social relations when it is beneficial for them to do so. Their study sheds light on discordant rapport orientations, as well as the rapport management strategies that people employ if they hold different discordant rapport orientations. The under-researched areas concerning discordant rapport and discordant rapport orientations merit further scholarly attention.

# 4.3 the Framework: Rapport-orientated Management of Negative Emotions

# 4.3.1 Two Preliminaries

(1) Surrogacy in interaction

The notion of surrogacy is traced back to Mazer et al. (2014) research on doctor-patient-companion interaction. When a patient cannot make medical decisions (e.g., coma, dementia), a companion's 'speaking as' the patient is referred to as 'surrogacy'. The notion of 'pseudo surrogacy' describes another interactional phenomenon: the companion speaks for the patient as if the patient were not in the room or unable to speak for him/herself.

The interactional phenomenon that one speaks as the other or describes the other's beliefs has been widely discussed. Beisecker (1989) finds that in the process of consultation, a patient's companion often answers questions from the doctor on behalf of the patient. For example, if an elderly male patient has hearing difficulty or other language disorders, his family members may communicate with the doctor on behalf of him. Coupland and Coupland (2001) examine how the son of an elderly woman expresses his mother's experiences and worries, and obtains the relevant information from the doctor. Drawing data from a Korean livestream of cat mukbang, Choe (2020) analyzes how the chat message participants speak as/for the cat and animate its voice. The participant's animation of the cat's voice is

accompanied prosodically—the tone of voice, amplitude, lexical emphasis, rhythm, and intonational contours.

The interactional phenomenon of surrogacy corresponds with Goffman's (1981) production format theory. According to Goffman (1981), the discourse roles an interlocutor undertakes involve (a) an animator who is responsible for producing the sound; (b) an author who constructs words uttered or written by the animator; (c) a principal whose belief is represented and who is committed to what the words say, and (d) a figure who is being referred to by others. Then, why does an interlocutor animate co-interlocutors' voices? To help the co-interlocutor deal with the communicative difficulties is one motivation (e.g., the patient who has aphasia may not have the ability to express himself/herself). Tannen (2004) discusses how and why people animate the voice of another in everyday family interaction. The family members speak as and to their family dogs to achieve different communicative goals: resolving a conflict, lightening the atmosphere, or teaching a misbehaving child.

This study concerns another communicative context in which the animation occurs, but also to resolve the conflict. The types of conflict involved, and the interactional background for the conflict will be introduced.

# (2) Two-layered Interaction

Several authors have discussed the layers of communication or the levels of communication (or communicative levels). For instance, in the fictional discourse of films and plays (Short 1981, 1994; Bubel 2008; Dynel 2011), the first level of communication is realized by an 'unknowing' character oblivious to the viewers. The second layer of communication refers to the interactions that are followed by the viewers whose inferential processes are guided by the production crew. Dynel (2014) examines the participation framework and the communicative levels in the (non)fictional multi-party media talk. Three levels of communication are identified: the first level of communication on YouTube is realized by the speaker and hearers in the videos, the second level of communication concerns YouTube viewers and their interpretation of YouTube video discourse, while the third level of communication is realized by the comments between YouTube speakers and hearers.

The interpersonal psychotherapy session involves two layers of interaction. The first layer goes to the social interaction between the interpersonal psychotherapist and clients, and the second concerns the interaction between clients and NPTPs. The second layer of social interaction can be embedded in the first layer of interaction (Dynel 2014).

The above discussions are the preliminaries of surrogate-based rapport management. Chapters 6 and 7 will lay out how interpersonal psychotherapists animate in different ways and speak for/as different people (e.g.,

clients, NTPPs), to manage interpersonal conflicts that exist between clients and NTPPs.

# 4.3.2 Surrogate-based Rmf

To remedy the first limitation of the RMF when applying to the psychotherapeutic interaction, surrogate-based RMF is proposed (cf. Figure 4.2). The two sub-models of rapport management in the surrogate-based RMF are: (1) In the first sub-model (cf. the right, upper part of Figure 4.2), therapists act as the surrogates of clients, and they two form coalitions (Coe & Prendergast 1985) or confederations (Coupland & Coupland 2001). As a result, the interaction, which involves two participants present and an absent third party, evolves into the interaction between clients and the NPTP. (2) With regards to the second sub-model (cf. the right, lower part of Figure 4.2), therapists serve as the surrogates of NPTPs, and the two parties two form coalitions. Accordingly, the psychotherapeutic interaction turns into the interaction between clients and NPTPs.

In sum, the disharmony between clients and NPTPs can be managed by bringing the surrogate role of the therapists. By switching from one discourse role (e.g., surrogating clients) to another (e.g., surrogating NPTPs) and different types of rapport management strategies, therapists manage the relational disharmony between clients and NPTPs.

When applying the surrogated-based RMF to this study, two aspects worth noting. (1) The bases of rapport should be specified under the principles of ganqing. To start with, this study particularly focuses on how the discordant rapport between clients and NPTPs is threatened by the 'rights-threatening behaviours' and the 'obligation-omission behaviours' (Spencer-Oatey 2000: 17). Which means, the other bases (face sensitivities, and interactional goals) that influence people's perceptions of rapport) are not taken into consideration in the analysis of the rapport threats.

Concerning the close relationship between clients and NPTPs in Chinese interpersonal relationships and communication, the relational principle of ganqing is foregrounded as the socio-pragmatic interactional principle that underpins their interaction. According to the ganqing principle, the sociality rights involve two components, i.e., feelings and emotional attachment; while the sociality obligations are specified as reciprocal obligations. Accordingly, rapport threats such as lack of emotional feelings, emotional detachment, and non-fulfilling of reciprocal obligations would negatively influence people's perceptions of rapport and result in their negative emotions.

The second aspect worth mentioning is that discordant rapport orientations that involved in this study. Given rapport orientation in the RMF concerns the harmonious relations between interlocutors and there are also the disharmonious relations to deal with under some circumstances, together with the claim that 'one can promote, maintain, or threaten both harmonious or discordant relationships' (Lauriks et al. 2015), this study proposes that the speakers can hold any of the following four types of discordant rapport orientation when it is in their best interest to do so:

(a) Discordant rapport enhancement orientation: a desire to enhance the disharmonious relations between the interlocutors;

(b) Discordant rapport maintenance orientation: a desire to maintain the current quality of the relationship and level of disharmony between the interlocutors;

(c) Discordant rapport neglect orientation: a lack of concern or interest in the disharmonious relations between the interlocutors (perhaps because of a focus on self's face, sociality rights);

(d) Discordant rapport challenge orientation: a desire to challenge or impair the disharmonious relations between the interlocutors (so 'the relationship becomes more harmonious', Lauriks et al. 2015: 26).

The discordant rapport orientations mentioned above can be integrated into the rapport orientations proposed by Spencer-Oatey (2000: 31-33), making it a comprehensive framework for the analysis of people's orientations when they are managing relations by employing different types of rapport management strategies.

# 4.3.3 a Model of Rapport-orientated Management of Negative Emotions

# (1) Theoretical Foundations

Before presenting the rapport-oriented negative emotion management model, the interrelationship between perceptions of rapport and negative emotions is highlighted. According to Spencer-Oatey (2000), during an interaction, people make dynamic judgments as to whether the rapport between themselves and another interlocutor has been enhanced, challenged, or maintained. These judgments are based on the following three bases: perceived sociality rights and obligations, face sensitivities, and interactional goals. According to the RMF, the NPTPs' rights-threatening behaviours and clients' obligation-omission behaviour, pose a threat to rapport. Accordingly, threats to rapport tend to lead to the creation of negative emotional reactions. Having identified the reasons for clients' negative emotions, therapists then need to intervene to effectively manage clients' perception of rapport (e.g., to justify NPTPs' rights-threatening acts). In so doing, therapists attempt to modify clients' perceptions of the rapport they share with NPTPs. Based on the above discussion, Figure 4.3 presents the dynamics of relational (dis)harmony and the resulting emotional reactions of interlocutors.

As shown in Figure 4.3, if one of the two parties does not obey the basis of rapport, the result is an evaluation of disharmony, accompanied by negative emotions. However, if both parties obey the basis of rapport, the result is an evaluation of harmonious relations, which is associated with the normal status of emotions. Moreover, disharmony and the interlocutor's negative emotional reactions motivate rapport management efforts,

which can result in positive changes to the emotional status of the interlocutor. After the discussion on the interrelationship between harmony and disharmony, a rapport-orientated negative emotion management model is proposed in the penultimate part of this chapter.

(2) Dynamics of disharmony-harmony and interlocutor's emotional reactions

The motivation for rapport management: Threats to rapport, as perceived by clients, is characterized as follows. First, they are reflected in the meta-pragmatic comments made by clients about any prior social interactions they have with NPTPs. Second, this study focuses on how the perceived rapport between clients and NPTPs is threatened by 'rights-threatening behaviour' and 'obligation-omission behaviour' as defined by the relational principle of ganqing. Third, perceived rapport threats are accompanied by clients' negative emotions, which include both 'other-condemning emotions', 'self-conscious emotions', and the emotions triggered by these emotions (i.e., sadness). When therapists are confronted with clients' negative emotions, surrogate-based rapport management strategies are adopted by therapists to manage rapport threats (cf. Figure 4.4).

Means of management—surrogate-based rapport management: When the discordant rapport between clients and NPTPs is discerned and it is how clients' negative emotions are accounted for, therapists should take some actions to manage the discordant rapport. The strategies used by therapists are largely dependent on the discordant rapport orientations they take during this process. For instance, if therapists aim to repair the relational disharmony between clients and NPTPs, they can serve as NPTPs' surrogates and speak for them. Concerning the specific linguistic strategies that are used to manage rapport, excuses, and justifications are frequently employed.

Figure 4.4 Surrogate-based rapport management in negative emotion management.

Figure 4.5 A model of rapport-orientated management of negative emotions.

Contextual factors influencing rapport-orientated management: The social distance between clients and NPTPs, whether it is featured as a higher degree of intimacy or a lower degree of intimacy, is a key factor that influences therapists' choice of different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies when managing negative emotions. Based on the three aspects discussed above, a model of rapport-orientated management of negative emotions is proposed (Figure 4.5).

# 4.4 Conclusion

This chapter establishes an analytical framework to analyze the management of negative emotions from the interpersonal pragmatics approach. The interrelationship between people's perception of rapport and their emotional reactions is crucial for the construct of the conceptual framework. According to Spencer-Oatey's (2000) Rapport Management Theory, a perception of a harmonious relationship is associated with positive emotional reactions, while a perception of disharmony is followed by negative emotions. Another important aspect of working towards an interpersonal pragmatics approach to negative emotion management lies in the fact that people's perception of rapport is discursively constructed in interaction. If rapport management practices are adopted, the damaged rapport between the two parties can be managed. Accordingly, negative emotions accompanying the damaged rapport can be managed.

A close look at the causes of negative emotions and their management in Chinese interpersonal psychotherapeutic interaction will find that we cannot solely rely on Spencer-Oatey's RMF to work out the analytical framework. One problem lies in the fact that Spencer-Oatey's theory is more applicable to the social interaction between more distant social members. For instance, the interaction between the host and the guest tends to emphasize equity rights and association rights, as well as the interactional goals. However, these aspects are not necessarily important in the interpersonal communication between Chinese parents and their children. Therefore, when analyzing the interpersonal relationship between parents and children/husbands and wives/close friends in the Chinese sociocultural context, the interactional principle/relational principle of ganqing that guides the interaction between the Chinese intimates should be drawn on.

Another aspect well worth highlighting is that Spencer-Oatey's framework mainly explains the interaction between direct interlocutors. In interpersonal psychotherapy sessions, therapists manage clients' negative emotions. However, it is not the disharmonious relationship between therapists and clients that has caused clients' negative emotions. These negative emotions emerge when clients disclose the damaged rapport between them and people from intimate relationships. To manage these negative emotions, therapists either surrogate clients or clients' parents/couples/ close friends, taking rapport practices on their behalf to manage the disharmony. This interactional phenomenon involves the surrogate role in rapport management.

By incorporating the ganqing principle and bringing into the surrogate role to Spencer-Oatey's RMF, a surrogate-based RMF is proposed to analyze emotion management in interpersonal psychotherapy sessions. This framework guides the analyses in Chapter 6 and Chapter 7, which concerns the triggers of negative emotions, and the rapport-orientated practices for managing the negative emotions.

# 5 Data and Methods

# 5.1 Introduction

The key issues I will address in this chapter are data and methodological issues that would make a detailed analysis of emotion management possible. Since it is impossible to make sense of the linguistic material without taking a step back to get a broader view of who our subjects are and why emotion management is relevant, this chapter attempts to do so by placing the discussion of negative emotion management and the interlinks between emotion management and rapport management into the context of interpersonal psychotherapy. In Section 5.3, I will describe the selection, processing, and analysis of the data. After describing the coding schemes in Section 5.4, I move on to the interactional approach on which the analysis is drawn. After that, dimensions for observing the effect of emotion management are described.

# 5.2 the Communicative Context: Interpersonal Psychotherapy Sessions

The psychotherapeutic approach the data represents is interpersonal psychotherapy (IPT), an evidence-based therapy grounded in attachment theory and interpersonal theory (e.g., Klerman et al. 1984; Weissman et al. 2018). IPT is recommended for the treatment of depression in the American Psychiatric Association Guidelines for Primary Care Physicians (Karasu et al. 1993). According to IPT, the causes of the symptoms of depression might vary. Whatever the causes, however, they are usually associated with something going on in clients' personal lives, usually with those whom clients feel close to and who are important to clients. IPT 'focuses specifically on interpersonal relationships as a means of bringing about interpersonal change and symptomatic recovery, intending to help clients to improve their interpersonal relationships' (Stuart & Robertson 2015: 3). The process of change in IPT is presumed to be interactive: the process of (depression) symptom resolution is facilitated by the process

in interpersonal resolution. This means that if the interpersonal disputes between clients and their partners start to resolve, clients will feel less depressed and if clients feel less depressed, they will be able to resolve the dispute more efficiently (Law 2011). IPT involves three phases (ibid: 25-28; Stuart & Robertson 2015: 179-223).

(1) The initial phase. This stage concerns therapists' close examination of clients' current depressive symptoms and relevant history. Clients' close relationships, patterns, and mutual expectations are reviewed in this phase. This review provides a framework for understanding the interpersonal context of clients' depressive symptoms and defining the focus of the treatment. Therapists can link clients' depressive symptoms to clients' interpersonal situations within the framework of one of the four interpersonal problem areas: (a) grief (due to the death of a loved one); (b) interpersonal role disputes (i.e., conflicts with a significant other); (c) role-transitions (e.g., to a new job); and (d) interpersonal deficits (e.g., problems in sustaining or initiating relationships). In this study, both types (b) and (d) are involved.

(2) The middle phase. This is a stage during which therapists work towards improved communication and recognition, and expression of associated affect. Taking the area of interpersonal role disputes as an example, if clients' conflicts with their significant others are involved, therapists tend to help clients explore the relationship and the nature of the dispute and options to resolve it.

(3) Ending phase. It involves the review and evaluation of treatment, and support for continued use of interpersonal strategies, particularly when they are faced with potential symptomatic relapses.

The extracts presented in Chapter 6 mainly concern the interactions that occur in the initial phase of interpersonal psychotherapy, and the extracts laid out in Chapter 7 mainly concern the interactions that occur in the middle phase of interpersonal psychotherapy. The initial phase and the middle phase sometimes are recurring processes between therapists and clients (Bercelli et al. 2013).

# 5.3 Data Issues

Naturally occurring data, which is strong in validity, provides the researchers with the opportunity to explore interaction in 'natural contexts where participants present their intentions with real-world consequences' (Felix-Brasdefer 2007: 181). This study is based on this type of authentic data, and contributes to language use in institutional settings, with language usage in health settings. In what follows,

information about data collection, data selection, and data transcription is presented.

(1) Data collection. Two types of data were collected and two data sets were involved. The first data set consisted of audio recordings of naturally occurring psychotherapeutic interactions between therapists and clients from the outpatients of a Chinese hospital. Altogether 86 psychotherapy sessions were audio-recorded, with each session ranging in length from around fifty to sixty minutes. The amount of audio data recorded overall was approximately 80 hours. The recordings were gathered in the hospital between April 2017 and May 2020. The analyst was not present in any of the psychotherapy sessions; the data collection was done by therapists who audio-recorded the psychotherapeutic discourse they had with clients.

The second data set includes several post-recording interviews that were conducted with therapists by the analyst. This type of data was collected to elicit the participants' understanding of the causes of clients' negative emotions, as well as the effect of an intervention on negative emotions. Such efforts were intended to investigate interlocutors' interpretations of the actions and meanings during the psychotherapeutic interaction and thus provide additional evidence for the analysis of the social-interactional elicitors of clients' negative emotions by the analyst. These interviews were conducted when the first type of data was processed. During the interviews, therapists were asked to make comments when the analyst played back a target episode (clients' negative emotions displayed when they disclose the prior social interactions between them and the guanxi members) intending to elicit interlocutors' understanding of the interaction. Therapists tend to understand the episodes from a psychological perspective; only the episodes that were relevant to the theme of the study (interpersonal interaction, negative emotions, emotion management) were analyzed.

The consent for the recording and using data for academic purposes was gathered before the recordings of therapist-client interaction and the post-recording interview. The data collected remained anonymous; any information that could reveal a participant's identity or personal details was anonymized. Table 5.1 summarizes the data gathered and employed.

(2) Data selection. Since the reasons for people's visiting therapists vary from one to another, only the recordings that simultaneously satisfy the following three criteria are selected: (a) that the clients are suffering from depressed feelings at the time of data collection, usually ranging from a mild degree to a moderate degree; (b) that the clients' negative emotions are dynamically constructed, observable during the

Table 5.1 Summary of the data

<table><tr><td>Type of recording</td><td>Participants</td><td>Aim</td><td>Number/Length of collection</td></tr><tr><td>Naturally occurring dyadic therapist-client interactions</td><td>Actual participants: 4 therapists and 56 clients; Virtual participants; people with whom clients have long-standing or intimate relationships</td><td>To examine interactionally achieved meanings in terms of rapport and rapport management</td><td>86 psychotherapy sessions, ranging from 50–60 minutes, approximately 80 hours in total</td></tr><tr><td>Post-recording interviews</td><td>4 therapists and the researcher</td><td>To justify the analysis of the cause/management of negative emotions</td><td>32 recordings, 18 in the note form, ranging from 1–3 minutes(s)—approximately 40 minutes in total</td></tr></table>

psychotherapy session; (c) that clients' negative emotions are displayed when they are self-disclosing prior interpersonal interactions with the guanxi members.

In total, the 30 most representative negative emotion management episodes were extracted from 86 separate psychotherapy sessions, with 15 sessions involving clients' interaction with the non-present third parties (NPTPs) with whom they have a higher degree of intimacy (i.e., clients' parents or spouses) and 15 sessions involving clients' interaction with NPTPs with whom they had a lower degree of intimacy (clients' close friends or long-term collaborative partners). Each unit of psychotherapeutic discourse featured one client and one therapist. A total of two therapists and twenty-two clients were involved in this study.

(3) Data transcription. In the first step, the audio recordings were roughly transcribed that follow CA standards (Jefferson 2004), and the emotional signalling and their causes were identified. Based on these, single episodes were selected for more detailed transcription. The transcripts depict both the unfolding of negative emotions and the rapport-sensitive incidents reported during this process. When transcribing the data, contextual information, such as clients' social role, and the relational history between clients and the guanxi members, were noted for further examination.

# 5.4 Methodological Issues

In this part, the coding schemes, the analytical method, and the dimensions for observing the effect of psychotherapeutic intervention on negative emotions are introduced.

# 5.4.1 the Coding Schemes

The ways for identifying different types of negative emotions will first be presented, followed by the ways for identifying the types of ganqing violation-induced discordant rapport (hereafter GQVI discordant rapport) that exist between clients and NPTPs. Then, the linguistic devices for indicating 'surrogacy' and 'pseudo-surrogacy', and the linguistic realizations of different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies, will be illustrated.

(1) Coding of negative emotions. Since emotions can be responses to perceptions of discordant rapport (Spencer-Oatey 2005; Isik-Guler & Ruhi 2010), locating these emotions in the interactional sequences would reveal to what antecedent events they are responding and

discover the bases of discordant rapport (e.g., the types of rapport threats, or the types of sociality rights that have been infringed).

The negative emotions (anger, guilt, and sadness) are manifested through emotion words, or in a composite way. With regards to the 'composite emotional signal' (Clark 1996; Langlotz & Locher 2013: 101), it means to signal one's emotional status by describing it with emotional words, and at the same time indicating it with vocal cues. Table 5.2 below presents the emotional signalling of three types of negative emotions.

(2) Coding of GQVI discordant rapport. This part discusses the identification of the triggers of negative emotions (i.e., the types of GQVI discordant rapport). Disharmonious relationships between two interlocutors could be caused by unmet qing expectations (Ran & Zhao 2018; Ran 2018; Zhao & Ran 2019). In other words, one party performs an act that hurts the other party's feelings (renqing) (Zhao 2017). According to the related notions of ganqing (Yang 1994; Chang & Holt 1994; Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998; Chang 2013, 2018; Zhu 2015; Ledeneva 2008), also the bases of rapport (Spencer-Oatey 2000, 2008), the notions of discordant rapport (Spencer-Oatey 2005; Spencer-Oatey & Franklin 2009: 102; Lauriks et al. 2015), GQVI discordant rapport involves the following two sub-categories: (a) unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport: Emotional detachment and being

Table 5.2 Coding of negative emotions

<table><tr><td>Type of negative emotions</td><td>Emotional signalling</td></tr><tr><td>Anger</td><td>• Verbal cues: speech acts of complaints (e.g., Räabis et al. 2019; Langlotz &amp; Locher 2013);
• Vocal cues, high pitch register, with rising-falling pitch, and in a pressed tense (Reber 2008, 2012; Selting 2010);</td></tr><tr><td>Sadness</td><td>• Verbal cues: emotional words such as &#x27;sad&#x27; and &#x27;hurt&#x27; (Culpeper 2011);
• Vocal cues: crying sounds (i.e., crying; Hepburn &amp; Potter 2007); a strong display of sadness through sobbing (Sorjonen &amp; Peräkylä 2012); intensified prosody (Muntigl &amp; Horvath 2014);</td></tr><tr><td>Guilt</td><td>• Verbal cues: emotional words such as guilt (Culpeper 2011; Haidt 2003);
• Emotion description: taking responsibility for one&#x27;s wrongful actions (Ho et al. 2004: 73)
• Implication through lexical connotations or conceptual implications (Langlotz &amp; Locher 2012; Schwarz-Friesel 2007)</td></tr></table>

treated without feelings are rapport threats; and (b) unfulfilled ganning obligation-induced discordant rapport. Filial obligation omission behaviour and failure to repay the other's emotional debt are the rapport threats.

The rapport threats that lead to the GQVI discordant rapport and clients' negative emotions can be reflected in the linguistic and non-linguistic cues—clients' complaints, and therapists' meta-pragmatic comments on these complaints. Table 5.3 shows the ways to identify GQVI discordant rapport.

Table 5.3 Coding of ganqing violation-induced discordant rapport

<table><tr><td>Categories</td><td>Definitions</td><td>Linguistic indicators</td></tr><tr><td>Unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport</td><td>Disharmony results from Cs&#x27;1 unmet expectations, the latter of which are based on the relational principle of ganqing</td><td>Cs&#x27; complaining ways of speaking that indicate detachment/distance (e.g., &#x27;XX is far away&#x27;; &#x27;when my father talks with me, I feel he is talking with a very ordinary person&#x27;)NPTPs&#x27; lack of consideration for Cs&#x27; feelings or lack of mutual good feelings (&#x27;he thinks I only want money from him, don&#x27;t care about him)Ts&#x27; meta-pragmatic comments that indicate NPTPs&#x27; detachment/de-empathy (e.g., &#x27;they can&#x27;t understand us&#x27;; &#x27;she is always saying something to hurt us&#x27;) and Cs&#x27; acceptance in the next turns</td></tr><tr><td>Unfulfilled ganqing obligation-induced discordant rapport</td><td>Disharmony results from Cs&#x27; unfulfilled obligations, the latter of which are based on the relational principle of ganqing</td><td>Cs&#x27; quoting of NPTPs&#x27; complaints to them, the content of which involves Cs&#x27; failure in reciprocating (&#x27;he says that I only care about money, but not him&#x27;)The negative assertion that indicates Cs fail to fulfil filial obligations (e.g., &#x27;I think that I don&#x27;t care about my mother at all&#x27;)Ts&#x27; meta-pragmatic comments that indicate Cs&#x27; failure to reciprocate (e.g., &#x27;we owe him&#x27;) and Cs&#x27; acceptance in the next turns</td></tr></table>

<sup>1</sup> From Table 5.3 to 5.6, 'Cs' refers to clients, 'Ts' refers to therapists, and 'NPTPs' refers to non-present third parties.

Table 5.4 Linguistic devices for indicating surrogacy

<table><tr><td>Types of Surrogacies</td><td>Linguistic indicators</td></tr><tr><td>Surrogating
Cs (pseudo surrogacy)</td><td>·Use of inclusive ‘we’ pronoun when comments on Cs’ prior social interaction with NPTPs (Mazer et al. 2014; Coupland &amp; Coupland 2001);
·Syntactic reduction to animate the imaginary footing of Cs;
·Animating the complaining tone of Cs (e.g., high-pitched voice) (Choe 2020; Gordon 2009)</td></tr><tr><td>Surrogating
NPTPs</td><td>·Use of first-person singular ‘I’ pronoun when speaking as NPTPs (i.e., direct animating of NPTPs’ voice) (Choe 2020; Tannen 2007)
·Use of third-person pronoun when describing NPTPs’ thoughts (i.e., indirect animating of NPTPs’ voice) (Mazer et al. 2014)</td></tr></table>

Coding of surrogate-based rapport management strategies. An examination of the data reveals that therapists are conscious and deliberate when they respond to clients' negative emotions caused by GQVI discordant rapport, which also can be verified from the dynamic discourse roles therapists take during psychotherapy sessions. When managing the GQVI, therapists take two kinds of discourse roles: (a) surrogating NPTPs, speaking on behalf of them, or speaking for them; or (b) surrogating clients (which is featured as pseudo-surrogacy). According to the prior studies, the linguistic devices for indicating surrogacy can be summarized as follows (cf. Table 5.4).

Surrogate-based rapport management concerns how therapists animate the voice of the people they surrogate (either clients or NPTPs) to manage the disharmony between clients and NPTPs. Based on prior research on rapport management between direct interlocutors, the surrogate-based rapport management strategies deployed by therapists are coded and categorized.

# 5.4.2 Examining Data from an Interactional Approach

An interactional approach, which is informed by findings and methods in CA (conversation analysis) (Arundale 2005: 56-69; Schegloff 2005: 474-475; Arundale 2010: 2094), concentrates on 'participants' interpretations and evaluations of persons and relationships in locally-situated discourse' (Haugh et al. 2015: 77). When taking such a discursive approach, the focus of analysis is not only placed on the actions but also the interpersonal stances and interpersonal evaluations in situated interactions (Arundale

Table 5.5 Surrogate-based rapport management strategies

<table><tr><td>Categories</td><td>Linguistic means and examples</td><td>Ts&#x27; discourse role</td></tr><tr><td>The accounting strategies</td><td>The use of linguistic devices designed to account for the addressees&#x27; right-threatening behaviour or obligation- omission behaviour, e.g., excusing NTPPs&#x27; emotional detachment from Cs by appealing to their impairments</td><td>Surrogating NTPPs</td></tr><tr><td>The aggressive strategies</td><td>The use of linguistic devices designed to attack the addressees (i.e., NTPPs), to reduce their self-esteem, or to destroy their social images (Meibauer 2014), e.g., using assertions to claim or to criticize NTPPs&#x27; lack of feelings towards Cs</td><td>Surrogating Cs (pseudo surrogacy)</td></tr><tr><td>The denial strategies</td><td>The use of strategies designed to deny the existence of the problem (i.e., Cs&#x27; unfulfilling of reciprocal obligations) or disagree with NTPPs&#x27; negative comments, e.g., denying an accusation with an explicit denial</td><td>Surrogating Cs (pseudo surrogacy)</td></tr></table>

2010: 2079, 2095). In Kádár and Mills (2011: 7-8), the features of an interactional approach are summarized as follows:

(1) It is discourse-based, as is reflected in the analysis of 'longer fragments of authentic interactions' and 'on the contextual variation of interpretation'. (2) Dual-focus of the utterances, which involves the speaker's production of utterances as well as the hearer's evaluation of them. (3) It is suitable to examine a range of relational phenomena, such as politeness and impoliteness. An interactional approach shares similarities with CA but differs somewhat from it. On the one hand, the interactional approach depicts how the participant understands and orients to the interactional practices, just as with CA traditions (e.g., Sacks 1992; Peräkylä et al. 2008). On the other hand, the interactional approach offers more 'theoretically motivated formulations of meanings, actions, and attitudes towards/evaluations of persons and relationships' (Haugh et al. 2015: 78).

The interactional approach facilitates the exploration of negative emotion management in the following aspects: the first aspect concerns how these negative emotions arise from and to what events they respond to. Drawing on the sequentiality of social action, the trigger for clients' negative emotions can be identified. By locating the presence of clients' negative

Table 5.6 Therapists' identification of the causes of negative emotions

<table><tr><td>·Cs&#x27; self-disclosure (display of negative emotions)</td></tr><tr><td>·Ts&#x27; identification of the causes of negative emotions</td></tr><tr><td>·Directly verbalizes ganqing violation behaviours (meta-pragmatic comments)</td></tr><tr><td>·Shows acceptance of the causes as expressed by Cs
—Repeat Cs&#x27; utterance and incorporate it into their utterances (Lerner 2004: 230)
—Elaborate an interpretation of the prior utterance (Peräkylä 2008: 115)
—Formulate or extend the prior utterance (Vehviläinen 2008: 130)
—Acknowledgment tokens (Bercelli et al. 2008: 54).</td></tr></table>

emotions in their self-reports about the interpersonal issues in the interactional sequences in which they occur, the close link between negative emotional reactions and the interpersonal issues (e.g., the types of rapport threats) can be identified (Işik-Güler & Ruhi 2010). The second aspect of the interactional approach facilitates the exploration of negative emotions management lies in the identification of the causes of negative emotions. Such identification is based on: (1) the types of discordant rapport that are disclosed in clients' self-reports; and (2) the 'sequential organization' of talk (Schegloff 1972; Levinson 2006), i.e., how therapists understand the preceding utterances. The ways through which therapists show their acceptance of clients' utterances are summarized in Table 5.6.

# 5.4.3 Effects of Surrogate-based Rapport Management and Negative Emotions

Given that emotions are 'socially regulated phenomena' (Fiehler 2002: 79) and are 'communicative observables' (Langlotz & Locher 2017: 288) which could be approached from a process-orientated perspective, the effect of therapists' intervention (i.e., surrogate-based rapport management) on clients' negative emotions can be observed. Importantly, emotion management involves both reducing/subduing or enhancing the experienced emotion (Thompson 1994: 29; Southam-Gerow & Kendall 2002). The dimensions and cues for observing changes in one's experienced emotions are listed in Figure 5.1.

In summary, drawing on the interactional approach, the effect of the management of clients' negative emotions is observable from the next turns. Different aspects of talk (e.g., sequential organization, turn construction, lexical choices, phonetic details) can be scrutinized to understand the interlocutors' emotionality (Local & Walker 2008).

Figure 5.1 Dimensions for observing the effect of emotion management.

# 5.5 Conclusion

The chapter familiarizes the readers with the data and sheds light on the research method of the study. By operationalizing some of the theoretical concepts introduced in the previous chapters, it steps up to the analysis in the next part of the book.

The interpersonal psychotherapy sessions provide us with an ideal communicative environment in which emotion management can be explored, in particular from an interpersonal pragmatics approach. Dealing with clients' negative emotions is a key issue of therapy talk, while the ways the negative emotions are managed are closely related to the therapeutic school the therapists adopt. The existing literature mainly takes a conversation analytic approach to examine the practices employed to manage clients' negative emotions in therapeutic contexts such as cognitive-constructivist therapy sessions, and emotion-focused therapy. Seldom do the studies consider the causes of negative emotions while examining emotion management. While interpersonal psychotherapists believe that interpersonal disputes trigger clients' depressed feelings or negative emotions, and interpersonal change brings positive changes to clients' emotional status. Such a therapeutic protocol resonates with the interpersonal pragmatists' understanding of negative emotions and facilitates an investigation into the interrelationship between interpersonal disharmony and negative emotions.

The dataset is composed of two parts: 86 therapy sessions between the interpersonal psychotherapists and clients, and 32 talks between therapists and the researcher.

While transcribing the data, the episodes that involve clients' disclosure of the disharmonious relationship between them and their intimates and the display of negative emotions are repeatedly listened to and carefully examined. Relying on the existing literature on negative emotions, disharmony, ganqing, and surrogacy, I have developed a catalogue of positioning the types of negative emotions, the types of disharmonies that trigger negative emotions, and the rapport practices employed to manage the negative emotions based on the data itself.

This study takes an interactional approach to examine the data. Such an approach absorbs the advantages of the conversation analytic method and presents the readers with the sequential features of the long fragments of authentic interactions. Moreover, it pays attention to the interpretation of relational meanings and relationships. Such a research method facilitates the exploration of negative emotions and its management. For instance, from the interactional sequences in which negative emotions occur, their triggers can be identified. Also, an interpersonal pragmatics motivated interpretation of negative emotions reveals that these emotions are evoked by disharmonious relationships. The types of relationships and the interactional principle guiding interpersonal interaction are further activated to understand the triggers of emotions. Also activated is the rapport management framework which explains therapists' response to clients' negative emotions.
