But that's a very frustrating thing.</td></tr><tr><td>22</td><td>C: 是，可能他也有他的伤……Yes, maybe he has his heartbreak too...</td></tr></table>

In the above extract, C's anger and sadness are evoked by the unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport. It is C's boyfriend's lack of feelings (i.e., he fails to reciprocate the good feeling to C) that threatens the rapport. To manage negative emotions, T firstly points out that C lacks real feelings towards the boy (Turn 7 and Turn 9). C's ratification of

T's argument is indicated by the interactional strategy of latching and by building her comment as an extension of T's prior turn (Turn 8 and Turn 10) (Pawelczyk 2011: 194).

Based on this shared understanding, T serves as the surrogate of C's boyfriend and gives a reason on behalf of him to account for why C's good feelings towards the boy are not reciprocated. Specifically, he addresses C's boyfriend by using the third-person singular pronoun 'he' and animates his beliefs (Tannen 1986, 2004; Gordon 2009) ('because he doesn't feel your love for him, So, he said he didn't like you', Turn 15 and Turn 17). That is, the boy didn't feel C's love towards him. In so doing, T speaks for the boy and accounts for his ganqing violation behaviour. In her response, C ratifies T's account not only by mere 'yes' statements (Turn 16, 18, 22), but also by providing further disclosure (Turn 18) and accounting for the boy's behaviour (Turn 22). Positive changes in C's emotional status are observable after the intervention, as signalled through the valence of emotion (i.e., her laughter and her smiling voice, Turn 12, 14, 20). Before this extract, C's sadness is explicitly communicated through her crying voice (data not shown here).

(5) Surrogating NPTPs and appealing to clients' responsibilities

During the psychotherapeutic discourse, clients' negative emotions arise when they experience emotional detachment from NPTPs. Under such a circumstance, therapists manage these negative emotions by offering an excuse, e.g., by appealing to the responsibilities of clients.

Extract 7.5 C's negative emotion of sadness emerges when she reports how she experiences exclusion when interacting with a close friend.

1 T：为什么她和你在一起的时候，总是提那几个朋友？

Why does she always mention those friends when she is with you?

2 C: 我不知道，我也有朋友。在她面前的时候，也不会提谁谁谁对我多好。

I don't know. I also have friends, too. I won't mention who is good to me in front of her.

T：是啊，因为我们知道如果那做，对方不好受。你和别人的关系可能会偷偷好，但是也不会拿出来显摆，是不是？

Yes, because we know that if we do that, the other party will not feel well. You may have a good relationship with others, but you won't show it off, will you?

4 C: 是。

是。

T: 那样会刺激人的。不过有一种可能你会那么做。比如两人谈恋爱，女孩和男孩说，今天和谁吃了一顿饭啊，故意用这种方式去刺激这个男孩，为什么要这样？

That would be provoking. But there's a possibility that you might do that. For example, when two people fall in love, the girl tells the boy whom she has dinner with today, that she deliberately stimulates the boy in this way, why?

C：我没有听太懂。

I don't quite understand.

T：女孩和男孩在热恋中，但是因为什么吵架了，后来女孩就告诉男孩，她和谁谁谁吃饭了。为什么要这样？

The girl and the boy fall head over heels in love, but they quarrel because of something. Later, the girl tells the boy whom she dines with. Why does the girl do this?

C：吃醋啊。你说的例子我不太喜欢，但是我知道你的意思。

我自己不会这样的，如果我很珍惜这段感情，我自己不会这样的……不过现在这种方式也挺好的，我想约她就约……

Because of jealousy. I don't like the example you say, but I know what you mean. I won't do it myself if I cherish this relationship, I won't do it like this...but it is a good way now, I'd like to ask her out...

T：所以你们之间的关系的节奏都是你控制的？

So the rhythm of your relationship is controlled by you?

C: ((笑)).

((Laughing)).

T：是吧？>↑你想保持在什么距离就什么距离上？<如果你是她，你什么心情？

Right?  $>\uparrow$  You can stay at whatever distance you want.< If you were her, how would you feel?

C: ¥很恼火¥

 ¥Very annoyed¥

T：然后在你面前说几句不爱听的，↑你就怎么了？

$\succ$  本身我就很有气的  $<$  ，对不对？

Then I say something in front of you that you don't like to hear,
↑what's the matter with you? >↑In fact I am very angry myself<, aren't I?

C：我想起来了……[省略4个话轮]

I remember something … [4 turns omitted]

T：那是不是意味着她是很在乎你的，或者她对这段关系是小心翼翼的？

Does that mean she cares so much about you, or does it mean she's careful about the relationship?

C：我在想有可能。她可能觉得关系好才那样说。她是聪明能干，但是她在情感方面其实也很脆弱。

I think it is possible. Or to say that because she assumes, she has a good relationship with others. She is very clever and very capable, but she's also emotionally vulnerable.

T：看起来她-她还是很珍惜这份友谊的。当你们走得近的时候，她会可能觉得很随便。有的时候她说话啊，或者训你的方式，都像-很随便的家里人一样，但是可能我们比较脆弱，她对我们没有见外，但是我们觉得你这么不见外，容易伤人=

It seems that she-she still cherishes this friendship very much. When you get close, she may feel very casual. Sometimes she talks or teaches you in the same way as a very casual family member, but maybe we are vulnerable. She doesn't see us as outsiders, but we think such a manner is easy to hurt people and easy to hurt people=

C: =对。是这样。

=Yes.That's right.

In the above extract, C's anger towards her close friend and strong sadness are triggered by unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport (threatened by her close friend's emotional detachment and lack of feelings towards her). Having identified the cause of the negative emotions, T uses several linguistic strategies to account for the NPTP's ganqing violation behaviour.

First, T justifies the NPTP's behaviour by 'transcendence' (i.e., placing the problematic event in a different, positive context) (Ware & Linkugel 1973; Benoit 2015: 25). T asks C to think about why Xixi always talks about other friends in front of her (Turn 1). Not having obtained the desirable answer from C, a certain communicative mode between a boy and a girl in love is offered by T (Turn 5 and Turn 7), serving as a hint for the question. C's response ('but I know what you mean', Turn 8), indicates that the hint works. By employing transcendence, T intends to minimize the severity of the offence and the negative influence on the perceived rapport. Although this justification strategy is recognized by C, it is not well accepted by her, as evidenced by the expression that she would not act in that way ('…I won't do it like this', Turn 8).

Second, T serves as the surrogate of C's friend and speaks like her. Specifically, T animates C's friend's voice and talks as if he were her (Choe 2020: 70) ('↑ What's the matter with you? >↑ I am very angry <', Turn 13; '>↑ You can stay at whatever distance you want?', Turn 11) in a complaining tone (as shown in the high pitch register and tensed voice, cf. Selting 2010). In so doing, T serves as the surrogate of C's friend and challenges the appropriateness of C's communication habits, given that this habit has violated the moral order of fairness (Kádár & Haugh 2013: 73). C's response, which expresses that she would be very angry if she were in her friend's position (Turn 12), indicates that she has realized the inappropriateness of her behaviour. In this way, T appeals to C's responsibility in the GQVI discordant rapport and reduces the NPTP's responsibility for ganging-violation behaviour.

Third, the closeness between C and her close friend is utilized by T to account for C's friends' inappropriate way of talking: that C has been treated by Xixi as a family member explains why Xixi talks with her in such a casual way (Turn 19, 21, 23). C ratifies T's account by providing further disclosure (Turn 20) and by latching an immediate agreement (Pawelczyk 2011: 194) with T's account. As for the communicative effect of T's intervention, positive changes in C's emotional status are observable from the vocal cues (i.e., signalled through her laughter in turns 10 and 12), contrasting with her crying sound when she talks about the unmet ganqing expectations.

In sum, T first justifies the NPTP's ganqing violation behaviour through the strategy of transcendence, but he finds that it is less effective. Then, T

surrogates the NPTP and offers another justification by appealing to C's responsibility in the failure event, which is accepted by C in the later turns.

(6) Surrogating NPTPs and appealing to their negative past

When accounting for violence, offence, or problematic events, people can appeal to a wide range of factors (Blumstein et al. 1974; Gordon 2011; Gerholm 2011). According to Schonbach (1980: 197), people can excuse themselves by appealing to a negative past, for instance, a childhood under oppressive situations can serve as an excuse.

Extract 7.6 C's sadness and annoyance emerge when she recalls the prior interaction with her mother.

This way of treating people doesn&#x27;t work well with my father. If she made my father angry, my father would throw things.</td></tr><tr><td>18</td><td>T: 所以你就是他的替罪羊。So, you&#x27;re his scapegoat.</td></tr><tr><td>19</td><td>C: 我不知道……((讲述妈妈从青少年到长大的经历))。我怀疑一个原因, 她可能被我奶奶苛责过, 因为我奶奶在文革时挨批斗过……I don&#x27;t know… ((talking about her mother&#x27;s experiences from being a teenager to an adult)). I suspect a reason; she may have been criticized by my grandmother because my grandmother was denounced during the Cultural Revolution…</td></tr><tr><td>20</td><td>T: 是啊, 显然-有她自己经历给她造成的一些-她肯定不是故意的,或者说=Yeah, something about her own experiences - she certainly didn&#x27;t mean to, or to say=</td></tr><tr><td>21</td><td>C: =对, 我也能明白。她肯定不是故意的=Yes, I can understand. She certainly didn&#x27;t mean to=</td></tr><tr><td>22</td><td>T: =¥但是她造成的结果却是很有破坏性的¥=¥But the results are devastating¥</td></tr></table>

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">23</td><td rowspan="2">C:</td><td>嗯，我现在还抱有希望想稍微改变一下她，让自己稍微好过一点，还没有达到说我百分百去接受，去完全化解，所以我还是有点儿难过。</td></tr><tr><td>Mm, I still hope that I can change her a little and make myself better. It hasn&#x27;t reached the point that I&#x27;m going to accept it 100% and completely resolve it, so I&#x27;m still a little sad.</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">24</td><td rowspan="2">T:</td><td>可是这一点怎么能做得到？按照专业的话讲，她病得已经太重了已经没法治。</td></tr><tr><td>But how can this be done? In professional terms, she was too sick to be treated.</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">25</td><td rowspan="2">C:</td><td>((笑))。她很辛苦。每天，你知道吗，你看她都61了，一早上起来，7点就从家里走，一直熬到下午4,5点钟……</td></tr><tr><td>((Laughing)). She lives a hard life. Every day, you know, she&#x27;s 61 years old, shortly after she gets up in the morning, she leaves home at 7 o&#x27;clock, and stays up until 4 or 5 p.m.</td></tr></table>

C's anger is caused by the unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport, and C's mother's failure to attend to her feelings and needs is the cause of the disharmony. To manage C's negative emotions, T serves as the surrogate of C's mother and excuses her ganqing violation behaviour by appealing to her 'negative past' (Schönbach 1980: 197). This is achieved through a series of turns.

First, a 'pre-accounting sequence', the content of which negates the mother's intention to hurt the daughter, is initiated by the therapist (Terasaki 2004[1976]; Schegloff 2007). This sequence is realized through a question-answer sequence. In Turn 9, a question targeting the reason for the mother's ganqing violation behaviour is proposed. In Turn 14, T speaks for C's mother by using an assertion ('She wasn't out of hostility or not loving her daughter'). In Turn 15, C responds with the acknowledgement tokens ('Yes, yes, yes') and provides further information. This shows C's acceptance of T's argument.

Then, the therapist speaks on behalf of C's mother and constructs an account for her ganqing violation behaviour. In Turn 16, an interrogative is initiated by T to explain the mother's behaviour. At Turn 18, he provides an answer by drawing on C's response, however, this is disclaimed by C in the next turn. Based on the further self-disclosure C makes on her mother, T speaks for C's mother by referring to her in the third person pronoun and providing an account of her behaviour in Turn 20. That is, to account for the mother's behaviour by appealing to her negative past. As for C's response, she demonstrates agreement by echoing T's utterance, indicating her acceptance of the account and the restoration of the social order (Blumstein et al. 1974; Goffman 1971: 171-202). This process also witnesses a positive change in C's emotional status. At the beginning of this extract, a strong degree of anger is displayed through C's high-pitched voice, the intensifiers ('very'), and the speech act of complaints. After the

enactment of accounting, a weakened status of negative emotion is communicated, as reflected through the mitigators ('…a little sad', Turn 23) she used to describe her feelings. This analysis shows that negative emotions caused by unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport can be managed by surrogate-based rapport management strategies (i.e., serving as surrogates of NPTPs and accounting by appealing to their negative past).

# 7.2.2 Pseudo-surrogating Clients and Using Aggressive Strategies

Aggressive strategies, which are linguistically realized using criticisms, insults, and accusations, are employed by speakers to respond to rudeness (Beebe & Waring 2005). By using such a strategy, the speaker can 'wound the addressee or bring a third party into disrespect' (Allan & Burridge 2006: 79), given that the discursive strategy of 'moral aggression' is linked to morality and can be used for 'the construction of the moral order' (Parvaresh & Kádár 2019: 4). The following extract demonstrates how the therapist performs 'pseudo-surrogacy (Mazer et al. 2014) and attacks the NPTP's ganqing violation behaviour.

(1) Pseudo-surrogating clients and criticizing NPTPs' ganqing-violating behaviour

The speech act of criticism refers to 'a speaker saying that s/he thinks someone else is doing something bad' (Wilkins 1986: 578), which shows the speaker's disapproval. During the dyadic therapist-client interaction, NPTPs' behaviours revealed in clients' self-disclosure are open to evaluation. Therapists often employ criticism to express their dissatisfaction with the inappropriateness of NPTPs. Stating a fact and rhetorical questions are the common ways to criticize (Tang 2016: 120).

Extract 7.7 C's annoyance towards her long-term collaborative partner arises when she reports how she is not empathically responded to by him.

```txt
1 C:  $\rightarrow$  ↑就是为什么都是我不对<，都是↑我不对，我怎么做能做得更好？>都是我不对<，为什么全是我成长，对方错了，%我还得帮()% $\rightarrow$  Why I am wrong always<，↑I am not right, how can I do better? >it is all my fault<，why I need to grow up even if the other person is wrong, %I must help ()%
2 T:  $=>$  ↑凭什么呀！< $=>$  ↑For what reason! <
3 C:  $>+$  凭什么呀！<对方错了就是错了。如果我开心，可以说我再调整调整，我再灵活一点；我也可以不开心。这么多年他都一直这么教别人。 $\rightarrow$  ↑For what reason! < If the other person is wrong, he is wrong, and no one else should bear the burden. If I'm happy, I can say I'll adjust again, I'll be more flexible. I can be unhappy. He has been teaching people that for years.
```

<table><tr><td>4</td><td>T:</td><td>看起来我们的感受，我们的喜怒哀乐是不重要的，完全[都It seems that our feelings, our joy, anger, and sorrow are unimportant, totally [all</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>C:</td><td>[↑对[↑yes</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>T:</td><td>没有价值，没有用=No value, no use=</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>C:</td><td>=对，你的喜怒哀乐不重要。就是你想不想变得更好啊！%成长得更好啊%=Yes, your joy, anger, and sorrow are not important. Do you want to be better or not! % growing better %</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>→</td><td>T: &gt;↑什么叫好啊? &lt; &gt;↑ What means good? &lt;</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>C:</td><td>((哭))((crying))</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>T:</td><td>就是对别人有用，It&#x27;s useful to others,</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>C:</td><td>°对°</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>T:</td><td>°Yes°</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>C:</td><td>°差不多°,对方( )，你都是想怎么怎么样，我怎么做得更好。尤其这两年这些观念使得我性格太压抑了……(进一步的自我讲述))°Almost °, the other person ( ) , what do you want to do, how can I do better. Especially in the past two years, these ideas have made me very depressed… (further self-disclosure))</td></tr></table>

In the above interaction, C's anger and sadness are manifested by her high-pitched voice, speech acts of complaint, and an act of crying (Planalp 1998; Langlotz & Locher 2013). These negative emotions are caused by the GQVI discordant rapport between C and her collaborative partner. More specifically, it is because he shows little concern for C's feelings that has threatened the rapport between them and evoked C's emotional reactions.

According to Chaemsaithong (2012: 469), the speakers can signal who they are at any interactional moment by resorting to linguistic markers such as a change in tone and pitch and style-shifting. Confronted with C's negative emotions, T states 'for what reason' in a high-pitched voice and a rushed manner (Turn 2), using a pitch and tone similar to C. During this process, T serves as a pseudo-surrogate by speaking as if C is unable to speak for herself (from C's prior turns, C is fully capable of expressing herself). By echoing the therapist literally and with the same intonation pattern, C ratifies T's comments and shows her affiliation with T (Pawelczyk 2011: 124) in Turn 3. Moreover, further self-disclosure, in which C releases her suppressed aspects of experience and emotional tensions (ibid: 97), is reported by C.

In the later turns, T continues to pseudo-surrogate C and speaks as C, as indicated through the use of the inclusive 'we' pronoun ('…our feelings, our joy, anger, and sorrow are unimportant, Turn 7'), and in a similar high

pitch<sup>1</sup> (Choe 2020: 63). When T speaks as C, T is attacking C's collaborative partner, thereby reducing his social image (Meibauer 2014).

To sum up, by pseudo-surrogating C, T aggressively criticizes C's collaborative partner's lack of feeling. According to the discordant rapport orientations laid out earlier in Chapter 4(cf. Section 4.3.2), T's practice is dedicated to maintaining disharmony between C and her collaborative partner. Such a surrogate-based rapport management strategy functions to reduce C's negative emotions. On the one hand, it helps C release emotional tension, as manifested by her echoing T's position (Ferrara 1994), extending T's dissatisfactory stance towards her collaborative partner (Turn 3 and Turn 7). On the other hand, the process of rapport management witnesses the minimizing of C's anger and sadness (Fiehler 2002: 79), that is, she speaks angrily in a high-pitched voice at the start of the extract (Turn 1), followed by a lower and softer voice in the latter part of the extract (Turn 11).

(2) Pseudo-surrogating clients and accusing NPTPs of ganqing-violating behaviour

Accusing someone involves 'displaying a negative affective and moral stance about the conduct of another party' (Haugh & Sinkeviciute 2018: 249). In making an accusation, the accuser is claiming that the accused has transgressed moral orders or has done wrong things. Accusations 'function to construct and maintain social order' (Castor 2015: 20). When therapists accuse NPTPs, they not only show their disapproval of their misdeeds but also believe that NPTPs are guilty of doing that. The following extract shows how therapists pseudo-surrogate clients and accuse NPTPs' ganqing-violating behaviour through utterances with derogative meanings.

Extract 7.8 C's negative emotions occur when he reports how he is inattentively treated by his mother.

[4 turns are omitted].

5

C：我妈说，我知道你疼，牙疼不是病，疼起来要命，你给消停

一点。可我就是疼，就是想发泄一下。后来就吃点儿牙-

疼损，那个吃了也疼，我妈就说“牙疼咋的啊？↑你咋就挺不了！

真的↑就给我↑气：……的啊！当时疼痛了，疼肿了，疼肿了……

My mother said I knew your tooth hurt, toothache was not a disease, but the pain was terrible, you should calm down. But it hurt, I just wanted to let it out. I took some medicine later, but it didn't work well. Then my mother said, it is only the toothache, '↑ why you could not bear it?' I was so angry:......, the pain was terrible and the tooth was swollen with pain, the tooth was swollen with pain...

6

T： 嗯。

Mm.

7

C：我同学都说，你回家说说你妈，你妈咋这样呢？

但是我回家我还是忍住了。对了，

我妈还咋说呢，“我大儿子挺住啊！我大儿子最坚强啊！”哎：....忍啊！

All my classmates said that my mother should be criticized. How

could she behave in such a way? But when I got home, I held my

temper. By the way, what did my mother say? She said that 'my

son could bear the pain, and my son was the toughest!' Well:  $\uparrow$  it

was difficult to hold back the temper.

8

T: 多憋屈啊! =

How suffocating it is!

9

C:  $= \uparrow$  愕屈，确实是憋屈。

$= \uparrow$  It is suffocating.

10

T：这种事情还挺多=

There are many things like this=

11

C: =嗯，还有，我得个病毒性感冒，那几天吐得都起不来了，打完吊瓶，立马给我送学校去了，到了学校我照样吐。

$= \mathrm{{Mm}}$  ,also, I had a viral cold, I kept vomiting those days and I could

not get up, when I had finished the injection, my mother sent me

to school immediately, and I vomited when I was at school.

12

T：她也没有说让你回来？

She didn't ask you to come back?

13

C: =没。有一次医生都说，说这当妈的不够格，真的。那次我阑尾炎，

疼得都不行了，我妈就说疼疼得了，肚子疼还能咋的。之后我受

不了了，就自个儿去医院，阑尾炎都要穿孔了。后来医生都和我

妈说，你这个家长咋这样呢？这个娃儿都要穿孔了。

she was. That time I got appendicitis and it hurt so badly. My

mother just let me hurt. She said it didn't matter to have stomach

pain. I couldn't bear it anymore, and then I went to the hospital,

only to find the appendicitis was in a terrible condition. The

doctors criticized my mother, saying she shouldn't have treated

me like that, and that my appendix was about to perforate.

14

T：你妈没说什么？

Didn't your mother say anything?

15

C：没说啥，她一遇到这种事情就说我不知道，她还觉得挺委屈的。之后还是这样，没变，让我觉得↑很反感。

No. She said that I didn't know when she met this kind of thing, she

even felt aggrieved. She remained unchanged since then, which

makes me feel  $\uparrow$  very disgusted.

16.

T：感觉她总是无动于衷，有的时候，就是无论我们遭什么罪，她好像都无动于衷。

It seems that she is always indifferent, and sometimes, whatever we

suffer, she seems to be indifferent.

17

C：她最在意的是学习，学习是第一位的。但是我感觉，咋说呢？

你没命了，你还咋学啊？我说我生病了不能好好听课，她说你就将

就一点儿，说能多学点儿是一点儿。

What she cares about most is study. But I feel, how do I put it? If

you were dead, how could you do your study? I tell her that it is

difficult to focus on the study when I am ill. She would reply that

a little more is better than nothing and ask me to accumulate little

by little.

As C's self-disclosure reveals, his mother doesn't pay much attention to his needs and feelings. This unmet ganqing expectation negatively influences C's perceived rapport, as reflected in the speech acts of complaints towards his mother. C's strong degree of anger is manifested through negatively valued adjectives, prosodic cues, raised pitch, increased loudness, rhetorical questions, and several intensifiers (Drew 1998; Selting 2012; Prior 2016: 226; Raabis et al. 2019).

Upon hearing C's mother's lack of concern for C's needs when C's tooth hurts, T initiates a zero-person construction, i.e., 'How suffocating it is!' (Turn 8) (Ruusuvuori 2005: 210) to show his recognition of T's experience (Voutilainen et al. 2010). It also verbalizes C's emotional status now. After more disclosure is provided, T serves as a pseudo-surrogate of clients (as reflected in the 'we' pronoun) and initiates a 'moral evaluation' (Kádár & Haugh 2013: 6) to point out the inappropriateness of C's mother's behaviour: whatever the child is suffering, the mother is turning a blind eye to it ('whatever we suffer, she seems to be indifferent', Turn 16). An utterance with such derogative meaning shows the mother's behaviour has transgressed the relational principle of ganqing—a parent shows concern for the child's feelings and needs (Hwang 1987; Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998). The intervening process has witnessed the changes in C's emotional status: a high degree of anger at the beginning of the extract, and a weakened degree of negative emotions at the later part of the extract (e.g., signalled through the normal pitched voice in Turn 17).

(3) Pseudo-surrogating clients and abusing NPTPs' ganqing-violating behaviour

Sometimes therapists will serve as pseudo-surrogates of clients and use abusive words to comment on NPTPs' emotional detachment. The abuses, which are linguistically realized through rude, offensive, and aggressive words, display strong dissatisfaction or anger towards the NPTPs. In so doing, therapists are dedicated to protecting clients' association rights.

Extract 7.9 C feels very sad and angry because her affection is not mutually shared and reciprocated by her boyfriend.

<table><tr><td>1</td><td>→</td><td>T: 最终决定想和他在一起的时候，发现人家根本没想和我们在一起。When I finally decided to be with him, I found that they didn&#x27;t want to be with us at all.</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>C:</td><td>是啊!That&#x27;s right!</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>→</td><td>T: 就是我们之前做的很多努力其实都是徒劳的。Many of our previous efforts are in vain.</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>C:</td><td>对，他不明白我内心里面挣扎了多少次[Yes, he didn&#x27;t understand how many times I struggled inside]</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>T:</td><td>[看起来有一种There seems to be one</td></tr></table>

<table><tr><td>6</td><td>C: 他对我好，有的时候()，因为我觉得有些东西其实过了朋友的界限，He is kind to me, sometimes() , because I feel that some things are beyond the limits of friends,</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>T: 是，看起来他有一种玩弄感情的感觉。在我看来一个男孩这么对一个女孩，这个女孩这么考虑的时候，是一点儿问题也没有，Yes, it seems that he is playing with the affection. In my opinion, when a boy treats a girl like this and the girl thinks about it like this, this is not a problem at all,</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>C: 我也不知道，反正[I don&#x27;t know, anyway [</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>T: [可是就是给我们造成那样一个= [but he just made us such an=</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>C: =假象= =Illusion=</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>T: =假象。其实我觉得他也不是假象，也许-他有病= =Illusion. I don&#x27;t think he&#x27;s an illusion. Maybe he&#x27;s insane=</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>C: =↑有病 =↑ insane</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>T: 对。就是这样。就是我觉得，如果这么对十个女孩，十个女孩都会像你这样。Yes. This is it. That&#x27;s what I think if he does that to ten girls, ten girls will respond like you.</td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>C: 他可能就是想在我身上找到一些安慰吧。↑特别特别难过，你知道吗？要么睡不着，要么半夜醒来，就是心里很痛很痛的感觉…… Maybe he just wants to find some comfort from me. ↑ Very very sad, you know? Either can&#x27;t sleep or wake up in the middle of the night, which leads to very painful feelings in my heart…</td></tr></table>

In this extract, it is C's boyfriend's ganqing violation behaviour (lack of good feelings towards C and emotional detachment) that has threatened the rapport between them, and thus evoked C's anger and sadness. During the process of emotion management, T serves as a pseudo-surrogate of the client and speaks for her (Mazer et al. 2014; Choe 2020). Specifically, it is reflected in T's uses of the inclusive 'we' pronoun when he comments on C's boyfriend's behaviour (Turn 1, Turn 3).

Based on such a surrogate role, the aggressive strategies, which aim to criticize the boy's inappropriate behaviour, are enacted in a step by step way. In Turn 1 and Turn 3, T formulates C's prior utterance, which is ratified by C in the next turns through the acknowledgement token 'yes' and through C's further disclosure of her experience (Pawelczyk 2011) (Turn 4). It seems that T intends to give an evaluation but it is interrupted by C, as manifested through the overlapping utterance in Turn 5. After ratifying C's utterance through an acknowledgement token 'yes', T points out the 'moral fault' (that is, infringement, misbehaviour) of the NPTP: the boy is playing with the affection (Turn 7). In her response in Turn 8, C shows a disaffiliating response by uttering 'I don't know'. In Turn 11, an abusive expression 'insane' is initiated by T to criticize the NPTP's ganqing violation behaviour.

C echoes T's abusive expression with a heightened voice, which indicates C's demonstration of empathic agreement with it (Pawelczyk 2011: 124).

In Turn 14 and later turns, C gives further self-disclosure with an increased pitch and a louder voice, compared with her low-pitched sad voice at the beginning of this extract. This manifests the communicative effect of therapists' intervention on clients' negative emotions.

# 7.2.3 the Combined Strategy

Under some circumstances, a combined strategy will be adopted by therapists when managing clients' negative emotions that are caused by GQVI discordant rapport. The following is an example.

Extract 7.10 C, who is a pharmacist in a large hospital, talks about a prior interaction with a good friend A (a chair of a department). Before this extract, C discloses an interaction that makes her sad and angry. One day, A calls to ask for C's accompany to the beauty department of the hospital for a consultation. C agrees and goes to the beauty department in advance, aiming to say hello to the colleagues there and affiliating A's consultation. When C arrives and only finds that A has contacted the chair of the beauty department and they know each other quite well.

<table><tr><td>1</td><td>→</td><td>T:</td><td>我觉得她这件事情做得有点让我们很很-挺生气的哦! 她可以开始直接和我们把事情交代清楚的话, 可能会好一些。 I think she did something that made us very - very angry! Maybe it would be better if she could start talking to us about it directly.</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td></td><td>C:</td><td>对。 Yes.</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td></td><td>T:</td><td>比如说,我老公认识谁谁谁,我已经和他们联系好了,我只是让你过去给我壮壮胆。 For example,telling me in advance that &#x27;my husband knows someone,and he already contacted someone for me.I just asked you to come over and give me some courage&#x27;.</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td></td><td>C:</td><td>对。我觉得奇怪的是你什么也不和我说……</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>→</td><td>T:</td><td>Yes. What surprises me is that you don&#x27;t tell me anything…嗯,如果我是你那个朋友的话,我会觉得这么做确实不恰当,我不会这样做……如果没顾虑到这一点,会不会-有-一种没有把我当回事儿,有一种被-耍了的感觉。 Mm,if I were that friend,I would think it is inappropriate,I wouldn&#x27;t do it... If she didn&#x27;t think about it,would there be a sense that I wasn&#x27;t being taken seriously,that I was being played with.</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td></td><td>C:</td><td>我觉得我应该有这样的感觉。 I think I should feel that way.</td></tr><tr><td colspan="4">[省略22个话轮] [22 turns omitted]</td></tr><tr><td>29</td><td></td><td>C:</td><td>有一次,我们请她吃饭,同时还有她的朋友……我去了以后她 没到…… Once we invited her to dinner along with her friends...she didn&#x27;t arrive when I arrived...</td></tr></table>

<table><tr><td>30</td><td>T: 她怎么经常不出现哦!有两次了。Why doesn&#x27;t she always show up! Twice already.</td></tr><tr><td>31</td><td>C: 她在赶来的路上……She was on her way here…</td></tr><tr><td>32</td><td>T: 嗯。这么想来,她并没有觉得这一切有什么问题哦。Mm. Thinking about it, she didn&#x27;t think there was anything wrong with all this.</td></tr><tr><td>33</td><td>C: 对呀。然后她应该没有觉得有什么问题……That&#x27;s right. She shouldn&#x27;t have thought it was a problem…</td></tr><tr><td>34</td><td>T: 感觉你这个朋友,她是遵循着有朋友好办事的原则哦,到哪儿好像都要认识两个熟人,好像好办事一点儿哦,无论是好办事还是在精神上给个支撑,她好像对于朋友都是很指望的哦。比如你刚才提到的事情,她可能就是把你当作一个朋友,而不是她可以获取某种好处或方便的熟人……It seems that your friend is following the principle that many friends make light work. It seems that she always contacts two acquaintances wherever she goes,and it seems that it would be easier to get things done. Like what you have just mentioned, she might just treat you like a friend, but rather an acquaintance of whom she can take advantage…</td></tr><tr><td>35</td><td>C: 对啊,有了你说的这种可能性之后,我就会好很多啊,我就没有什么了。你刚才还说了什么,¥然后她来就是让我-自卑的吗?¥我觉得肯定不是啊,她肯定没有这一块儿啊……Yeah,with the possibility you&#x27;re talking about,I feel much better,I stopped obsessing over her behaviour,¥what else did you say? And she came here just to make me feel inferior? ¥I don&#x27;t think so,I&#x27;m sure she doesn&#x27;t have that intention…</td></tr></table>

C's anger is triggered by unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport, and her close friend's failure to consider her feelings is the cause of the disharmony. When managing clients' negative emotions, two types of surrogate-based rapport management are involved.

First, T serves as the pseudo-surrogate of C and employs aggressive strategies. The use of inclusive 'we' in Turn 1 shows how T pseudo-surrogates C. T speaks on behalf of C and criticizes C's friend's inappropriate behaviour ('maybe it would be better if she could start talking to us about it directly', Turn 1). Further, T directly speaks as C ('I wasn't being taken seriously', Turn 5) and points out the negative consequences caused by C's friend's inconsideration of C's feelings ('that I was being played with', Turn 5). T's criticism towards C's friend's behaviour is also reflected in the negative meta-pragmatic comments he made (i.e., 'it is inappropriate', Turn 5). C agrees with T, as manifested through her echoing of T's words (Turn 6).

Then, more information concerning C's interaction with her close friend is disclosed, and the details are omitted due to space limitations. Drawing on C's self-disclosure, a meta-pragmatic comment, which targets C's friend's problematic habit of showing up at a gathering, is provided by T ('Why doesn't she always show up!' Turn 30). Upon hearing the negative comment, further information is given by C in Turn 31, dedicated to accounting for her friend's problematic behaviour. C shows affiliation in the next turn.

Based on the shared knowledge that is established, T addresses C's friend in a third singular form and describes her beliefs. By surrogating C's friend and speaking on behalf of her, T justifies her act by claiming "that [the]effect has been misrepresented' (Antaki 1994: 50; also refer to Semin & Manstead 1983), in this way to deny the injury to C. In C's response in the next turn, a verbalized acceptance is stated (Turn 35).

After the account is acknowledged, as shown in C's verbalized acceptance in the next turn, positive changes in C's negative emotion (i.e., in the dimension of emotion valence) are observable. Before the intervention, C's anger and sadness were explicitly communicated. After the enactment of surrogate-based management, the negative emotions fade away, followed by C's positive emotions (as indicated by her laughter in Turn 35).

# 7.3 Managing Guilt and Anger (and Sadness) Through Surrogate-based Rapport Management Strategies

Denial is a speech act that does not 'add new information but retracts information', and has been recognized as having 'a clear corrective function' (Spenader and Maier 2009: 1708). Denials can either be explicitly expressed (by negating the prior speaker's utterance with the negative marker 'no'), or implicitly represented (by making a contrasting statement in response to a prior statement) (Geurts & Maier 2013).

# 7.3.1 Pseudo-surrogating Clients and Using Denial Strategies

When denying is expressed on behalf of clients, therapists intend to express what NPTPs say is not true and/or refuse to admit or accept (Ware & Linkugel 1973; Gordon 2011; Benoit 2015). The following extract demonstrates how therapists perform pseudo-surrogacy and use explicit denials to manage disharmony when managing negative emotions.

Extract 7.11 C feels upset when she reports how she is blamed by her long-term collaborative partner for owing him emotional debts.

<table><tr><td>1</td><td>→</td><td>T:看起来你并不欠他的，It looks like you don&#x27;t owe him anything,</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td></td><td>C:啊?Ah?</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>→</td><td>T:看起来你并不欠他的，只不过他不断在向你灌输让你觉得你是低他一等。It seems that you don&#x27;t owe him anything, it is just that he is constantly trying to convince you that you are inferior.</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td></td><td>C:他会，他最喜欢说的就是对我这么好，其实如果欠的话，他带我入行，我的人生有了一些变化，可能就是这方面有一些，他和我做的，他也有他的目的，我自己也做了我的那个部分。He would, his favourite talk is he be so nice to me if I had owed him, he brought me into this industry, and there were some changes in my life, and maybe there was a change in that, and he did it with me, and he had a purpose, and I did my part.</td></tr></table>

5 T: 你的人生有变化，是谁的功劳？为什么不是你自己的功劳，而是他的功劳？

There has been a change in your life, whose credit, is it? Why is it not your credit, but his credit?

There has been a change in your life, whose credit, is it? Why is it not your credit, but his credit?

C：嗯，我感觉就是他他-引领了一些，其实还是我自己=

Mm, I feel like he has led some, it's actually up to my own=

T: =比如说有一天我们之间的工作有了一定起色，嗯，获得一些成长，你是否也会说那也是我的功劳？

= For example, one day our work has improved, mm, to get some growth, will you also say that it is also my credit?

8 C: 我会想你给我很大帮助。

I would think you were very helpful to me.

9 T：那不是你应得的吗？因为你付了钱嘛！或者如果你自己不

用一些机会找到这里，或者自己去做那个努力，不做一些努力，也不会有那个成长。实际上我们好像挺忽视自己的功劳的。

Don't you deserve that? Because you have paid for it! Or if you don't grasp some opportunities to come here, or do that yourself, without some effort, there will be no growth. We seem to neglect our credit.

10 C: 好像是，嗯。

It seems to be, mm.

11 T: 好像在内心中我们没真正认可过自己，我们一心希望有个更有能力的人在前面带领我们。

It seems that in our hearts we have never really acknowledged ourselves, and we wholeheartedly hope to have a more capable person leading us forward.

12 C:°是。

Yes.

In the extract mentioned above, C perceives herself as owing the long-term collaborative partner emotional debts. This ganqing violation behaviour negatively influences the rapport between them, evoking C's guilt, anger, and sadness. To manage negative emotions, T serves as a pseudo-surrogate of C and speaks on her behalf. The utterance 'you don't owe him anything' (Turn 3) can be viewed as a response towards C's collaborative partner. When pseudo-surrogating clients, the denial strategy is also employed. In Turn 1, T initiates a 'metalinguistic denial' (Horn 1985: 121), which is linguistically realized in the negative sentence, to 'remove previously introduced material from the common ground' (van der Sandt 1991: 1). In so doing, T intends to protect C's self-image. In Turn 2, the interjection ab, being articulated in the rising pitch, is used by C to initiate repair (Ohala 1984; Gussenhoven 2004). In the next turn (Turn 3), T initiates repair by repeating what he uttered previously and adding some extra information. C ratifies T's utterance by providing further disclosure (Pawelczyk 2011: 192) in the following turn.

The sequence continues with T's further intervention. By using rhetorical questions with the illocutionary force of negation (Turn 5), T challenges C's belief and denies that C owes the collaborative partner something. In Turn 6, C responds by adopting T's perspective and recognizing her effort

in her progress ('it's actually up to my own', Turn 6). In another turn, C shows agreement with T's argument by delivering a mitigated agreement and acknowledgement token mm (Turn 10).

In sum, T speaks on behalf of C denies C's reciprocal obligation to the NPTP, and releases her from the accusations. T's intervention brings changes in C's emotional status. (1) Concerning sadness: C talks in a normal pitched voice (Turn 6, 8, 10), compared with her crying sound when she discloses an interaction with the collaborative partner before this extract. (2) Concerning guilt: C recognizes her efforts in growing up and at the same time denies owing the collaborative partner emotional debts. Both of which would help C generate a new 'relational meaning' and 'emotional evaluation' towards herself (Langlotz & Locher 2012: 1603).

In addition to explicit denials, therapists also use implicit denial—making contrastive statements concerning the prior one (Geurts & Maier 2013; Ho 2019) when managing the discordant rapport. The following is an example.

Extract 7.12 Sadness and shame occur when C discloses an interaction with her father. [When C gave her father a birthday gift, she was told by her father that he didn't need anything and that nothing was useful. She felt that she didn't care about him, otherwise, she should know what he needed. Also, C's father accused C of being unfilial and not understanding his real needs.]

1 T：想起我家里哦……(咨询师讲述自己的母亲如何拉拢他们兄弟两个和他的爸爸对抗，以确立自己在家里的位置)。现在呢，我不想和妈妈太亲近，想从她的管控中挣脱出来。可能和爸爸以前太疏远了。现在当我想和爸爸亲近时，妈妈会感到孤立，就会闹腾。结果就是你离开了妈妈，也离开了爸爸，他们俩就是越来越亲密，有老来伴的感觉。

Think of my family... ((T tells how his mother won over T and T's brother and fought against his father to establish her position in the family)). Now, I don't want to be too close to my mother and want to get out of her control. Maybe I was too estranged from my father before. Now when I want to be close to my father, my mother will feel isolated and lose her temper. As a result, I leave my mother and my father. They are getting closer and closer, and they feel like old friends.

2 C: 呵呵。 Interesting.

T：我妈发现，之前她有俩孩子，而且都听她的话。现在就只能依靠那个男人。结果就是孩子成为替罪羊了，他俩开始抱团了。我觉得这没什么伤心的，这正是我想要的。

My mother found that she had two children before, and they all listened to her. Now she can only rely on my father. As a result, the child became the scapegoat, and they began to get together. I don't think it's sad. This is what I want.

4 C: 呵呵，这样也会觉得轻松。Interesting, you will also feel relaxed.

5 T：我妈妈也会做像你爸爸那样的事情，比如你给她买什么东西啊，她也不觉得怎么受用，就觉得怎么做都不对。其实不是怎么做都不对，其实是因为他们想让我们做的事情我们没有做。是他们一种很强大的控制力，好像做这些才开心，其他的做太多也不领情。

My mother does similar things as your father, for example, if I buy something for her, she doesn't think it's helpful, and she thinks it's wrong to do anything. It's not that whatever you do is wrong. It's because we don't do what they expect us to do. It's because of their powerful control. It seems that only by doing what they expect, can they be happy.

6 C: 对…Yes…

T：我们现在背上这样的骂名也是必然的哦。不让他们满意，我们就一定会是被他们指责的哦。可我觉得这种感觉很有指导作用，让我们看清楚他们的行为是什么。

It is inevitable for us to bear such a bad name now. If we do not satisfy them, we will be criticized by them. But I think this feeling is very guiding, allowing us to see clearly what their behaviour is.

8 C: 可是如果说，当我知道这个以后，我可以怎么办？

But if I know this, what can I do?

9 T：我想-可能就是在一定程度上原谅自己……

I think - maybe to some extent...

10 T: 后来我渐渐发现是这样的，如果你对他们特别孝顺的话，比如我一味满足我妈妈意愿的话，他们夫妻感情还会不好的，我也成了一个妈宝男。最后你也会害了妈妈，也害了自己，也害了爸爸。

I gradually realized that if I am very filial to them, for example, if I blindly satisfy my mother's wishes, the relationship between my father and my mother will not be good, and I will become a mummy's boy. Finally, I will harm my mother, my father and myself.

11 C: 对。

Yes.

12 T：对于妈妈而言，可能是痛苦的，但也是成长的。现在无论他们说我是不孝顺什么的，我会觉得很忍辱负重，做了一件伟大的事情。

It may be painful for your mother, but it also brings her growth. However they criticize me for not being filial, I'll feel like I've put up with it a lot, and I'll feel like I've done a great thing.

In this extract, it is C's perception of not fulfilling the filial obligation that has negatively influenced the rapport she has with her father, triggering her negative emotions (guilt and anger at her father). To manage these negative emotions, implicit denial strategies are employed.

First, T serves as the pseudo-surrogate of C (as shown by the use of the inclusive 'we' pronoun) and excuses C from blaming by appealing to 'the misdeeds of another person as a frame for reference for the evaluation of the failure event' (Schönbach 1980: 197). To start with, T discloses an experience similar to C's. By using the narratives or the 'stored account' (Antaki 1988), T intends to indicate that the children are not filial, but the parents have strong control over the children (Turn 5).

Second, T justifies C's unfilial behaviour by appealing to its positive consequence (McLaughlin et al. 1983). He supports his argument by talking about his interaction with his mother. T doesn't obey his mother's wishes and is considered as not filial, however, this failed event has its positive effects—making the relationship between parents closer ('they are getting closer and closer', Turn 1).

Upon hearing T's argument, C shows agreement by making an utterance relevant to the prior one (Davidson 2002) (Turn 4). On this basis, T accounts for the child's seemingly unfilial behaviour by appealing to the misdeeds of the NPTP (Turn 5). C ratifies it with the acknowledgement token 'yes' and by providing further disclosure (Turn 6).

By appealing to the 'positive consequence of the failure event' (Schönbach 1980: 197; Antaki 1988) and the misdeeds of another person in the analysis of the failure event, the obligation-omission behaviour can be reinterpreted. That is, C has done whatever she could do to fulfil her filial obligations. Moreover, these behaviours have positive consequences for her parents. C's affiliative response and the embodiment of positive emotions (i.e., laughter in Turn 2 and Turn 4) show the accounts are honoured by C and it functions to manage C's sadness. Moreover, C's ratification with T's argument implies she has released herself from self-accusation and attributes new emotional evaluations towards herself (Kessler et al. 2010).

# 7.3.2 the Combined Strategy

When therapists are confronted with clients' guilt and anger triggered by clients' interaction with their parents, the combined strategy is adopted.

Extract 7.13 C feels guilty when she discloses the interaction with her parents. She is blamed by her parents as inattentive.

1 T: 可是我们内心也是很脆弱的，承受不了这样的指责，这样的罪名。其实在我们的肩上，其实还-是扛了很多东西，你有没有觉得自己扛得很沉？But we are also very fragile, we cannot withstand such accusations, such charges. In fact, on our shoulders, in fact also -we have carried a lot of things, do you feel that you have carried very heavy things?

2 C: 有时候觉得那啥-自己肩上扛的东西太多了。 Sometimes I feel I have too much on my shoulders.

3 T: 要把自己压垮。可是又不能垮。有什么原因让我们不能垮？
4 Might crush ourselves. But we can't break down. Why? (2.0).

5 T: 如果真的死掉了呢，一了百了呢，会有什么？这个世界有什么让我们留恋的，让我们放不下的东西？If it died, what would happen if it were all over? What makes us linger in this world? What keeps us from letting go?

6 C: 我现在就是觉得，我不知道对于我未来的，就是不知道我人生的价值和意义到底在哪里？

Now I just feel that I don't know my future, just don't know the value and meaning of my life, where do they exactly exist?

Now I just feel that I don't know my future, just don't know the value and meaning of my life, where do they exactly exist?

7 T: 有担心如果自己真的死了以后，父母= Have you ever worried about your parents when you die, your parents=

8 C: =我有想过。

= I've thought about it.

9 T: 他们可能也很难活下去，作为他们唯一的孩子死掉了话，他们可能也真的没有力量活下去。即使你爸爸那么说，死也死在部队里，但是你可能心里会想，如果真死了的话，这种打击对他来说，也是受不了的啊，也是要他命的哦。

If your parents' only child dies, they may hard to live, and they may not have the strength to live. Even if your father says that, if you die, you must die in the army, you may think, if you were dead, this kind of blow is unbearable to him, and it will kill him.

10 C: 嗯。

Mm.

11 T: 就是虽然他们是那么的可恨，但是你不想要他们的命。就是为了他们，你要努力地活下去。哪怕他们对我们的关心不够，也会把很多压力、责任归到我们身上，但是真正这些责任-我们不扛的时候，他们反倒也活不下去哦= Even though they are hateful, you don't want their lives. Just for them, you have to work hard to survive. Even if they do not care enough about us, they will put a lot of pressure, and responsibility on us, but when we don't carry these responsibilities, they can't live =

12 C: =嗯。

$= \mathrm{Mm}$

13 T: 有时候会不会觉得其实是在为他们而活着，即使自己想放弃，也不允许，也不能放弃。不管怎么说，还是爱着父母的哦，并没有说要想把他们，通过让自己不活了，让自己去死了，让这种方式，去惩罚他们哦。有的时候那种憋屈，那种压在心里的那种憋屈的东西，发不出去。其实我们也是在保护他们，保护他们，好像他们也挺脆弱的，他们也受不了我们的这种指责，你能感觉到他们的脆弱吗，感觉不到？只是觉得自己挺脆弱的哦。

Sometimes you think that you are living for them, even if you want to give up, but we cannot give up. Anyway, we still love our parents, we don't punish them by ending ourselves. Sometimes this kind of oppression, that kind of oppressed thing in our heart, cannot be released. We are protecting them, protecting them. It seems that they are also very vulnerable. They can't bear our accusations. Can you feel their vulnerability. Can't you feel it? Just feel we are vulnerable.

14 C: 嗯。我以前可能很少想到-这些。

Mm. I probably didn't think about these before.

15 T：当时可能也顾不了那么多了啊，压得太难受了。

I could not have taken care of that much at that time, the pressure was too oppressive.

16 C: 是啊, 就是以前的时候……(进一步的讲述))

Yeah, that's when... ((providing further self-disclosure))

In the above interaction, C's guilt and anger toward her parents are evoked by the unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport, and C's filial obligation omission behaviour is the cause of that disharmony. When managing C's negative emotions, T acts as C in a 'pseudo' way and uses implicit denial strategies (i.e., asserting C's fulfilment of the filial obligation to the parents). Such an intervention is achieved step by step.

To start with, the pre-sequences (i.e., questions asked by T) are constructed to establish a common ground on which both parties can align and prepare for T's further delivery of information. By asking questions (Turn 1, 3, 5, 7), T asks C to talk about why she persists though she feels very tired and depressed. The questions are designed to elicit the preferred answers from C. When T doesn't receive the desired answer, two  $wh$ -questions (Turn 5), and a yes-no question with a hint answer (hinting C to consider from the perspective of parents, Turn 7), are initiated. In so doing, T aims to elicit a preferred response from C (Koshik 2005: 101). Additionally, when T constructs shared knowledge between him and C, the inclusive 'we' pronoun is adopted, showing how T pseudo-surrogates C and speaks on her behalf (Mazer et al. 2014).

T's surrogate role is further reflected in the later turns. In addition to using the inclusive 'we' pronoun when commenting on C's behaviour, T speaks as if he were C, as reflected in the utterance 'even if we want to give up, but we cannot give up. In any case, we still love our parents' (Turn 13). Also, T asserts C's fulfilment of the filial obligation on her behalf. Specifically, C is asserted as a person who strives for life for the sake of her parents ('just for them, you have to work hard to survive'), a person who loves her parents ('we still love our parents'), a person who is always protecting her parents ('we are protecting them, protecting them'). With these arguments, C is positioned as a person who has fulfilled the filial obligations: to show affection and concern to her parents, and to take the responsibility of protecting the parents (Lai 2006; Hwang 1987, 1999).

Besides pseudo-surrogating C and using a denial strategy, T simultaneously surrogates C's parents and employs an accounting strategy. In Turn 13, T describes C's parents' situation and feelings (Mazer et al. 2014: 39) ('it seems that they are also very vulnerable'). In this way, T accounts for the parent's inappropriate behaviour.

As for C's response, she confirms T's argument minimally (mm, Turn 14 and 16) and shows her acceptance by producing an utterance relevant to the prior one (Davidson 2002) ('I probably didn't think about these before'). Later, C gives further self-disclosure on what she has done for the parents. This points to C's new understanding of herself (i.e., she is a filial child) and new emotional evaluations towards herself (i.e., compared with the emotional evaluation of guilt before the therapist's intervention).

Extract 7.14 C is a young man who is always blamed by his mother as inattentive to the well-being of his parents and the family.

1 T：你说母亲为什么会说那样的话，她真是那么想的吗？她总是反复唠叨这个话，她能够得到什么东西，能够满足她什么需求？

Can you explain why mother says that? Is that really what she thinks? She always keeps repeating this sentence, what can she get and what kind of her needs can be met?

2 C: 以前没想。现在想她不能承担自己的痛苦，而要把这种痛苦给别人，°都是你干的，或者是( )°

I haven't thought about that before. Now I think that's because she can't bear her pain and wants to give it to others, or all is done by you, or ( )°

3 T: 所以就是一个控制人非常好的方式。在人的内心中下了一个蛊一样，只要念咒语，就可以任意摆布。她就是在我们心里下蛊了，内疚感就像一个虫子在我们心里翻腾。她只要一念咒，我们就开始疼。

So, it's a very good way to control people. Just like having placed a legendary venomous insect in our hearts, if she says the mantra, then we are controlled by her. She has placed that kind of insect in our hearts, the feeling of guilt is like a worm that writhes in our hearts. If she says the mantra, we hurt.

4 C: 一遇到这些事情，就对我起作用了。

As soon as these things happen, they will affect me.

5 T: 嗯。

Mm.

6 C: 现在我做啥事前儿，我都会想，她会不会同意，我现在成为她的傀儡(她的思想了)()((叹气))。

Whatever I do now, I think about whether she would agree  $^\circ$  I am now her puppet (her thoughts) ((sighing))

T: 其实她之所以能够控制我们，也是我们心疼她哦。因为无论怎样，在我们内心中我们都希望她过得好，我们希望能够帮她分担她的难受。她之所以这样，也可能是她确实过得不太好，我们不想再让她雪上加霜。似乎我们迁就，迎合顺从她，她能够开心一点的话，我们宁可遭这个罪。似乎今天遭的罪都是让她开心，让她满意哦。

The reason why she can control us is because we love her. Because whatever it is, deep down, we all hope that she has a good life, we hope to share her pain. The reason why she is like this may also be that she has led a bad life. We don't want to make it even worse for her. It seems that we accommodate and obey her, and if she can be happy, we would rather suffer this. It seems like all we've suffered today is to make her happy, to satisfy her.

8 C: 但是()

°But( )°

9 T: 这也是我们-我们实际上一直在迁就顾虑她的感受，但是我们的感受从来没有人顾虑，这就很苦闷哦！

This is also us - we have been accommodating and worrying about her feelings, but our feelings have never been concerned, which is very depressing!

10 C: 嗯。

Mm.

<table><tr><td>11</td><td>→</td><td>T: 好像又放不下顾虑她，她的影响又拿不掉，我
    觉得是一种不忍心，不忍心摘掉她对我们的
    影响……只能压抑这个自我需求，只能去顺从父母的需求……
It seems that we can&#x27;t let go of worrying about her, and her influence can&#x27;t be taken away. I think it&#x27;s a kind of unwillingness. we can&#x27;t bear to take off her influence on us... we can only suppress this self-demand, and we can only comply with our parents&#x27; demand…</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>C:</td><td>↑对对对，她不满意，我努力让她满意。
    ↑Yes, yes, yes, she&#x27;s not satisfied, I&#x27;m trying to satisfy her.</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>T:</td><td>对。
Yes.</td></tr></table>

In this extract, the filial obligation-omission behaviour threatens the rapport between C and his mother, triggering C's self-conscious emotion (guilt) and other-condemning emotion(anger). T uses surrogate-based denial strategies to manage these negative emotions. Specifically, T addresses C's mother directly as 'mother' rather than 'your mother' ('Can you explain why mother says that', Turn 1). The denial strategy is achieved over a series of turns.

First, T establishes shared knowledge with C that his mother intentionally blamers her son, but not necessarily the fact that C fails to fulfil his filial obligation. By asking an affirmative question ('she always keeps repeating this sentence, what can she get and what needs can she meet?', Turn 1), T implies that C's mother intends to blame her son, not necessarily that he fails to fulfil his responsibility to take care of his family. In the next turn, C provides a relevant answer, showing his ratification of the prior utterance (Davidson 2002). Then, T initiates a 'so'-prefaced utterance, building his comments as an extension of C's Turn (Turn 8). C acknowledges T's argument by providing further disclosure (Pawelczyk 2011) in the next turn. C's acknowledgement indicates the establishment of mutual understanding between the two parties—though C is always blamed by his mother for not fulfilling the filial obligation, this is not necessarily the case. His mother has her underlying reasons for complaining about him.

In the following turns, T asserts that C has fulfilled his filial obligations to his parents. C is positioned as a son who cares about his mother ('the reason why she can control us is because we love her', Turn 7); a son who always tries to please his mother, even when he is suffering, and who is always obedient to his parent. In Turn 17, C ratifies T's argument by using the acknowledgement tokens and building his utterance as an extension of the prior utterance (Vehviläinen 2008: 130).

The enactment of surrogate-based rapport management has witnessed changes in C's emotional status. His acceptance and acknowledgement of his effort in caring about her mother (Turn 12, Turn 10) implicate new relational meaning and emotional evaluations. That is, he could be released from the guilt he held before the intervention (Langlotz and Locher 2012;

Kessler et al. 2010). By analyzing this extract, the client's negative emotions (guilt and anger towards NPTPs), which have been created by a threat to rapport because of ganqing obligation-omission behaviour, can be managed by employing the denial strategies and the accounting strategies, both of which are based on the respective surrogate roles performed by T.

# 7.4 Conclusion

This chapter lays out how therapists use surrogate-based rapport management strategies to manage clients' negative emotions (cf. Figure 7.1).

Figure 7.1 Surrogate-based rapport management strategies for managing negative emotions.

Anger/anger and sadness are managed by three types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies: (1) surrogating NPTPs and using the accounting strategies; (2) pseudo-surrogating clients and using the aggressive strategies; and (3) the combined strategy (pseudo-surrogating clients and surrogating NPTPs respectively, whilst using the aggressive strategy and the accounting strategy respectively).

Guilt and anger (and sadness) are managed by two types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies: (1) pseudo-surrogating clients and using the denial strategies; and (2) the combined strategy (pseudo-surrogating clients and surrogating NPTPs respectively, whilst using the denial strategy and the accounting strategy respectively).

To facilitate the discussions to be proceeded in Chapter 8, 'surrogating NPTPs and using accounting strategies' is shortly expressed as 'surrogate-based accounting strategies'; 'pseudo-surrogating clients and using aggressive strategies' is shortly expressed as 'surrogate-based aggressive strategies', and 'pseudo-surrogating clients and using denial strategies' is shortly expressed as 'surrogate-based denial strategies'.

Considering the surrogate-based rapport management strategies in terms of the relationship type between clients and NPTPs, it is found that: (1) the accounting strategies are mainly found in therapists' management of clients' negative emotions caused by an interaction with their parents, or couples; it is also found in clients' interactions with close friends; (2) the aggressive strategies are mainly found in therapists' management of clients' negative emotions caused by an interaction with a long-term collaborative partner or parents; (3) the denial strategies are mainly found in therapists' management of clients' negative emotions caused by interactions with parents and with long-term collaborative partners; (4) the combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs successively and using aggressiveaccounting strategies) is found to be used in therapists' management of clients' negative emotions caused by interactions with parents and with long-term collaborative partners; and (5) the combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs and using denial and accounting strategies) is mainly found in therapists' management of clients' negative emotions caused by interactions with parents.

Regarding the effect of therapists' intervention (i.e., surrogate-based rapport management) on clients' negative emotions, the following conclusions can be drawn: (1) much of the intervention has witnessed the reduction of clients' anger/sadness. For instance, a reduction of intensity (cf. Extract 7-1; Extract 7-3), or a reduction of intensity and the change of valence (i.e., from negative to positive) (cf. Extract 7-2; Extract 7-4), or a reducing in intensity and persistence, as in Extract 7-5; Extract 7-6; Extract 7-9; Extract 7-10; Extract 7-11; Extract 7-12. (2) The majority of the intervention has witnessed the reduction of clients' shame/guilt

(cf. Extract 7-11 to 6-14). (3) The effect of the intervention can also be reflected in the enhancement of clients' anger and sadness, for instance, as in Extract 7-13, and Extract 7-14. The analysis shows how emotion management practices function to maintain and enhance emotional status, which is also a type of emotion management (Thompson 1994: 28). This argument can also be justified by the post-interview data. When therapists are asked to comment on the ways to observe the effect of their efforts in managing negative emotions, one of the therapists notes as follows:

In the beginning, clients' emotions are trapped. But later, he may make some associations, and he may think about the parents, this is a kind of progress. At first, the mind is exhausted, and static, while later, in the mind there is association, description, his emotion shows, this is a positive change.

(From therapist 1)

Additionally, the comments from therapists also show their effort in managing perceptions of relationships when they are confronted with clients' negative emotions.

The clients tend to be rigid and inflexible in their thinking. Emotional expression is related to cognitive expression. The consultation process is about presenting clients with a new perspective on the problem, such as a new perspective to think about the relationship, or a perspective to look at themselves in the relationship. [italics are added]

(From therapist 3)

These people tend to be negative, so in the counselling process, I sometimes guide them to look at relationships and things more positively. This process is more about leading them to think, and following them in the process, rather than imposing opinions on them.

(From therapist 2)

# Note

1 Compare T's voice in other turns (e.g., turns 10 and 12) where T serves as the therapist and speaks in a normal tone of voice.

# 8 Accounting for Negative Emotion Management Through Rapport Management Strategies

# 8.1 Introduction

In Chapter 6, the types of negative emotions clients display when they disclose ganqing violation-induced discordant rapport (hereafter GQVI discordant rapport) are discussed. In Chapter 7, different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies employed by therapists to manage negative emotions are examined. The question thus becomes: why do therapists deploy different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies to manage negative emotions? Is there any factor that influences therapists' choice of surrogate-based rapport management strategies? A close analysis of different types of strategies finds that they can be categorized into three major types: (1) discordant rapport-challenge orientated strategies; (2) discordant rapport-maintenance orientated strategies; and (3) discordant rapport-neglect orientated strategies. Based on this categorization, the chapter focuses on the factor that influences therapists' choice of strategies.

# 8.2 Recategorizing Surrogate-based Rapport Management Strategies Concerning Rapport Orientations

Since the rapport orientations in Spencer-Oatey's RMF concern the harmonious relations between interlocutors and that there are also the disharmonious relations to deal with under some circumstances, together with the viewpoint that 'one can promote, maintain, or threaten both harmonious or discordant relationships' (Lauriks et al. 2015: 24), this study thus contends that the speakers can hold any of the following types of discordant rapport orientations when it is in their best interest to do so. In Table 8.1, 'Ts' refers to therapists, 'Cs' refers to clients, and 'NPTPs' refers to non-present third parties.

Based on the rapport orientations proposed above, surrogate-based rapport management strategies can be re-categorized concerning rapport orientations.

Table 8.1 Three types of discordant rapport orientations

<table><tr><td>Discordant rapport orientation1</td><td>Description</td></tr><tr><td>Discordant rapport challenge</td><td>Ts attempt to challenge the disharmony that exists between Cs and NPTPs. Ts say something advantageous for the relationship between Cs and NPTPs (e.g., account for NPTPs&#x27; lack of feelings when interacting with Cs and thus account for NPTPs&#x27; ganqing violation behaviour)</td></tr><tr><td>Discordant rapport neglect</td><td>Ts are indifferent to the quality of the relationship between Cs and NPTPs (e.g., because of a focus on Cs&#x27;s self-images)</td></tr><tr><td>Discordant rapport maintenance</td><td>Ts utter something that aims to maintain the current quality of the relationship and level of disharmony between Cs and NPTPs (e.g., criticizing NPTPs&#x27; ganqing behaviour)</td></tr></table>

<sup>1</sup> The proposal of 'discordant rapport orientation' is based on (1) Lauriks et al. (2015) notion of 'discordant rapport' and the relevant discussions on the management of discordant rapport; and (2) Spencer-Oatey's (2008: 31-33) argument on rapport orientations. Moreover, four types of rapport orientations are discussed in Spencer-Oatey's framework; this study lists three relevant types given the research focus.

(1) Surrogate-based accounting strategies are discordant rapport-challenge orientated. As laid out in Chapter 7, surrogate-based accounting strategies are the major practices adopted by therapists when they are confronted with clients' anger/anger and sadness. Taking the triggers of these negative emotions into consideration, this type of strategy is used to manage negative emotions caused by unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport.

When implementing surrogate-based accounting strategies, therapists speak for or on behalf of NPTPs (Coupland & Coupland 2001; Halkowski 2011; Mazer et al. 2014; Fioramonte & Vásquez 2019) and help them to deny full responsibility (Austin 1961; Goffman 1971; Buttny 1993) for their gan- qing violation behaviour. The surrogate-based accounting strategies function to 'bridge the gap between action and expectations' (Scott & Lyman 1968: 46). The success of an account is evaluated in terms of whether it is accepted or acknowledged by the offended party (Blumstein et al. 1974; Read 1992). The acceptance of an excuse indicates the restoration of the social order or 'ritual equilibrium' (Goffman 1971: 171-202). Drawing on the discursive approach, clients' acceptance of the accounts offered by therapists they do on behalf of NPTPs) is observable, as displayed through their ratification of therapists' utterances in the next turns.

To sum up, the surrogate-based accounting strategies challenge the discordant relationship between clients and NPTPs and restore the harmonious relations between the two parties (Lauriks et al. 2015). That is, the surrogate-based accounting strategies are discordant rapport-challenge orientated.

(2) Surrogate-based aggressive strategies are discordant rapport-maintenance orientated. Apart from surrogate-based accounting strategies, surrogate-based aggressive strategies are also deployed to manage clients' negative emotions caused by unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport. Rather than taking the surrogate roles and speaking for/on behalf of clients, therapists pseudo-surrogate clients and adopt a common position as clients (Hasselkus 1992; Coupland & Coupland 2001), resulting in NPTPs being complained about.

Criticizing NPTPs' ganqing violation behaviour, accusing NPTPs' of ganqing violation behaviour, and abusing NPTPs' ganqing violation behaviour are three commonly used aggressive strategies. By employing these strategies, therapists highlight or emphasize NPTPs' inappropriate behaviour and the infringement of clients' association rights, thereby maintaining the current quality of the relationship and the level of disharmony between clients and NPTPs.

(3) A combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs successively and using aggressive accounting strategies) is discordant rapport-challenge orientated. Under some circumstances, therapists first pseudo-surrogate clients and adopt aggressive strategies. If this strategy is not effective, therapists would then surrogate NPTPs and adopt the accounting strategies. Therapists take a discordant rapport maintenance orientation at first; however, they challenge the disharmonious relations between the two parties afterwards. Thus, the combined strategy can be understood as discordant rapport-challenge orientated.

(4) Surrogate-based denial strategies are discordant rapport-neglect orientated. This type of strategy differs from the two strategies mentioned above because it is used to manage negative emotions caused by unfulfilled ganqing obligation-induced discordant rapport. When enacting this type of strategy, therapists pseudo-surrogate clients and use explicit denials (e.g., negating clients' obligation to reciprocate) or implicit denials (e.g., asserting clients' fulfilment of reciprocal obligations). During this process, therapists are concerned little about the quality of the relationship between clients and NPTPs but pay great attention to clients' self-images. Because of NPTPs' accusations, for instance, clients are blamed for caring little about their parents, who fail to reciprocate the favour from friends, etc. Surrogate-based denial

strategies function to change clients' prior perception about themselves or reduce their responsibility for the failure event. Clients' responses in the next turns indicate the acceptance of the therapists' utterances and the recovery of the damaged self-images. To sum up, then, surrogate-based denial strategies lack concern or interest in the disharmonious relations between clients and NPTPs. This means they are discordant rapport-neglect orientated.

(5) The combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs and using denial and accounting strategies) is discordant rapport-challenge orientated. Occasionally, therapists pseudo-surrogate clients and use denial strategies. Moreover, they speak for NPTPs and use excuses or justifications to account for NPTPs' ganqing violation behaviour.

In so doing, therapists bridge the gap between what has been expected and the actual behaviour. Specifically, the denial strategy bridges the gap between the NPTP's expectations concerning the relational principle of ganqing and the behaviour, while the accounting strategy bridges the gap between clients' expectation according to the relational principle of ganqing and NPTPs' behaviour. Understanding it from this perspective, the combined strategy is discordant rapport-challenge orientated. Different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies that are used to manage clients' negative emotions are presented in Table 8.2.

# 8.3 Variation in Surrogate-based Rapport Management in Terms of Social Distance

People's perceptions of social distance/relational intimacy/familiarity vary from one to another (Holmes 1990; Holtgraves 1992). For instance,

Table 8.2 Surrogate-based rapport management strategies in terms of rapport orientations

<table><tr><td>Rapport orientations</td><td>Surrogate-based rapport management strategies</td></tr><tr><td>Discordant rapport-challenge orientated</td><td>• Surrogate-based accounting strategies
• The combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs successively and using aggressive accounting strategies)
• The combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs and using denial and accounting strategies)</td></tr><tr><td>Discordant rapport-maintenance orientated</td><td>• Surrogate-based aggressive strategies</td></tr><tr><td>Discordant rapport-neglect orientated</td><td>• Surrogate-based denial strategies</td></tr></table>

Blum-Kulka et al. (1985: 123) claim that the relationship between 'members of a nuclear family' (e.g., parent-child or between couples) is located at the 'high' end of the social distance continuum, while the relationship between 'friends and non-nuclear kin' is located at the 'medium' end of the social distance continuum. Similarly, Holmes (1990) classifies the relationship between spouses and family members as close and the relationship between distant acquaintances as distant, while the social relations between friends and colleagues are located between the 'close' and 'distant' ends.

This study, inspired by Blum-Kulka et al. (1985) and Holmes (1990), distinguishes two types of relative social distance (italics added) between clients and the NPTP: (1) a higher degree of intimacy, which involves the relationship between a child and a parent, and within couples; and (2) a lower degree of intimacy, which involves the relationship between close friends (e.g., boyfriend-girlfriend; between bosom friends) and long-term collaborative partners. Based on these, the relative social distance between clients and NPTPs is laid out in Figure 8.1.

Second, as mentioned in Chapter 5, altogether 30 extracts that concern emotion management through managing GQVI discordant rapport have been identified. This section starts with a description of surrogate-based rapport management strategies employed by therapists to manage clients' negative emotions.

In Table 8.3, the types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies, and the distribution of strategies in terms of relationship dyads (clients' relationship with NPTPs is featured as a child-parent dyad or wife-husband dyad), are laid out. The relationship dyads, include: (1) parent-child; (2) husband-wife; (3) bosom friends; (4) boyfriend-girlfriend; and (5) long-term collaborative partners. Though these five types of relationship dyads are different, they are featured as long-standing and intimate in Chinese interpersonal communication (Gao & Ting-Toomey 1998; Zhu 2015). Accordingly, the relational principle of ganqing underpins the interaction between these relationship dyads (Yang 1994; Chang 2013, 2018).

Figure 8.1 Continuum of social distance between the Cs and NPTPs.

Table 8.3 Distribution of surrogate-based rapport management strategies

<table><tr><td rowspan="3">Types of strategies</td><td colspan="5">Distribution of strategies in terms of relationship dyad</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">Direct family members</td><td colspan="3">Friends and collaborative partners</td></tr><tr><td>Child-parent</td><td>Husband-wife</td><td>Between bosom friends</td><td>Boyfriend-girlfriend</td><td>Long-term collaborative partners</td></tr><tr><td>Surrogate-based accounting strategies</td><td>5</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Surrogate-based aggressive strategies</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td>4</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><td>The combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs successively and using aggressive accounting strategies)</td><td>1</td><td></td><td>2</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Surrogate-based denial strategies</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>The combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs and using denial and accounting strategies)</td><td>3</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

# 8.3.1 Higher Degree of Intimacy: Discordant Rapport-challenge Orientated Strategies

As shown in Figure 8.1, when clients' relationships with NPTPs feature a higher degree of intimacy—meaning that it is clients' interactions with their parents or their romantic partners that have triggered their negative emotions (anger, anger, and sadness, guilt, and anger)—it is much more likely that therapists will adopt discordant rapport-challenge orientated strategies (amounting to  $73\%$ ). Surrogate-based accounting strategies cover  $47\%$ , the combined strategy (serving two surrogate roles and using aggressive accounting strategies) amounts to  $6.7\%$ , and the combined strategy (serving two different surrogate roles and using denial and accounting strategies) covers  $20\%$ . Moreover, discordant rapport-maintenance orientated strategies cover  $20\%$  and discordant rapport-neglect orientated strategies cover  $7\%$ . Based on this, it tentatively concluded that if clients' interactions with NPTPs are characterized as having a higher degree of intimacy, it is more likely that therapists will employ discordant rapport-challenge orientated strategies to manage clients' negative emotions.

# 8.3.2 Lower Degree of Intimacy: Discordant Rapport-maintenance Orientated Strategies

According to Table 8.1, when clients' relationships with NPTPs are characterized as having a lower degree of intimacy—for instance, if it is the client's interaction with her boyfriend that has evoked her negative emotions—it is much more likely that the therapist will adopt discordant rapport-maintenance orientated strategies (amounting to  $53\%$ ). Further, the discordant rapport-neglect orientated strategies cover  $14\%$ , and the discordant rapport-challenge orientated strategies amount to  $33\%$ . One possible explanation for therapists' deployment of discordant rapport-challenge orientated strategies in this relationship dyad is that for good friends who have known each other for a long time and who become very close, people consider them as 'members of the family' (Peng you ru jia ren) (Gao and Ting-Toomey 1998: 14).

If clients' interactions with NPTPs are characterized as having a lower degree of intimacy (e.g., between long-term collaborative partners, or between boyfriend and girlfriend), it is more likely that therapists will deploy discordant rapport-maintenance orientated strategies to manage clients' negative emotions. Based on what has been discussed in Section 8.3, Figure 8.2 is drawn to show the influence of social distance on therapists' choice of surrogate-based rapport management strategies when managing clients' negative emotions.

Figure 8.2 Influence of social distance (between Cs and NPTPs) on surrogate-based rapport management strategies.

# 8.4 Conclusion

This chapter accounts for therapists' employment of different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies when managing negative emotions. To achieve this, these strategies discussed in the previous chapter are re-categorized according to the rapport orientation each type of strategy falls into. Specifically, (1) surrogate-based accounting strategies and the combined strategy are discordant rapport-challenge orientated; (2) surrogate-based aggressive strategies are discordant rapport-maintenance orientated; (3) surrogate-based denial strategies are discordant rapport-neglect orientated; (4) the combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs successively and using aggressive accounting strategies) is discordant rapport-challenge orientated; and (5) the combined strategy (surrogating clients and NPTPs and using denial and accounting strategies) is discordant rapport-challenge orientated. Then, the statistical data is presented to show the distribution of surrogate-based rapport management strategies in terms of the relationship type.

The underlying factor that influences therapists' choice of different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies in managing negative emotions has been identified. The social distance between clients and NPTPs is a key factor that mandates the language use of therapists. On the one hand, if clients' relationships with NPTPs feature higher intimacy—that is, if clients' negative emotions are caused by clients' interactions with their parents or romantic partners—it is more likely that therapists would employ discordant rapport-challenge orientated strategies, which aim to restore the harmonious relationship between the two parties and then manage clients' negative emotions accordingly. On the other hand, if clients' relationships with NPTPs feature a lower degree of intimacy, it is more likely that therapists will deploy discordant rapport-maintenance orientated strategies. Occasionally (e.g., in the case of negative emotions resulting from the disharmonious interaction clients have with the long-term collaborative partners), discordant rapport-neglect orientated strategies will be adopted.
