# 9 Conclusion

# 9.1 Major Research Findings

This study has firstly analyzed the types of negative emotions clients display when they disclose the ganqing violation-induced discordant rapport (hereafter GQVI discordant rapport) that exists between them and NPTPs. Then, the types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies deployed to manage these negative emotions are explored. Thereafter, therapists' reasons for using different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies to manage negative emotions are accounted for.

# 9.1.1 Types of Negative Emotions

When clients disclose the GQVI discordant rapport that exists between them and NTPPs, two major types of negative emotions are displayed:

1 Anger/anger and sadness. This category of negative emotions is displayed when clients disclose the unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport that exists between them and the guanxi members (i.e., NPTPs). The guanxi members' (i.e., clients' parents, couples, close friends, and long-term collaborative partners) lack of feelings towards clients and the guanxi members' emotional detachment from clients are rapport threats, resulting in clients' perceptions of GQVI discordant rapport and evoking clients' anger/anger and sadness.

2 Guilt and anger (and sadness). The category of negative emotions displayed when clients disclose the unfulfilled ganqing obligation-induced discordant rapport that exists between them and the guanxi members (i.e., the client's parent and long-term collaborative partners). Clients' filial obligation omission behaviour and failure to return the emotional debt are the rapport threats, evoking clients' perceptions of GQVI discordant rapport and triggering clients' guilt and anger (and sadness).

When clients display anger and sadness, guilt, and anger (and sadness), they are entangled in 'emotional interactional chains' (Langlotz & Locher 2017: 310). Sadness is evoked by anger, while anger (and sadness) is triggered by guilt. That is to say, unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport tends to evoke anger and anger-related emotions, while unfulfilled ganqing obligation-induced discordant rapport tends to trigger guilt and guilt-related emotions.

# 9.1.2 Expressions of Negative Emotions

The ways that negative emotions are expressed are closely related to their categories. Anger is the type of negative emotion that is most frequently observed, which occurs when clients are disclosing unmet ganqing expectations or unfulfilled ganqing obligations. Anger is displayed verbally through the speech acts of complaints, and prosodically with a high-pitch register. Clients rarely name their anger.

Sadness is expressed when clients talk about the unmet ganqing expectations or unfulfilled ganqing obligations. Similar to anger, sadness is seldom named by clients. Few clients describe their sadness with emotional words such as sad or hurt. More frequently, sadness is displayed vocally and prosodically through crying sounds and sobbing. Clients mainly express guilt by employing emotional description or implication. As for the former, clients describe the wrongful acts and take responsibility, while in the latter case, guilt is implied through lexical connotations or conceptual implications. Occasionally, clients explicitly name guilt.

Concerning the emotional displays, it can be summarized that, to a large extent, clients display their negative emotions implicitly (e.g., through the speech acts of complaints or the implications), which reflects the influence of a high-context communication culture on the emotional expression of Chinese speakers (Hall 1976; Kim et al. 1998). Negative emotions are more frequently indicated and demonstrated than explicitly described.

# 9.1.3 Managing Negative Emotions Through Surrogate-based Rapport Management

Given it is the GQVI discordant rapport between clients and NPTPs that has caused clients' negative emotions, and based also on the premise that people's perception of relational disharmony/harmony is a dynamic construct (Spencer-Oatey 2005; Spencer-Oatey & Franklin 2009), it is therefore maintained that clients' negative emotions can be managed by managing the GQVI discordant rapport between clients and NPTPs. Data analysis finds that therapists employ five types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies to manage clients' negative emotions. Moreover,

Figure 9.1 An integrated model of rapport-orientated negative emotion management.

a rapport-orientated negative emotion management model is proposed to account for the interactive process of emotion management.

Concerning Figure 9.1, the following aspects are worth noting. First, the figure depicts two key stages when therapists manage clients' negative emotions. (1) Stage one (the process as reflected through single lines) illustrates the point that clients' perception of GQVI discordant rapport results in their negative emotions. When therapists are confronted with negative emotions (e.g., anger, anger and sadness) that arise out of the social interaction with NPTPs, they are obliged to take some actions to change the status of interpersonal relations to act upon these negative emotions. Then it comes to (2) stage two (the process as reflected in the double lines). During this stage, therapists pseudo-surrogate clients or surrogate NPTPs

to manage the relational disharmony between clients and NPTPs. Based on respective surrogate roles, different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies are employed to manage the GQVI discordant rapport. The management of relational disharmony between clients and NPTPs acts on clients' negative emotions. Thus, the whole process<sup>1</sup> has witnessed how negative emotions are triggered by GQVI discordant rapport, and how these negative emotions can be managed through managing the discordant rapport. Specifically, the following five types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies are employed by therapists to manage clients' negative emotions.

(1) Surrogate-based accounting strategies. To manage clients' anger at the NPTPs/anger at NPTPs and sadness, both of which are negative emotions caused by unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport, therapists surrogate NPTPs and speak on their behalf. Drawing on such a discourse role (Sarangi 2010), therapists employ accounting strategies to manage the relational disharmony between clients and NPTPs. In terms of linguistic realizations, the accounts are realized through excuses/justifications. For instance, excusing NPTPs' ganqing violation behaviour by appealing to pressure from third parties, by appealing to their need for face maintenance, etc. In so doing, NPTPs' ganqing violation behaviour that has caused the discordant rapport is explained. From clients' responses in the next turns (e.g., the acknowledgement tokens or other ways of showing recognition), it is found that the gap between clients' ganqing expectations and NPTPs' acts is bridged.

(2) Surrogate-based aggressive strategies. To manage clients' anger at the NPTPs/anger at NPTPs and sadness, both types of negative emotions evoked by unmet ganqing expectation-induced discordant rapport, therapists pseudo-surrogate clients and speak as or for them. Aggressive strategies are linguistically realized through criticisms, abuses, or accusations. In this way, the disharmony between clients and NPTPs is maintained. The emotional cues clients display after the intervention (e.g., an increased emotional intensity which is indicated through a higher pitch register and faster tempo) show that surrogate-based aggressive strategies are effective in managing negative emotions.

(3) Surrogate-based denial strategies. To manage clients' guilt and anger at the NPTPs, the types of negative emotions that are caused by unfulfilled ganqing obligation-induced discordant rapport, therapists serve as pseudo-surrogates of clients and speak as or for them. When implementing this type of strategy, therapists show little concern for the relational disharmony between clients and NPTPs but dedicate it to

restoring clients' damaged self-images. From clients' responses in the next turns (e.g., the fading away of negative emotions), it is observable that this type of strategy is effective in managing clients' negative emotions.

(4) The combined strategy<sup>1</sup> (i.e., surrogating clients and NPTPs successively and using aggressiveaccounting strategies). Under some circumstances (e.g., if clients' anger at NPTPs/anger at NPTPs and sadness are very strong), therapists would first serve as pseudo-surrogates of clients and employ aggressive strategies. If the intended effect is not achieved, therapists would surrogate NPTPs and deploy the accounting strategies.

(5) The combined strategy $^2$  (i.e., surrogating clients and NPTPs and using denial and accounting strategies). If clients' guilt and anger at NPTPs are caused by clients' interactions with their parents, it is probable that therapists may serve as two types of surrogate roles simultaneously (i.e., as pseudo-surrogates of clients and surrogates of NPTPs) and adopt denial strategies and accounting strategies. In so doing, therapists on the one hand bridge the gap between what has been expected by NPTPs and what has been done by clients (i.e., by using denial strategies), and on the other bridge the gap between what has been expected by clients and what has performed by NPTPs (i.e., through accounting NPTPs' inappropriate behaviour).

# 9.1.4 Social Distance as a Key Factor Affecting Surrogate-based Rapport Management

Based on the types of negative emotions clients display when they disclose the GQVI discordant rapport between clients and NPTPs, as well as the different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies deployed by therapists to manage these negative emotions, the underlying factor that influences therapists' choice of different types of surrogate-based management strategies need to be further explored.

The question is explored following several steps. First, it is argued that rapport orientation, discordant rapport orientation more specifically, offers a theoretical construct upon which different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies can be systematically categorized. Drawing on Spencer-Oatey's (2000:29-31) research on four types of rapport orientations—namely rapport enhancement orientation, rapport maintenance orientation, rapport challenge orientation, and rapport neglect orientation—and also on the fact it is discordant rapport rather than harmony that therapists are managing, three types of discordant rapport orientation have been put forward: (1) the discordant rapport-maintenance orientation, (2) the discordant rapport-challenge orientation, and (3) the

discordant rapport-neglect orientation. Based on such a theoretical construct, the discordant rapport orientations of the five types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies can be identified.

Then, the analysis moves on to explore how different types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies are distributed concerning the relationship between clients and NPTPs (e.g., a child-parent dyad or wife-husband dyad). Data analysis reveals that the social distance between clients and NPTPs is a key factor that influences therapists' choice of surrogate-based rapport management strategies. (1) If clients' relationships with NPTPs are characterized as having a higher degree of intimacy, or, put in another way, if it is clients' interactions with their parents or with their romantic partners that have evoked their negative emotions, it seems that therapists will deploy discordant rapport-challenge orientated strategies when managing clients' negative emotions. (2) If clients' relationships with NPTPs fall within a lower degree of intimacy—if, for instance, it is clients' interactions with their long-term collaborative partners that have triggered their negative emotions—therapists tend to use discordant maintenance orientated strategies to manage the discordant rapport when managing clients' negative emotions.

Summing up, social distance (whether clients' relationships with NPTPs are characterized as having a higher or lower degree of intimacy) greatly influences a therapist's choice of surrogate-based rapport management when managing clients' negative emotions.

# 9.2 Further Theoretical, Methodological, and Practical Implications

# 9.2.1 Theoretical Implication: a Ganqing Approach to Rapport (management)

An analysis of therapists' management of clients' negative emotions has revealed how GQVI discordant rapport triggers negative emotions, and how rapport management strategies are employed to manage these negative emotions. Unmet ganqing expectations and unfulfilled ganqing obligations have caused disharmony between clients and NTPPs. Unmet ganqing expectations involve a lack of feelings and/or emotional detachment, while unfulfilled ganqing obligations concern the interlocutor's filial obligation omission behaviour, as well as owing emotional debt to others.

By comparing the triggers of rapport threats between clients and NPTPs, I find these threats are different from the infringement of autonomy or benefit and differ from interactional detachment or affective detachment. That is to say, the two fundamental socio-pragmatic interactional principles—the equity principle and the association principle—cannot fully explain interpersonal communication in intimate relationships (e.g., parent-child or close friendships). Against this backdrop, the relational principle of

ganqing, whose components involve affection, concern, mutuality, reciprocity, and relational obligations, can be drawn. Accordingly, a ganqing approach to rapport (management) is proposed.

In sum, ganqing is regarded as a relational principle that influences Chinese people's interactions, and it also a social norm that guides people's interactions with those close to them or who have a long-term relationship with them. Its unique features and the components it emphasizes make it a specific interactional principle that needs to be noticed and highlighted.

# 9.2.2 Theoretical Implication of Ganqing for (im)politeness Theory

Analysis of the ganqing violation induced discordant rapport that has triggered interlocutors' negative emotions and adoption of a ganqing approach has important implications for the understanding of (im)politeness theory in general. First, by exploring ganqing threats in interpersonal disharmony, this study moves beyond the current focus on face threatening as impolite. Face threat has been the core of analysis of conflictive interpersonal interactions over the years. This study, however, reveals that ganqing threats are first and foremost evaluated as threats to interpersonal harmony.

Second, the analysis shows how people's (im) politeness evaluations are influenced by culturally-based expectations. It is widely accepted that (im) politeness evaluation is underpinned by the notion of 'expectation' (e.g., Spencer-Oatey 2005, 2008; Culpeper 2011; Haugh 2015; Kádár & Haugh 2013; Tayebi 2016). For example, Culpeper (2011: 23) defines impoliteness as clashes of 'expectations' when a situated behaviour 'conflicts with how one expects them to be, how one wants them to be and/or how one thinks things ought to be'. Garcés-Conejos Blitivich (2010: 63) defines impoliteness as the disregard for ' (pre) genre-sanctioned, norms and interactional parameters managing the rights and obligations associated therein with a given individual/social identity'. According to Haugh (2015), 'moral expectations' or 'moral order' are closely associated with (im)politeness evaluations. Most of the studies analyze the interlink between expectation and (im)politeness from a general perspective, ignoring cultural variations or emic perspectives (Zhao & Ran 2019). Ganqing is a culturally specific expectation underlying Chinese people's interaction with people from an intimate/close relationship (e.g., child-parent, husband-wife, and between intimate friends), which is different from the expectation that exists between strangers, relatives, neighbours, acquaintances, etc. It also differs from the expectations that have been discussed within the Chinese sociocultural context (e.g., qingmian expectation and renqing expectation).

Third, the study has implications for the relation between emotion and impulsiveness. Prior studies have adopted an outcome-orientated view of

emotion, mainly examining the emotional responses to rapport violations or impulsiveness (Spencer-Oatey 2005, 2008). Some other scholars explore the impact of emotions on impulsiveness evaluations (Langlotz & Locher 2012; Vergis & Terkourafi 2015). Most of these studies focus on the individual's emotional status. However, the study of ganqing threat explores how affection between persons can be threatened and damaged.

# 9.2.3 Surrogate-based Rmf

The research findings discussed in Chapter 9 support the surrogate-based RMF proposed in Chapter 4. Since the surrogate-based RMF is drawn based on Spencer-Oatey's RMF (2000, 2005), Table 8.1 is presented to illustrate the differences between the surrogate-based RMF and the RMF addressed by Spencer-Oatey. In addition to what is displayed in Figure 9.1, two points are worth mentioning. First, surrogate-based rapport management is more applicable to the management of discordant rapport/disharmonious relationships. More concretely, an interlocutor (e.g., interlocutor A) recognizes that there exists a disharmonious relationship between the co-interlocutor (interlocutor B) and the NPTP. The disharmony between B and NPTP can be manifested through the meta/comments he/she makes about the rapport-sensitive issues disclosed by B, and it can also be sequentially displayed in B's responses in the next turns (e.g., acknowledgement tokens, a second assessment that affiliates with A's prior assessment).

Second, the surrogate roles taken by A when he/she manages the discordant rapport between B and NPTP are dependent on the discordant rapport orientation he/she takes, which involves at least three circumstances<sup>2</sup>: (1) if A intends to challenge the disharmonious relationship or to restore relational harmony between B and NPTP, he/she tends to acts as the surrogate of the NPTP and to account for the NPTP's inappropriate behaviour; (2) if A aims to maintain the disharmony between B and the NPTP (if he/she believes it is in A's interest/benefit to do so), A tends to serve as a pseudo-surrogate of B and uses aggressive strategies to abuse or criticize the NPTP's inappropriateness; (3) if A shows little concern about the disharmony between the NPTP and B but focuses on the latter's self-image, he/she may act as a pseudo-surrogate of B and employ denial strategies, or he/she might adopt two surrogate roles simultaneously and use two types of strategies (e.g., denial strategies and accounting strategies). Concerning the underlying factor that influences A's choice of surrogate roles and linguistic strategies, the social distance between B and NPTP is a key one.

Based on what is discussed in this section and laid out in Table 9.1, this study thereby theoretically contributes to rapport management in interpersonal pragmatics by proposing the surrogate-based RMF, which accounts

for an interlocutor's management of discordant rapport that exists between a co-interlocutor and an NPTP.

# 9.2.4 Theoretical Implication: an Integral Relationship Between Interpersonal Emotions and Interpersonal Relations

In terms of the interconnection between interpersonal relations and interpersonal emotions, most of the studies have been examined from an outcome-orientated view, that is, interpersonal emotions are responses to antecedent incidents such as face threat, impulsiveness, or rapport threats (Spencer-Oatey 2002, 2005, 2008, 2011; Culpeper 2011; Langlotz & Locher 2012; Vergis & Terkourafi 2015).

By examining emotion from a process-oriented view, that is, by examining the triggers of interlocutors' negative emotions, by exploring the interactive management of these negative emotions, as well as by referencing the communicative effect of emotion management, an integral interrelationship between interpersonal emotion and interpersonal relation is shown (cf. Figure 9.1). On the one hand, it supports the claim that interpersonal communication is the primary elicitor of most emotions (Andersen & Guerrero 1998). On the other hand, negative emotions are not simply products or residues of the experience of disharmonious interpersonal interaction; rather they are the drivers of the dynamic movement of interpersonal relations, including the maintaining, challenging, ignoring, or neglecting of the status of interpersonal relations.

In sum, such a finding not only offers a lens from which negative emotion can be understood, more importantly, but it also uncovers the pragmatic mechanism through which negative interpersonal emotions can be managed.

# 9.2.5 Methodological Implication: Self-reports as a Useful Site for Interpersonal Pragmatics Research

This study echoes Haugh et al's (2013) views that the self-reports of individuals are useful sites for the analysis of interpersonal relationships as well as interpersonal emotions. The primary site for analyzing interpersonal relationships is the 'relationship achieved through interaction by two or more individuals' (ibid 2013: 5), just like most of the studies that are examined from the interpersonal pragmatics approach (e.g., Arundale 2006, 2010; Norrby et al. 2015; Orthaber & Reiter 2016; Haugh & Sinkeviciute 2018; Ran & Zhao 2018). The reason why self-reports in psychotherapy sessions are suitable for examining interpersonal relations can be explained as follows. (1) The self-reports involve the interaction between two individuals (the interaction between clients and the guanxi

members). (2) The identity features of the participants are clear, and the construction and the changes in the relationship between them are observable. (3) The content being communicated has specific interpersonal features, such as expression of emotion or attitude. In other words, self-reports satisfy the data scope of interpersonal pragmatics analysis (Ran & Liu 2015).

This study takes a process-oriented approach to examine interpersonal relationships and interpersonal emotions, thus distinguishing itself from the prior studies (Spencer-Oatey 2002; Culpeper 2011) which draw on self-reports for analysis. Moreover, the role of the participants, the nature of their relationship, and the social norms within the society contribute to a comprehensive understanding of interpersonal relationships and interpersonal emotions. It is hoped this study can advance people's understanding of relating and expand the research in interpersonal pragmatics.

# 9.2.6 Practical Implication: a Framework for Improving Mental and Emotional Health

In addition to the above theoretical implications, this study has the following practical implications: (1) the types of GQVI discordant rapport presented in Chapter 6 may be applied to the analysis of the cause of clients' negative emotions in psychotherapeutic training and practice, especially in the Chinese context. The surrogate-based rapport management strategies presented in Chapter 7 could be integrated into emotion-managing guidelines. (2) This study sheds light on the harmonious relationship between people. As the data analysis shows, people's emotional well-being is closely associated with their significant others (the parents or close friends). Though language cannot unite or separate people physically (Langlotz 2010), they have a direct impact on people's understanding of relationships and their emotional status. An analysis of the background information of clients further reveals that the emotional problems caused by relational disharmony lead to more severe physical or mental or social problems, such as severe depression, schoolchildren dropping out of school, etc. To solve these social problems, the healing power of language cannot be overlooked.

Moreover, as one is likely to see a fast increase in the number of people who have depressed feelings and their reliance on trusted ones to help them out, possessing the ability and knowledge to handle others' unmet expectations and the accusations they receive is becoming an essential attribute of good teachers, good parents, good partners, etc. It is hoped that the findings reported will be of value not only to therapists and practitioners but also to those who are concerned about the well-being of

their significant others, for instance, the school teachers who are concerned about their students' well-being; the parents who show concern to the psychological well-being of their children, and people who care about their friends' emotional health and mental health (Pawelczyk 2011: 55).

# 9.3 Limitations and Suggestions for Future Research

Although this study has obtained interesting findings and drawn conclusions to the research questions, there are certainly some limitations that await further exploration.

Firstly, the limitation of the amount and sources of data should be noted. It is a demanding job to collect enough data for a qualitative study or a pragmatic study. It has requirements on how much data is collected and how it is collected. Although much effort has been made to collect data, it still leaves much to be improved. It has been suggested that video data can provide more information about interlocutors' emotional status than audio data and they are preferable for analyzing emotions, however the difficulty in accessing it makes it a limitation.

The second limitation of this study suffers from its generalizability of the findings. GQVI's discordant rapport is limited to interpersonal interaction in close and long-term relationships. In Chinese interpersonal relationships and communication, other expectations other than ganqing could be involved, serving as the cause of interlocutors' negative emotions. Moreover, because of the variabilities of the psychotherapeutic philosophies of therapists, the research findings cannot reflect all types of surrogate-based rapport management strategies that are employed to manage negative emotions caused by discordant rapport in psychotherapeutic discourse.

Thirdly, As the data analysis shows, surrogate-based rapport management is dedicated to managing GQVI discordant rapport clients have with NPTPs and in this way managing clients' negative emotions, which is the major concern and dominant functions played by these surrogate-based rapport management strategies. In addition to this, these strategies might simultaneously manage the relationship therapists have with their clients. For instance, by using surrogate-based aggressive strategies (i.e., pseudo-surrogating clients and using aggressive strategies), therapists not only protect the sociality rights of clients but also display similar feelings with clients. Further studies can approach this perspective and examine therapists' efforts in managing the rapport they have with clients, which provides emotional support and might also contribute to the management of clients' negative emotions.

The analytic method is the fourth limitation that is worth noting. In the analysis of interlocutors' negative emotional reactions and therapists' intervention to these negative emotions, the interactional approach is

taken. To capture more detailed information regarding interlocutors' emotional features, Praat software can be used.

All in all, the attempts made in this research have at least provided some suggestions, cues, or hopefully even directions for further indigenous research on interpersonal pragmatics. As scholars of Chinese pragmatics have proposed, we should consider the social and humanistic factors (Qian 2001), and the social needs when doing our pragmatic research (He 2018, quoted from Ran 2018). Meanwhile, we should give prominence to Chinese sociocultural features/specific contextual features while drawing on Western theories of pragmatics (Huo 2009). The brilliant Chinese culture and various forms of interpersonal communication provide fertile ground for the study of Chinese interpersonal pragmatics, making it a research area that deserves sustained and further exploration.
